_add_newdocs.py 
1    # Docstrings for generated ufuncs
2    #
3    # The syntax is designed to look like the function add_newdoc is being
4    # called from numpy.lib, but in this file add_newdoc puts the
5    # docstrings in a dictionary. This dictionary is used in
6    # _generate_pyx.py to generate the docstrings for the ufuncs in
7    # scipy.special at the C level when the ufuncs are created at compile
8    # time.
9    
10   docdict: dict[str, str] = {}
11   
12   
13   def get(name):
14       return docdict.get(name)
15   
16   
17   def add_newdoc(name, doc):
18       docdict[name] = doc
19   
20   
21   add_newdoc("_sf_error_test_function",
22       """ 
23       Private function; do not use. 
24       """)
25

32    
33                    {             0,              x < -pi 
34           cdf(x) = { (pi + x + sin(x))/(2*pi),   -pi <= x <= pi 
35                    {             1,              x > pi 
36    
37       Parameters 
38       ---------- 
39       x : array_like 
40           `x` must contain real numbers. 
41    
42       Returns 
43       ------- 
44       scalar or ndarray 
45           The cosine distribution CDF evaluated at `x`. 
46    
47       """)

55

61    
62       Parameters 
63       ----------

66           `nan` is returned for values of `p` outside the interval [0, 1]. 
67

85   add_newdoc("voigt_profile",
86       r""" 
87       voigt_profile(x, sigma, gamma, out=None) 
88    
89       Voigt profile. 
90    
91       The Voigt profile is a convolution of a 1-D Normal distribution with 
92       standard deviation ``sigma`` and a 1-D Cauchy distribution with half-width at 
93       half-maximum ``gamma``. 
94    
95       If ``sigma = 0``, PDF of Cauchy distribution is returned. 
96       Conversely, if ``gamma = 0``, PDF of Normal distribution is returned. 
97       If ``sigma = gamma = 0``, the return value is ``Inf`` for ``x = 0``, 
98       and ``0`` for all other ``x``. 
99    
100      Parameters 
101      ---------- 
102      x : array_like 
103          Real argument 
104      sigma : array_like 
105          The standard deviation of the Normal distribution part 
106      gamma : array_like 
107          The half-width at half-maximum of the Cauchy distribution part 
108      out : ndarray, optional 
109          Optional output array for the function values 
110   
111      Returns 
112      ------- 
113      scalar or ndarray 
114          The Voigt profile at the given arguments 
115   
116      See Also 
117      -------- 
118      wofz : Faddeeva function 
119   
120      Notes 
121      ----- 
122      It can be expressed in terms of Faddeeva function 
123   
124      .. math:: V(x; \sigma, \gamma) = \frac{Re[w(z)]}{\sigma\sqrt{2\pi}}, 
125      .. math:: z = \frac{x + i\gamma}{\sqrt{2}\sigma} 
126   
127      where :math:`w(z)` is the Faddeeva function. 
128   
129      References 
130      ---------- 
131      .. [1] https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voigt_profile 
132   
133      Examples 
134      -------- 
135      Calculate the function at point 2 for ``sigma=1`` and ``gamma=1``. 
136   
137      >>> from scipy.special import voigt_profile 
138      >>> import numpy as np 
139      >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
140      >>> voigt_profile(2, 1., 1.) 
141      0.09071519942627544 
142   
143      Calculate the function at several points by providing a NumPy array 
144      for `x`. 
145   
146      >>> values = np.array([-2., 0., 5]) 
147      >>> voigt_profile(values, 1., 1.) 
148      array([0.0907152 , 0.20870928, 0.01388492]) 
149   
150      Plot the function for different parameter sets. 
151   
152      >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, 8)) 
153      >>> x = np.linspace(-10, 10, 500) 
154      >>> parameters_list = [(1.5, 0., "solid"), (1.3, 0.5, "dashed"), 
155      ...                    (0., 1.8, "dotted"), (1., 1., "dashdot")] 
156      >>> for params in parameters_list: 
157      ...     sigma, gamma, linestyle = params 
158      ...     voigt = voigt_profile(x, sigma, gamma) 
159      ...     ax.plot(x, voigt, label=rf"$\sigma={sigma},\, \gamma={gamma}$", 
160      ...             ls=linestyle) 
161      >>> ax.legend() 
162      >>> plt.show() 
163   
164      Verify visually that the Voigt profile indeed arises as the convolution 
165      of a normal and a Cauchy distribution. 
166   
167      >>> from scipy.signal import convolve 
168      >>> x, dx = np.linspace(-10, 10, 500, retstep=True) 
169      >>> def gaussian(x, sigma): 
170      ...     return np.exp(-0.5 * x**2/sigma**2)/(sigma * np.sqrt(2*np.pi)) 
171      >>> def cauchy(x, gamma): 
172      ...     return gamma/(np.pi * (np.square(x)+gamma**2)) 
173      >>> sigma = 2 
174      >>> gamma = 1 
175      >>> gauss_profile = gaussian(x, sigma) 
176      >>> cauchy_profile = cauchy(x, gamma) 
177      >>> convolved = dx * convolve(cauchy_profile, gauss_profile, mode="same") 
178      >>> voigt = voigt_profile(x, sigma, gamma) 
179      >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, 8)) 
180      >>> ax.plot(x, gauss_profile, label="Gauss: $G$", c='b') 
181      >>> ax.plot(x, cauchy_profile, label="Cauchy: $C$", c='y', ls="dashed") 
182      >>> xx = 0.5*(x[1:] + x[:-1])  # midpoints 
183      >>> ax.plot(xx, convolved[1:], label="Convolution: $G * C$", ls='dashdot', 
184      ...         c='k') 
185      >>> ax.plot(x, voigt, label="Voigt", ls='dotted', c='r') 
186      >>> ax.legend() 
187      >>> plt.show() 
188      """)
189  
190  add_newdoc("wrightomega",
191      r""" 
192      wrightomega(z, out=None) 
193   
194      Wright Omega function. 
195   
196      Defined as the solution to 
197   
198      .. math:: 
199   
200          \omega + \log(\omega) = z 
201   
202      where :math:`\log` is the principal branch of the complex logarithm. 
203   
204      Parameters 
205      ---------- 
206      z : array_like 
207          Points at which to evaluate the Wright Omega function 
208      out : ndarray, optional 
209          Optional output array for the function values 
210   
211      Returns 
212      ------- 
213      omega : scalar or ndarray 
214          Values of the Wright Omega function 
215   
216      See Also 
217      -------- 
218      lambertw : The Lambert W function 
219   
220      Notes 
221      ----- 
222      .. versionadded:: 0.19.0 
223   
224      The function can also be defined as 
225   
226      .. math:: 
227   
228          \omega(z) = W_{K(z)}(e^z) 
229   
230      where :math:`K(z) = \lceil (\Im(z) - \pi)/(2\pi) \rceil` is the 
231      unwinding number and :math:`W` is the Lambert W function. 
232   
233      The implementation here is taken from [1]_. 
234   
235      References 
236      ---------- 
237      .. [1] Lawrence, Corless, and Jeffrey, "Algorithm 917: Complex 
238             Double-Precision Evaluation of the Wright :math:`\omega` 
239             Function." ACM Transactions on Mathematical Software, 
240             2012. :doi:`10.1145/2168773.2168779`. 
241   
242      Examples 
243      -------- 
244      >>> import numpy as np 
245      >>> from scipy.special import wrightomega, lambertw 
246   
247      >>> wrightomega([-2, -1, 0, 1, 2]) 
248      array([0.12002824, 0.27846454, 0.56714329, 1.        , 1.5571456 ]) 
249   
250      Complex input: 
251   
252      >>> wrightomega(3 + 5j) 
253      (1.5804428632097158+3.8213626783287937j) 
254   
255      Verify that ``wrightomega(z)`` satisfies ``w + log(w) = z``: 
256   
257      >>> w = -5 + 4j 
258      >>> wrightomega(w + np.log(w)) 
259      (-5+4j) 
260   
261      Verify the connection to ``lambertw``: 
262   
263      >>> z = 0.5 + 3j 
264      >>> wrightomega(z) 
265      (0.0966015889280649+1.4937828458191993j) 
266      >>> lambertw(np.exp(z)) 
267      (0.09660158892806493+1.4937828458191993j) 
268   
269      >>> z = 0.5 + 4j 
270      >>> wrightomega(z) 
271      (-0.3362123489037213+2.282986001579032j) 
272      >>> lambertw(np.exp(z), k=1) 
273      (-0.33621234890372115+2.282986001579032j) 
274      """)
275

277  add_newdoc("agm",
278      """ 
279      agm(a, b, out=None) 
280   
281      Compute the arithmetic-geometric mean of `a` and `b`. 
282   
283      Start with a_0 = a and b_0 = b and iteratively compute:: 
284   
285          a_{n+1} = (a_n + b_n)/2 
286          b_{n+1} = sqrt(a_n*b_n) 
287   
288      a_n and b_n converge to the same limit as n increases; their common 
289      limit is agm(a, b). 
290   
291      Parameters 
292      ---------- 
293      a, b : array_like 
294          Real values only. If the values are both negative, the result 
295          is negative. If one value is negative and the other is positive, 
296          `nan` is returned. 
297      out : ndarray, optional 
298          Optional output array for the function values 
299   
300      Returns 
301      ------- 
302      scalar or ndarray 
303          The arithmetic-geometric mean of `a` and `b`. 
304   
305      Examples 
306      -------- 
307      >>> import numpy as np 
308      >>> from scipy.special import agm 
309      >>> a, b = 24.0, 6.0 
310      >>> agm(a, b) 
311      13.458171481725614 
312   
313      Compare that result to the iteration: 
314   
315      >>> while a != b: 
316      ...     a, b = (a + b)/2, np.sqrt(a*b) 
317      ...     print("a = %19.16f  b=%19.16f" % (a, b)) 
318      ... 
319      a = 15.0000000000000000  b=12.0000000000000000 
320      a = 13.5000000000000000  b=13.4164078649987388 
321      a = 13.4582039324993694  b=13.4581390309909850 
322      a = 13.4581714817451772  b=13.4581714817060547 
323      a = 13.4581714817256159  b=13.4581714817256159 
324   
325      When array-like arguments are given, broadcasting applies: 
326   
327      >>> a = np.array([[1.5], [3], [6]])  # a has shape (3, 1). 
328      >>> b = np.array([6, 12, 24, 48])    # b has shape (4,). 
329      >>> agm(a, b) 
330      array([[  3.36454287,   5.42363427,   9.05798751,  15.53650756], 
331             [  4.37037309,   6.72908574,  10.84726853,  18.11597502], 
332             [  6.        ,   8.74074619,  13.45817148,  21.69453707]]) 
333      """)
334  
335  add_newdoc("airy",
336      r""" 
337      airy(z, out=None) 
338   
339      Airy functions and their derivatives. 
340   
341      Parameters 
342      ---------- 
343      z : array_like 
344          Real or complex argument. 
345      out : tuple of ndarray, optional 
346          Optional output arrays for the function values 
347   
348      Returns 
349      ------- 
350      Ai, Aip, Bi, Bip : 4-tuple of scalar or ndarray 
351          Airy functions Ai and Bi, and their derivatives Aip and Bip. 
352   
353      See Also 
354      -------- 
355      airye : exponentially scaled Airy functions. 
356   
357      Notes 
358      ----- 
359      The Airy functions Ai and Bi are two independent solutions of 
360   
361      .. math:: y''(x) = x y(x). 
362   
363      For real `z` in [-10, 10], the computation is carried out by calling 
364      the Cephes [1]_ `airy` routine, which uses power series summation 
365      for small `z` and rational minimax approximations for large `z`. 
366   
367      Outside this range, the AMOS [2]_ `zairy` and `zbiry` routines are 
368      employed.  They are computed using power series for :math:`|z| < 1` and 
369      the following relations to modified Bessel functions for larger `z` 
370      (where :math:`t \equiv 2 z^{3/2}/3`): 
371   
372      .. math:: 
373   
374          Ai(z) = \frac{1}{\pi \sqrt{3}} K_{1/3}(t) 
375   
376          Ai'(z) = -\frac{z}{\pi \sqrt{3}} K_{2/3}(t) 
377   
378          Bi(z) = \sqrt{\frac{z}{3}} \left(I_{-1/3}(t) + I_{1/3}(t) \right) 
379   
380          Bi'(z) = \frac{z}{\sqrt{3}} \left(I_{-2/3}(t) + I_{2/3}(t)\right) 
381   
382      References 
383      ---------- 
384      .. [1] Cephes Mathematical Functions Library, 
385             http://www.netlib.org/cephes/ 
386      .. [2] Donald E. Amos, "AMOS, A Portable Package for Bessel Functions 
387             of a Complex Argument and Nonnegative Order", 
388             http://netlib.org/amos/ 
389   
390      Examples 
391      -------- 
392      Compute the Airy functions on the interval [-15, 5]. 
393   
394      >>> import numpy as np 
395      >>> from scipy import special 
396      >>> x = np.linspace(-15, 5, 201) 
397      >>> ai, aip, bi, bip = special.airy(x) 
398   
399      Plot Ai(x) and Bi(x). 
400   
401      >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
402      >>> plt.plot(x, ai, 'r', label='Ai(x)') 
403      >>> plt.plot(x, bi, 'b--', label='Bi(x)') 
404      >>> plt.ylim(-0.5, 1.0) 
405      >>> plt.grid() 
406      >>> plt.legend(loc='upper left') 
407      >>> plt.show() 
408   
409      """)
410  
411  add_newdoc("airye",
412      """ 
413      airye(z, out=None) 
414   
415      Exponentially scaled Airy functions and their derivatives. 
416   
417      Scaling:: 
418   
419          eAi  = Ai  * exp(2.0/3.0*z*sqrt(z)) 
420          eAip = Aip * exp(2.0/3.0*z*sqrt(z)) 
421          eBi  = Bi  * exp(-abs(2.0/3.0*(z*sqrt(z)).real)) 
422          eBip = Bip * exp(-abs(2.0/3.0*(z*sqrt(z)).real)) 
423   
424      Parameters 
425      ---------- 
426      z : array_like 
427          Real or complex argument. 
428      out : tuple of ndarray, optional 
429          Optional output arrays for the function values 
430   
431      Returns 
432      ------- 
433      eAi, eAip, eBi, eBip : 4-tuple of scalar or ndarray 
434          Exponentially scaled Airy functions eAi and eBi, and their derivatives 
435          eAip and eBip 
436   
437      See Also 
438      -------- 
439      airy 
440   
441      Notes 
442      ----- 
443      Wrapper for the AMOS [1]_ routines `zairy` and `zbiry`. 
444   
445      References 
446      ---------- 
447      .. [1] Donald E. Amos, "AMOS, A Portable Package for Bessel Functions 
448             of a Complex Argument and Nonnegative Order", 
449             http://netlib.org/amos/ 
450   
451      Examples 
452      -------- 
453      We can compute exponentially scaled Airy functions and their derivatives: 
454   
455      >>> import numpy as np 
456      >>> from scipy.special import airye 
457      >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
458      >>> z = np.linspace(0, 50, 500) 
459      >>> eAi, eAip, eBi, eBip = airye(z) 
460      >>> f, ax = plt.subplots(2, 1, sharex=True) 
461      >>> for ind, data in enumerate([[eAi, eAip, ["eAi", "eAip"]], 
462      ...                             [eBi, eBip, ["eBi", "eBip"]]]): 
463      ...     ax[ind].plot(z, data[0], "-r", z, data[1], "-b") 
464      ...     ax[ind].legend(data[2]) 
465      ...     ax[ind].grid(True) 
466      >>> plt.show() 
467   
468      We can compute these using usual non-scaled Airy functions by: 
469   
470      >>> from scipy.special import airy 
471      >>> Ai, Aip, Bi, Bip = airy(z) 
472      >>> np.allclose(eAi, Ai * np.exp(2.0 / 3.0 * z * np.sqrt(z))) 
473      True 
474      >>> np.allclose(eAip, Aip * np.exp(2.0 / 3.0 * z * np.sqrt(z))) 
475      True 
476      >>> np.allclose(eBi, Bi * np.exp(-abs(np.real(2.0 / 3.0 * z * np.sqrt(z))))) 
477      True 
478      >>> np.allclose(eBip, Bip * np.exp(-abs(np.real(2.0 / 3.0 * z * np.sqrt(z))))) 
479      True 
480   
481      Comparing non-scaled and exponentially scaled ones, the usual non-scaled 
482      function quickly underflows for large values, whereas the exponentially 
483      scaled function does not. 
484   
485      >>> airy(200) 
486      (0.0, 0.0, nan, nan) 
487      >>> airye(200) 
488      (0.07501041684381093, -1.0609012305109042, 0.15003188417418148, 2.1215836725571093) 
489   
490      """)
491  
492  add_newdoc("bdtr",
493      r""" 
494      bdtr(k, n, p, out=None) 
495   
496      Binomial distribution cumulative distribution function. 
497   
498      Sum of the terms 0 through `floor(k)` of the Binomial probability density. 
499   
500      .. math:: 
501          \mathrm{bdtr}(k, n, p) = 
502          \sum_{j=0}^{\lfloor k \rfloor} {{n}\choose{j}} p^j (1-p)^{n-j} 
503   
504      Parameters 
505      ---------- 
506      k : array_like 
507          Number of successes (double), rounded down to the nearest integer. 
508      n : array_like 
509          Number of events (int). 
510      p : array_like 
511          Probability of success in a single event (float). 
512      out : ndarray, optional 
513          Optional output array for the function values 
514   
515      Returns 
516      ------- 
517      y : scalar or ndarray 
518          Probability of `floor(k)` or fewer successes in `n` independent events with 
519          success probabilities of `p`. 
520   
521      Notes 
522      ----- 
523      The terms are not summed directly; instead the regularized incomplete beta 
524      function is employed, according to the formula, 
525   
526      .. math:: 
527          \mathrm{bdtr}(k, n, p) = 
528          I_{1 - p}(n - \lfloor k \rfloor, \lfloor k \rfloor + 1). 
529   
530      Wrapper for the Cephes [1]_ routine `bdtr`. 
531   
532      References 
533      ---------- 
534      .. [1] Cephes Mathematical Functions Library, 
535             http://www.netlib.org/cephes/ 
536   
537      """)
538  
539  add_newdoc("bdtrc",
540      r""" 
541      bdtrc(k, n, p, out=None) 
542   
543      Binomial distribution survival function. 
544   
545      Sum of the terms `floor(k) + 1` through `n` of the binomial probability 
546      density, 
547   
548      .. math:: 
549          \mathrm{bdtrc}(k, n, p) = 
550          \sum_{j=\lfloor k \rfloor +1}^n {{n}\choose{j}} p^j (1-p)^{n-j} 
551   
552      Parameters 
553      ---------- 
554      k : array_like 
555          Number of successes (double), rounded down to nearest integer. 
556      n : array_like 
557          Number of events (int) 
558      p : array_like 
559          Probability of success in a single event. 
560      out : ndarray, optional 
561          Optional output array for the function values 
562   
563      Returns 
564      ------- 
565      y : scalar or ndarray 
566          Probability of `floor(k) + 1` or more successes in `n` independent 
567          events with success probabilities of `p`. 
568   
569      See Also 
570      -------- 
571      bdtr 
572      betainc 
573   
574      Notes 
575      ----- 
576      The terms are not summed directly; instead the regularized incomplete beta 
577      function is employed, according to the formula, 
578   
579      .. math:: 
580          \mathrm{bdtrc}(k, n, p) = I_{p}(\lfloor k \rfloor + 1, n - \lfloor k \rfloor). 
581   
582      Wrapper for the Cephes [1]_ routine `bdtrc`. 
583   
584      References 
585      ---------- 
586      .. [1] Cephes Mathematical Functions Library, 
587             http://www.netlib.org/cephes/ 
588   
589      """)
590  
591  add_newdoc("bdtri",
592      r""" 
593      bdtri(k, n, y, out=None) 
594   
595      Inverse function to `bdtr` with respect to `p`. 
596   
597      Finds the event probability `p` such that the sum of the terms 0 through 
598      `k` of the binomial probability density is equal to the given cumulative 
599      probability `y`. 
600   
601      Parameters 
602      ---------- 
603      k : array_like 
604          Number of successes (float), rounded down to the nearest integer. 
605      n : array_like 
606          Number of events (float) 
607      y : array_like 
608          Cumulative probability (probability of `k` or fewer successes in `n` 
609          events). 
610      out : ndarray, optional 
611          Optional output array for the function values 
612   
613      Returns 
614      ------- 
615      p : scalar or ndarray 
616          The event probability such that `bdtr(\lfloor k \rfloor, n, p) = y`. 
617   
618      See Also 
619      -------- 
620      bdtr 
621      betaincinv 
622   
623      Notes 
624      ----- 
625      The computation is carried out using the inverse beta integral function 
626      and the relation,:: 
627   
628          1 - p = betaincinv(n - k, k + 1, y). 
629   
630      Wrapper for the Cephes [1]_ routine `bdtri`. 
631   
632      References 
633      ---------- 
634      .. [1] Cephes Mathematical Functions Library, 
635             http://www.netlib.org/cephes/ 
636      """)
637  
638  add_newdoc("bdtrik",
639      """ 
640      bdtrik(y, n, p, out=None) 
641   
642      Inverse function to `bdtr` with respect to `k`. 
643   
644      Finds the number of successes `k` such that the sum of the terms 0 through 
645      `k` of the Binomial probability density for `n` events with probability 
646      `p` is equal to the given cumulative probability `y`. 
647   
648      Parameters 
649      ---------- 
650      y : array_like 
651          Cumulative probability (probability of `k` or fewer successes in `n` 
652          events). 
653      n : array_like 
654          Number of events (float). 
655      p : array_like 
656          Success probability (float). 
657      out : ndarray, optional 
658          Optional output array for the function values 
659   
660      Returns 
661      ------- 
662      k : scalar or ndarray 
663          The number of successes `k` such that `bdtr(k, n, p) = y`. 
664   
665      See Also 
666      -------- 
667      bdtr 
668   
669      Notes 
670      ----- 
671      Formula 26.5.24 of [1]_ is used to reduce the binomial distribution to the 
672      cumulative incomplete beta distribution. 
673   
674      Computation of `k` involves a search for a value that produces the desired 
675      value of `y`. The search relies on the monotonicity of `y` with `k`. 
676   
677      Wrapper for the CDFLIB [2]_ Fortran routine `cdfbin`. 
678   
679      References 
680      ---------- 
681      .. [1] Milton Abramowitz and Irene A. Stegun, eds. 
682             Handbook of Mathematical Functions with Formulas, 
683             Graphs, and Mathematical Tables. New York: Dover, 1972. 
684      .. [2] Barry Brown, James Lovato, and Kathy Russell, 
685             CDFLIB: Library of Fortran Routines for Cumulative Distribution 
686             Functions, Inverses, and Other Parameters. 
687   
688      """)
689  
690  add_newdoc("bdtrin",
691      """ 
692      bdtrin(k, y, p, out=None) 
693   
694      Inverse function to `bdtr` with respect to `n`. 
695   
696      Finds the number of events `n` such that the sum of the terms 0 through 
697      `k` of the Binomial probability density for events with probability `p` is 
698      equal to the given cumulative probability `y`. 
699   
700      Parameters 
701      ---------- 
702      k : array_like 
703          Number of successes (float). 
704      y : array_like 
705          Cumulative probability (probability of `k` or fewer successes in `n` 
706          events). 
707      p : array_like 
708          Success probability (float). 
709      out : ndarray, optional 
710          Optional output array for the function values 
711   
712      Returns 
713      ------- 
714      n : scalar or ndarray 
715          The number of events `n` such that `bdtr(k, n, p) = y`. 
716   
717      See Also 
718      -------- 
719      bdtr 
720   
721      Notes 
722      ----- 
723      Formula 26.5.24 of [1]_ is used to reduce the binomial distribution to the 
724      cumulative incomplete beta distribution. 
725   
726      Computation of `n` involves a search for a value that produces the desired 
727      value of `y`. The search relies on the monotonicity of `y` with `n`. 
728   
729      Wrapper for the CDFLIB [2]_ Fortran routine `cdfbin`. 
730   
731      References 
732      ---------- 
733      .. [1] Milton Abramowitz and Irene A. Stegun, eds. 
734             Handbook of Mathematical Functions with Formulas, 
735             Graphs, and Mathematical Tables. New York: Dover, 1972. 
736      .. [2] Barry Brown, James Lovato, and Kathy Russell, 
737             CDFLIB: Library of Fortran Routines for Cumulative Distribution 
738             Functions, Inverses, and Other Parameters. 
739      """)
740  
741  add_newdoc("btdtria",
742      r""" 
743      btdtria(p, b, x, out=None) 
744   
745      Inverse of `betainc` with respect to `a`. 
746   
747      This is the inverse of the beta cumulative distribution function, `betainc`, 
748      considered as a function of `a`, returning the value of `a` for which 
749      `betainc(a, b, x) = p`, or 
750   
751      .. math:: 
752          p = \int_0^x \frac{\Gamma(a + b)}{\Gamma(a)\Gamma(b)} t^{a-1} (1-t)^{b-1}\,dt 
753   
754      Parameters 
755      ---------- 
756      p : array_like 
757          Cumulative probability, in [0, 1]. 
758      b : array_like 
759          Shape parameter (`b` > 0). 
760      x : array_like 
761          The quantile, in [0, 1]. 
762      out : ndarray, optional 
763          Optional output array for the function values 
764   
765      Returns 
766      ------- 
767      a : scalar or ndarray 
768          The value of the shape parameter `a` such that `betainc(a, b, x) = p`. 
769   
770      See Also 
771      -------- 
772      btdtrib : Inverse of the beta cumulative distribution function, with respect to `b`. 
773   
774      Notes 
775      ----- 
776      Wrapper for the CDFLIB [1]_ Fortran routine `cdfbet`. 
777   
778      The cumulative distribution function `p` is computed using a routine by 
779      DiDinato and Morris [2]_. Computation of `a` involves a search for a value 
780      that produces the desired value of `p`. The search relies on the 
781      monotonicity of `p` with `a`. 
782   
783      References 
784      ---------- 
785      .. [1] Barry Brown, James Lovato, and Kathy Russell, 
786             CDFLIB: Library of Fortran Routines for Cumulative Distribution 
787             Functions, Inverses, and Other Parameters. 
788      .. [2] DiDinato, A. R. and Morris, A. H., 
789             Algorithm 708: Significant Digit Computation of the Incomplete Beta 
790             Function Ratios. ACM Trans. Math. Softw. 18 (1993), 360-373. 
791   
792      """)

848  add_newdoc(
849      "betainc",
850      r""" 
851      betainc(a, b, x, out=None) 
852   
853      Regularized incomplete beta function. 
854   
855      Computes the regularized incomplete beta function, defined as [1]_: 
856   
857      .. math:: 
858   
859          I_x(a, b) = \frac{\Gamma(a+b)}{\Gamma(a)\Gamma(b)} \int_0^x 
860          t^{a-1}(1-t)^{b-1}dt, 
861   
862      for :math:`0 \leq x \leq 1`. 
863   
864      This function is the cumulative distribution function for the beta 
865      distribution; its range is [0, 1]. 
866   
867      Parameters 
868      ---------- 
869      a, b : array_like 
870             Positive, real-valued parameters 
871      x : array_like 
872          Real-valued such that :math:`0 \leq x \leq 1`, 
873          the upper limit of integration 
874      out : ndarray, optional 
875          Optional output array for the function values 
876   
877      Returns 
878      ------- 
879      scalar or ndarray 
880          Value of the regularized incomplete beta function 
881   
882      See Also 
883      -------- 
884      beta : beta function 
885      betaincinv : inverse of the regularized incomplete beta function 
886      betaincc : complement of the regularized incomplete beta function 
887      scipy.stats.beta : beta distribution 
888   
889      Notes 
890      ----- 
891      The term *regularized* in the name of this function refers to the 
892      scaling of the function by the gamma function terms shown in the 
893      formula.  When not qualified as *regularized*, the name *incomplete 
894      beta function* often refers to just the integral expression, 
895      without the gamma terms.  One can use the function `beta` from 
896      `scipy.special` to get this "nonregularized" incomplete beta 
897      function by multiplying the result of ``betainc(a, b, x)`` by 
898      ``beta(a, b)``. 
899   
900      This function wraps the ``ibeta`` routine from the 
901      Boost Math C++ library [2]_. 
902   
903      References 
904      ---------- 
905      .. [1] NIST Digital Library of Mathematical Functions 
906             https://dlmf.nist.gov/8.17 
907      .. [2] The Boost Developers. "Boost C++ Libraries". https://www.boost.org/. 
908   
909      Examples 
910      -------- 
911   
912      Let :math:`B(a, b)` be the `beta` function. 
913   
914      >>> import scipy.special as sc 
915   
916      The coefficient in terms of `gamma` is equal to 
917      :math:`1/B(a, b)`. Also, when :math:`x=1` 
918      the integral is equal to :math:`B(a, b)`. 
919      Therefore, :math:`I_{x=1}(a, b) = 1` for any :math:`a, b`. 
920   
921      >>> sc.betainc(0.2, 3.5, 1.0) 
922      1.0 
923   
924      It satisfies 
925      :math:`I_x(a, b) = x^a F(a, 1-b, a+1, x)/ (aB(a, b))`, 
926      where :math:`F` is the hypergeometric function `hyp2f1`: 
927   
928      >>> a, b, x = 1.4, 3.1, 0.5 
929      >>> x**a * sc.hyp2f1(a, 1 - b, a + 1, x)/(a * sc.beta(a, b)) 
930      0.8148904036225295 
931      >>> sc.betainc(a, b, x) 
932      0.8148904036225296 
933   
934      This functions satisfies the relationship 
935      :math:`I_x(a, b) = 1 - I_{1-x}(b, a)`: 
936   
937      >>> sc.betainc(2.2, 3.1, 0.4) 
938      0.49339638807619446 
939      >>> 1 - sc.betainc(3.1, 2.2, 1 - 0.4) 
940      0.49339638807619446 
941   
942      """)
943  
944  
945  add_newdoc(
946      "betaincc",
947      r""" 
948      betaincc(a, b, x, out=None) 
949   
950      Complement of the regularized incomplete beta function. 
951   
952      Computes the complement of the regularized incomplete beta function, 
953      defined as [1]_: 
954   
955      .. math:: 
956   
957          \bar{I}_x(a, b) = 1 - I_x(a, b) 
958                          = 1 - \frac{\Gamma(a+b)}{\Gamma(a)\Gamma(b)} \int_0^x 
959                                    t^{a-1}(1-t)^{b-1}dt, 
960   
961      for :math:`0 \leq x \leq 1`. 
962   
963      Parameters 
964      ---------- 
965      a, b : array_like 
966             Positive, real-valued parameters 
967      x : array_like 
968          Real-valued such that :math:`0 \leq x \leq 1`, 
969          the upper limit of integration 
970      out : ndarray, optional 
971          Optional output array for the function values 
972   
973      Returns 
974      ------- 
975      scalar or ndarray 
976          Value of the regularized incomplete beta function 
977   
978      See Also 
979      -------- 
980      betainc : regularized incomplete beta function 
981      betaincinv : inverse of the regularized incomplete beta function 
982      betainccinv : 
983          inverse of the complement of the regularized incomplete beta function 
984      beta : beta function 
985      scipy.stats.beta : beta distribution 
986   
987      Notes 
988      ----- 
989      .. versionadded:: 1.11.0 
990   
991      This function wraps the ``ibetac`` routine from the 
992      Boost Math C++ library [2]_. 
993   
994      References 
995      ---------- 
996      .. [1] NIST Digital Library of Mathematical Functions 
997             https://dlmf.nist.gov/8.17 
998      .. [2] The Boost Developers. "Boost C++ Libraries". https://www.boost.org/. 
999   
1000     Examples 
1001     -------- 
1002     >>> from scipy.special import betaincc, betainc 
1003  
1004     The naive calculation ``1 - betainc(a, b, x)`` loses precision when 
1005     the values of ``betainc(a, b, x)`` are close to 1: 
1006  
1007     >>> 1 - betainc(0.5, 8, [0.9, 0.99, 0.999]) 
1008     array([2.0574632e-09, 0.0000000e+00, 0.0000000e+00]) 
1009  
1010     By using ``betaincc``, we get the correct values: 
1011  
1012     >>> betaincc(0.5, 8, [0.9, 0.99, 0.999]) 
1013     array([2.05746321e-09, 1.97259354e-17, 1.96467954e-25]) 
1014  
1015     """)
1016 
1017 add_newdoc(
1018     "betaincinv",
1019     r""" 
1020     betaincinv(a, b, y, out=None) 
1021  
1022     Inverse of the regularized incomplete beta function. 
1023  
1024     Computes :math:`x` such that: 
1025  
1026     .. math:: 
1027  
1028         y = I_x(a, b) = \frac{\Gamma(a+b)}{\Gamma(a)\Gamma(b)} 
1029         \int_0^x t^{a-1}(1-t)^{b-1}dt, 
1030  
1031     where :math:`I_x` is the normalized incomplete beta function `betainc` 
1032     and :math:`\Gamma` is the `gamma` function [1]_. 
1033  
1034     Parameters 
1035     ---------- 
1036     a, b : array_like 
1037         Positive, real-valued parameters 
1038     y : array_like 
1039         Real-valued input 
1040     out : ndarray, optional 
1041         Optional output array for function values 
1042  
1043     Returns 
1044     ------- 
1045     scalar or ndarray 
1046         Value of the inverse of the regularized incomplete beta function 
1047  
1048     See Also 
1049     -------- 
1050     betainc : regularized incomplete beta function 
1051     gamma : gamma function 
1052  
1053     Notes 
1054     ----- 
1055     This function wraps the ``ibeta_inv`` routine from the 
1056     Boost Math C++ library [2]_. 
1057  
1058     References 
1059     ---------- 
1060     .. [1] NIST Digital Library of Mathematical Functions 
1061            https://dlmf.nist.gov/8.17 
1062     .. [2] The Boost Developers. "Boost C++ Libraries". https://www.boost.org/. 
1063  
1064     Examples 
1065     -------- 
1066     >>> import scipy.special as sc 
1067  
1068     This function is the inverse of `betainc` for fixed 
1069     values of :math:`a` and :math:`b`. 
1070  
1071     >>> a, b = 1.2, 3.1 
1072     >>> y = sc.betainc(a, b, 0.2) 
1073     >>> sc.betaincinv(a, b, y) 
1074     0.2 
1075     >>> 
1076     >>> a, b = 7.5, 0.4 
1077     >>> x = sc.betaincinv(a, b, 0.5) 
1078     >>> sc.betainc(a, b, x) 
1079     0.5 
1080  
1081     """)

1152 add_newdoc("boxcox",
1153     """ 
1154     boxcox(x, lmbda, out=None) 
1155  
1156     Compute the Box-Cox transformation. 
1157  
1158     The Box-Cox transformation is:: 
1159  
1160         y = (x**lmbda - 1) / lmbda  if lmbda != 0 
1161             log(x)                  if lmbda == 0 
1162  
1163     Returns `nan` if ``x < 0``. 
1164     Returns `-inf` if ``x == 0`` and ``lmbda < 0``. 
1165  
1166     Parameters 
1167     ---------- 
1168     x : array_like 
1169         Data to be transformed. 
1170     lmbda : array_like 
1171         Power parameter of the Box-Cox transform. 
1172     out : ndarray, optional 
1173         Optional output array for the function values 
1174  
1175     Returns 
1176     ------- 
1177     y : scalar or ndarray 
1178         Transformed data. 
1179  
1180     Notes 
1181     ----- 
1182  
1183     .. versionadded:: 0.14.0 
1184  
1185     Examples 
1186     -------- 
1187     >>> from scipy.special import boxcox 
1188     >>> boxcox([1, 4, 10], 2.5) 
1189     array([   0.        ,   12.4       ,  126.09110641]) 
1190     >>> boxcox(2, [0, 1, 2]) 
1191     array([ 0.69314718,  1.        ,  1.5       ]) 
1192     """)
1193

1203             log(1+x)                    if lmbda == 0 
1204  
1205     Returns `nan` if ``x < -1``. 
1206     Returns `-inf` if ``x == -1`` and ``lmbda < 0``. 
1207  
1208     Parameters

1213         Power parameter of the Box-Cox transform.

1217     Returns

1312 add_newdoc("chdtr",
1313     r""" 
1314     chdtr(v, x, out=None) 
1315  
1316     Chi square cumulative distribution function. 
1317  
1318     Returns the area under the left tail (from 0 to `x`) of the Chi 
1319     square probability density function with `v` degrees of freedom: 
1320  
1321     .. math:: 
1322  
1323         \frac{1}{2^{v/2} \Gamma(v/2)} \int_0^x t^{v/2 - 1} e^{-t/2} dt 
1324  
1325     Here :math:`\Gamma` is the Gamma function; see `gamma`. This 
1326     integral can be expressed in terms of the regularized lower 
1327     incomplete gamma function `gammainc` as 
1328     ``gammainc(v / 2, x / 2)``. [1]_ 
1329  
1330     Parameters 
1331     ---------- 
1332     v : array_like 
1333         Degrees of freedom. 
1334     x : array_like 
1335         Upper bound of the integral. 
1336     out : ndarray, optional 
1337         Optional output array for the function results. 
1338  
1339     Returns 
1340     ------- 
1341     scalar or ndarray 
1342         Values of the cumulative distribution function. 
1343  
1344     See Also 
1345     -------- 
1346     chdtrc, chdtri, chdtriv, gammainc 
1347  
1348     References 
1349     ---------- 
1350     .. [1] Chi-Square distribution, 
1351         https://www.itl.nist.gov/div898/handbook/eda/section3/eda3666.htm 
1352  
1353     Examples 
1354     -------- 
1355     >>> import numpy as np 
1356     >>> import scipy.special as sc 
1357  
1358     It can be expressed in terms of the regularized lower incomplete 
1359     gamma function. 
1360  
1361     >>> v = 1 
1362     >>> x = np.arange(4) 
1363     >>> sc.chdtr(v, x) 
1364     array([0.        , 0.68268949, 0.84270079, 0.91673548]) 
1365     >>> sc.gammainc(v / 2, x / 2) 
1366     array([0.        , 0.68268949, 0.84270079, 0.91673548]) 
1367  
1368     """)
1369 
1370 add_newdoc("chdtrc",
1371     r""" 
1372     chdtrc(v, x, out=None) 
1373  
1374     Chi square survival function. 
1375  
1376     Returns the area under the right hand tail (from `x` to infinity) 
1377     of the Chi square probability density function with `v` degrees of 
1378     freedom: 
1379  
1380     .. math:: 
1381  
1382         \frac{1}{2^{v/2} \Gamma(v/2)} \int_x^\infty t^{v/2 - 1} e^{-t/2} dt 
1383  
1384     Here :math:`\Gamma` is the Gamma function; see `gamma`. This 
1385     integral can be expressed in terms of the regularized upper 
1386     incomplete gamma function `gammaincc` as 
1387     ``gammaincc(v / 2, x / 2)``. [1]_ 
1388  
1389     Parameters 
1390     ---------- 
1391     v : array_like 
1392         Degrees of freedom. 
1393     x : array_like 
1394         Lower bound of the integral. 
1395     out : ndarray, optional 
1396         Optional output array for the function results. 
1397  
1398     Returns 
1399     ------- 
1400     scalar or ndarray 
1401         Values of the survival function. 
1402  
1403     See Also 
1404     -------- 
1405     chdtr, chdtri, chdtriv, gammaincc 
1406  
1407     References 
1408     ---------- 
1409     .. [1] Chi-Square distribution, 
1410         https://www.itl.nist.gov/div898/handbook/eda/section3/eda3666.htm 
1411  
1412     Examples 
1413     -------- 
1414     >>> import numpy as np 
1415     >>> import scipy.special as sc 
1416  
1417     It can be expressed in terms of the regularized upper incomplete 
1418     gamma function. 
1419  
1420     >>> v = 1 
1421     >>> x = np.arange(4) 
1422     >>> sc.chdtrc(v, x) 
1423     array([1.        , 0.31731051, 0.15729921, 0.08326452]) 
1424     >>> sc.gammaincc(v / 2, x / 2) 
1425     array([1.        , 0.31731051, 0.15729921, 0.08326452])

1523 add_newdoc("chndtr",
1524     r""" 
1525     chndtr(x, df, nc, out=None) 
1526  
1527     Non-central chi square cumulative distribution function 
1528  
1529     The cumulative distribution function is given by: 
1530  
1531     .. math:: 
1532  
1533         P(\chi^{\prime 2} \vert \nu, \lambda) =\sum_{j=0}^{\infty} 
1534         e^{-\lambda /2} 
1535         \frac{(\lambda /2)^j}{j!} P(\chi^{\prime 2} \vert \nu + 2j), 
1536  
1537     where :math:`\nu > 0` is the degrees of freedom (``df``) and 
1538     :math:`\lambda \geq 0` is the non-centrality parameter (``nc``). 
1539  
1540     Parameters 
1541     ---------- 
1542     x : array_like 
1543         Upper bound of the integral; must satisfy ``x >= 0`` 
1544     df : array_like 
1545         Degrees of freedom; must satisfy ``df > 0`` 
1546     nc : array_like 
1547         Non-centrality parameter; must satisfy ``nc >= 0`` 
1548     out : ndarray, optional 
1549         Optional output array for the function results 
1550  
1551     Returns 
1552     ------- 
1553     x : scalar or ndarray 
1554         Value of the non-central chi square cumulative distribution function. 
1555  
1556     See Also 
1557     -------- 
1558     chndtrix, chndtridf, chndtrinc 
1559  
1560     """)
1561

1567

1569     desired value of `p`. 
1570  
1571     Parameters 
1572     ---------- 
1573     p : array_like 
1574         Probability; must satisfy ``0 <= p < 1`` 
1575     df : array_like 
1576         Degrees of freedom; must satisfy ``df > 0`` 
1577     nc : array_like 
1578         Non-centrality parameter; must satisfy ``nc >= 0`` 
1579     out : ndarray, optional 
1580         Optional output array for the function results 
1581  
1582     Returns 
1583     ------- 
1584     x : scalar or ndarray 
1585         Value so that the probability a non-central Chi square random variable 
1586         with `df` degrees of freedom and non-centrality, `nc`, is greater than 
1587         `x` equals `p`. 
1588  
1589     See Also

1701 add_newdoc(
1702     "elliprc",
1703     r""" 
1704     elliprc(x, y, out=None) 
1705  
1706     Degenerate symmetric elliptic integral. 
1707  
1708     The function RC is defined as [1]_ 
1709  
1710     .. math:: 
1711  
1712         R_{\mathrm{C}}(x, y) = 
1713            \frac{1}{2} \int_0^{+\infty} (t + x)^{-1/2} (t + y)^{-1} dt 
1714            = R_{\mathrm{F}}(x, y, y) 
1715  
1716     Parameters 
1717     ---------- 
1718     x, y : array_like 
1719         Real or complex input parameters. `x` can be any number in the 
1720         complex plane cut along the negative real axis. `y` must be non-zero. 
1721     out : ndarray, optional 
1722         Optional output array for the function values 
1723  
1724     Returns 
1725     ------- 
1726     R : scalar or ndarray 
1727         Value of the integral. If `y` is real and negative, the Cauchy 
1728         principal value is returned. If both of `x` and `y` are real, the 
1729         return value is real. Otherwise, the return value is complex. 
1730  
1731     See Also 
1732     -------- 
1733     elliprf : Completely-symmetric elliptic integral of the first kind. 
1734     elliprd : Symmetric elliptic integral of the second kind. 
1735     elliprg : Completely-symmetric elliptic integral of the second kind. 
1736     elliprj : Symmetric elliptic integral of the third kind. 
1737  
1738     Notes 
1739     ----- 
1740     RC is a degenerate case of the symmetric integral RF: ``elliprc(x, y) == 
1741     elliprf(x, y, y)``. It is an elementary function rather than an elliptic 
1742     integral. 
1743  
1744     The code implements Carlson's algorithm based on the duplication theorems 
1745     and series expansion up to the 7th order. [2]_ 
1746  
1747     .. versionadded:: 1.8.0 
1748  
1749     References 
1750     ---------- 
1751     .. [1] B. C. Carlson, ed., Chapter 19 in "Digital Library of Mathematical 
1752            Functions," NIST, US Dept. of Commerce. 
1753            https://dlmf.nist.gov/19.16.E6 
1754     .. [2] B. C. Carlson, "Numerical computation of real or complex elliptic 
1755            integrals," Numer. Algorithm, vol. 10, no. 1, pp. 13-26, 1995. 
1756            https://arxiv.org/abs/math/9409227 
1757            https://doi.org/10.1007/BF02198293 
1758  
1759     Examples 
1760     -------- 
1761     Basic homogeneity property: 
1762  
1763     >>> import numpy as np 
1764     >>> from scipy.special import elliprc 
1765  
1766     >>> x = 1.2 + 3.4j 
1767     >>> y = 5. 
1768     >>> scale = 0.3 + 0.4j 
1769     >>> elliprc(scale*x, scale*y) 
1770     (0.5484493976710874-0.4169557678995833j) 
1771  
1772     >>> elliprc(x, y)/np.sqrt(scale) 
1773     (0.5484493976710874-0.41695576789958333j) 
1774  
1775     When the two arguments coincide, the integral is particularly 
1776     simple: 
1777  
1778     >>> x = 1.2 + 3.4j 
1779     >>> elliprc(x, x) 
1780     (0.4299173120614631-0.3041729818745595j) 
1781  
1782     >>> 1/np.sqrt(x) 
1783     (0.4299173120614631-0.30417298187455954j) 
1784  
1785     Another simple case: the first argument vanishes: 
1786  
1787     >>> y = 1.2 + 3.4j 
1788     >>> elliprc(0, y) 
1789     (0.6753125346116815-0.47779380263880866j) 
1790  
1791     >>> np.pi/2/np.sqrt(y) 
1792     (0.6753125346116815-0.4777938026388088j) 
1793  
1794     When `x` and `y` are both positive, we can express 
1795     :math:`R_C(x,y)` in terms of more elementary functions.  For the 
1796     case :math:`0 \le x < y`, 
1797  
1798     >>> x = 3.2 
1799     >>> y = 6. 
1800     >>> elliprc(x, y) 
1801     0.44942991498453444 
1802  
1803     >>> np.arctan(np.sqrt((y-x)/x))/np.sqrt(y-x) 
1804     0.44942991498453433 
1805  
1806     And for the case :math:`0 \le y < x`, 
1807  
1808     >>> x = 6. 
1809     >>> y = 3.2 
1810     >>> elliprc(x,y) 
1811     0.4989837501576147 
1812  
1813     >>> np.log((np.sqrt(x)+np.sqrt(x-y))/np.sqrt(y))/np.sqrt(x-y) 
1814     0.49898375015761476 
1815  
1816     """)
1817 
1818 add_newdoc(
1819     "elliprd",
1820     r""" 
1821     elliprd(x, y, z, out=None) 
1822  
1823     Symmetric elliptic integral of the second kind. 
1824  
1825     The function RD is defined as [1]_ 
1826  
1827     .. math:: 
1828  
1829         R_{\mathrm{D}}(x, y, z) = 
1830            \frac{3}{2} \int_0^{+\infty} [(t + x) (t + y)]^{-1/2} (t + z)^{-3/2} 
1831            dt 
1832  
1833     Parameters 
1834     ---------- 
1835     x, y, z : array_like 
1836         Real or complex input parameters. `x` or `y` can be any number in the 
1837         complex plane cut along the negative real axis, but at most one of them 
1838         can be zero, while `z` must be non-zero. 
1839     out : ndarray, optional 
1840         Optional output array for the function values 
1841  
1842     Returns 
1843     ------- 
1844     R : scalar or ndarray 
1845         Value of the integral. If all of `x`, `y`, and `z` are real, the 
1846         return value is real. Otherwise, the return value is complex. 
1847  
1848     See Also 
1849     -------- 
1850     elliprc : Degenerate symmetric elliptic integral. 
1851     elliprf : Completely-symmetric elliptic integral of the first kind. 
1852     elliprg : Completely-symmetric elliptic integral of the second kind. 
1853     elliprj : Symmetric elliptic integral of the third kind. 
1854  
1855     Notes 
1856     ----- 
1857     RD is a degenerate case of the elliptic integral RJ: ``elliprd(x, y, z) == 
1858     elliprj(x, y, z, z)``. 
1859  
1860     The code implements Carlson's algorithm based on the duplication theorems 
1861     and series expansion up to the 7th order. [2]_ 
1862  
1863     .. versionadded:: 1.8.0 
1864  
1865     References 
1866     ---------- 
1867     .. [1] B. C. Carlson, ed., Chapter 19 in "Digital Library of Mathematical 
1868            Functions," NIST, US Dept. of Commerce. 
1869            https://dlmf.nist.gov/19.16.E5 
1870     .. [2] B. C. Carlson, "Numerical computation of real or complex elliptic 
1871            integrals," Numer. Algorithm, vol. 10, no. 1, pp. 13-26, 1995. 
1872            https://arxiv.org/abs/math/9409227 
1873            https://doi.org/10.1007/BF02198293 
1874  
1875     Examples 
1876     -------- 
1877     Basic homogeneity property: 
1878  
1879     >>> import numpy as np 
1880     >>> from scipy.special import elliprd 
1881  
1882     >>> x = 1.2 + 3.4j 
1883     >>> y = 5. 
1884     >>> z = 6. 
1885     >>> scale = 0.3 + 0.4j 
1886     >>> elliprd(scale*x, scale*y, scale*z) 
1887     (-0.03703043835680379-0.24500934665683802j) 
1888  
1889     >>> elliprd(x, y, z)*np.power(scale, -1.5) 
1890     (-0.0370304383568038-0.24500934665683805j) 
1891  
1892     All three arguments coincide: 
1893  
1894     >>> x = 1.2 + 3.4j 
1895     >>> elliprd(x, x, x) 
1896     (-0.03986825876151896-0.14051741840449586j) 
1897  
1898     >>> np.power(x, -1.5) 
1899     (-0.03986825876151894-0.14051741840449583j) 
1900  
1901     The so-called "second lemniscate constant": 
1902  
1903     >>> elliprd(0, 2, 1)/3 
1904     0.5990701173677961 
1905  
1906     >>> from scipy.special import gamma 
1907     >>> gamma(0.75)**2/np.sqrt(2*np.pi) 
1908     0.5990701173677959 
1909  
1910     """)
1911 
1912 add_newdoc(
1913     "elliprf",
1914     r""" 
1915     elliprf(x, y, z, out=None) 
1916  
1917     Completely-symmetric elliptic integral of the first kind. 
1918  
1919     The function RF is defined as [1]_ 
1920  
1921     .. math:: 
1922  
1923         R_{\mathrm{F}}(x, y, z) = 
1924            \frac{1}{2} \int_0^{+\infty} [(t + x) (t + y) (t + z)]^{-1/2} dt 
1925  
1926     Parameters 
1927     ---------- 
1928     x, y, z : array_like 
1929         Real or complex input parameters. `x`, `y`, or `z` can be any number in 
1930         the complex plane cut along the negative real axis, but at most one of 
1931         them can be zero. 
1932     out : ndarray, optional 
1933         Optional output array for the function values 
1934  
1935     Returns 
1936     ------- 
1937     R : scalar or ndarray 
1938         Value of the integral. If all of `x`, `y`, and `z` are real, the return 
1939         value is real. Otherwise, the return value is complex. 
1940  
1941     See Also 
1942     -------- 
1943     elliprc : Degenerate symmetric integral. 
1944     elliprd : Symmetric elliptic integral of the second kind. 
1945     elliprg : Completely-symmetric elliptic integral of the second kind. 
1946     elliprj : Symmetric elliptic integral of the third kind. 
1947  
1948     Notes 
1949     ----- 
1950     The code implements Carlson's algorithm based on the duplication theorems 
1951     and series expansion up to the 7th order (cf.: 
1952     https://dlmf.nist.gov/19.36.i) and the AGM algorithm for the complete 
1953     integral. [2]_ 
1954  
1955     .. versionadded:: 1.8.0 
1956  
1957     References 
1958     ---------- 
1959     .. [1] B. C. Carlson, ed., Chapter 19 in "Digital Library of Mathematical 
1960            Functions," NIST, US Dept. of Commerce. 
1961            https://dlmf.nist.gov/19.16.E1 
1962     .. [2] B. C. Carlson, "Numerical computation of real or complex elliptic 
1963            integrals," Numer. Algorithm, vol. 10, no. 1, pp. 13-26, 1995. 
1964            https://arxiv.org/abs/math/9409227 
1965            https://doi.org/10.1007/BF02198293 
1966  
1967     Examples 
1968     -------- 
1969     Basic homogeneity property: 
1970  
1971     >>> import numpy as np 
1972     >>> from scipy.special import elliprf 
1973  
1974     >>> x = 1.2 + 3.4j 
1975     >>> y = 5. 
1976     >>> z = 6. 
1977     >>> scale = 0.3 + 0.4j 
1978     >>> elliprf(scale*x, scale*y, scale*z) 
1979     (0.5328051227278146-0.4008623567957094j) 
1980  
1981     >>> elliprf(x, y, z)/np.sqrt(scale) 
1982     (0.5328051227278147-0.4008623567957095j) 
1983  
1984     All three arguments coincide: 
1985  
1986     >>> x = 1.2 + 3.4j 
1987     >>> elliprf(x, x, x) 
1988     (0.42991731206146316-0.30417298187455954j) 
1989  
1990     >>> 1/np.sqrt(x) 
1991     (0.4299173120614631-0.30417298187455954j) 
1992  
1993     The so-called "first lemniscate constant": 
1994  
1995     >>> elliprf(0, 1, 2) 
1996     1.3110287771460598 
1997  
1998     >>> from scipy.special import gamma 
1999     >>> gamma(0.25)**2/(4*np.sqrt(2*np.pi)) 
2000     1.3110287771460598 
2001  
2002     """)
2003 
2004 add_newdoc(
2005     "elliprg",
2006     r""" 
2007     elliprg(x, y, z, out=None) 
2008  
2009     Completely-symmetric elliptic integral of the second kind. 
2010  
2011     The function RG is defined as [1]_ 
2012  
2013     .. math:: 
2014  
2015         R_{\mathrm{G}}(x, y, z) = 
2016            \frac{1}{4} \int_0^{+\infty} [(t + x) (t + y) (t + z)]^{-1/2} 
2017            \left(\frac{x}{t + x} + \frac{y}{t + y} + \frac{z}{t + z}\right) t 
2018            dt 
2019  
2020     Parameters 
2021     ---------- 
2022     x, y, z : array_like 
2023         Real or complex input parameters. `x`, `y`, or `z` can be any number in 
2024         the complex plane cut along the negative real axis. 
2025     out : ndarray, optional 
2026         Optional output array for the function values 
2027  
2028     Returns 
2029     ------- 
2030     R : scalar or ndarray 
2031         Value of the integral. If all of `x`, `y`, and `z` are real, the return 
2032         value is real. Otherwise, the return value is complex. 
2033  
2034     See Also 
2035     -------- 
2036     elliprc : Degenerate symmetric integral. 
2037     elliprd : Symmetric elliptic integral of the second kind. 
2038     elliprf : Completely-symmetric elliptic integral of the first kind. 
2039     elliprj : Symmetric elliptic integral of the third kind. 
2040  
2041     Notes 
2042     ----- 
2043     The implementation uses the relation [1]_ 
2044  
2045     .. math:: 
2046  
2047         2 R_{\mathrm{G}}(x, y, z) = 
2048            z R_{\mathrm{F}}(x, y, z) - 
2049            \frac{1}{3} (x - z) (y - z) R_{\mathrm{D}}(x, y, z) + 
2050            \sqrt{\frac{x y}{z}} 
2051  
2052     and the symmetry of `x`, `y`, `z` when at least one non-zero parameter can 
2053     be chosen as the pivot. When one of the arguments is close to zero, the AGM 
2054     method is applied instead. Other special cases are computed following Ref. 
2055     [2]_ 
2056  
2057     .. versionadded:: 1.8.0 
2058  
2059     References 
2060     ---------- 
2061     .. [1] B. C. Carlson, "Numerical computation of real or complex elliptic 
2062            integrals," Numer. Algorithm, vol. 10, no. 1, pp. 13-26, 1995. 
2063            https://arxiv.org/abs/math/9409227 
2064            https://doi.org/10.1007/BF02198293 
2065     .. [2] B. C. Carlson, ed., Chapter 19 in "Digital Library of Mathematical 
2066            Functions," NIST, US Dept. of Commerce. 
2067            https://dlmf.nist.gov/19.16.E1 
2068            https://dlmf.nist.gov/19.20.ii 
2069  
2070     Examples 
2071     -------- 
2072     Basic homogeneity property: 
2073  
2074     >>> import numpy as np 
2075     >>> from scipy.special import elliprg 
2076  
2077     >>> x = 1.2 + 3.4j 
2078     >>> y = 5. 
2079     >>> z = 6. 
2080     >>> scale = 0.3 + 0.4j 
2081     >>> elliprg(scale*x, scale*y, scale*z) 
2082     (1.195936862005246+0.8470988320464167j) 
2083  
2084     >>> elliprg(x, y, z)*np.sqrt(scale) 
2085     (1.195936862005246+0.8470988320464165j) 
2086  
2087     Simplifications: 
2088  
2089     >>> elliprg(0, y, y) 
2090     1.756203682760182 
2091  
2092     >>> 0.25*np.pi*np.sqrt(y) 
2093     1.7562036827601817 
2094  
2095     >>> elliprg(0, 0, z) 
2096     1.224744871391589 
2097  
2098     >>> 0.5*np.sqrt(z) 
2099     1.224744871391589 
2100  
2101     The surface area of a triaxial ellipsoid with semiaxes ``a``, ``b``, and 
2102     ``c`` is given by 
2103  
2104     .. math:: 
2105  
2106         S = 4 \pi a b c R_{\mathrm{G}}(1 / a^2, 1 / b^2, 1 / c^2). 
2107  
2108     >>> def ellipsoid_area(a, b, c): 
2109     ...     r = 4.0 * np.pi * a * b * c 
2110     ...     return r * elliprg(1.0 / (a * a), 1.0 / (b * b), 1.0 / (c * c)) 
2111     >>> print(ellipsoid_area(1, 3, 5)) 
2112     108.62688289491807 
2113     """)
2114 
2115 add_newdoc(
2116     "elliprj",
2117     r""" 
2118     elliprj(x, y, z, p, out=None) 
2119  
2120     Symmetric elliptic integral of the third kind. 
2121  
2122     The function RJ is defined as [1]_ 
2123  
2124     .. math:: 
2125  
2126         R_{\mathrm{J}}(x, y, z, p) = 
2127            \frac{3}{2} \int_0^{+\infty} [(t + x) (t + y) (t + z)]^{-1/2} 
2128            (t + p)^{-1} dt 
2129  
2130     .. warning:: 
2131         This function should be considered experimental when the inputs are 
2132         unbalanced.  Check correctness with another independent implementation. 
2133  
2134     Parameters 
2135     ---------- 
2136     x, y, z, p : array_like 
2137         Real or complex input parameters. `x`, `y`, or `z` are numbers in 
2138         the complex plane cut along the negative real axis (subject to further 
2139         constraints, see Notes), and at most one of them can be zero. `p` must 
2140         be non-zero. 
2141     out : ndarray, optional 
2142         Optional output array for the function values 
2143  
2144     Returns 
2145     ------- 
2146     R : scalar or ndarray 
2147         Value of the integral. If all of `x`, `y`, `z`, and `p` are real, the 
2148         return value is real. Otherwise, the return value is complex. 
2149  
2150         If `p` is real and negative, while `x`, `y`, and `z` are real, 
2151         non-negative, and at most one of them is zero, the Cauchy principal 
2152         value is returned. [1]_ [2]_ 
2153  
2154     See Also 
2155     -------- 
2156     elliprc : Degenerate symmetric integral. 
2157     elliprd : Symmetric elliptic integral of the second kind. 
2158     elliprf : Completely-symmetric elliptic integral of the first kind. 
2159     elliprg : Completely-symmetric elliptic integral of the second kind. 
2160  
2161     Notes 
2162     ----- 
2163     The code implements Carlson's algorithm based on the duplication theorems 
2164     and series expansion up to the 7th order. [3]_ The algorithm is slightly 
2165     different from its earlier incarnation as it appears in [1]_, in that the 
2166     call to `elliprc` (or ``atan``/``atanh``, see [4]_) is no longer needed in 
2167     the inner loop. Asymptotic approximations are used where arguments differ 
2168     widely in the order of magnitude. [5]_ 
2169  
2170     The input values are subject to certain sufficient but not necessary 
2171     constraints when input arguments are complex. Notably, ``x``, ``y``, and 
2172     ``z`` must have non-negative real parts, unless two of them are 
2173     non-negative and complex-conjugates to each other while the other is a real 
2174     non-negative number. [1]_ If the inputs do not satisfy the sufficient 
2175     condition described in Ref. [1]_ they are rejected outright with the output 
2176     set to NaN. 
2177  
2178     In the case where one of ``x``, ``y``, and ``z`` is equal to ``p``, the 
2179     function ``elliprd`` should be preferred because of its less restrictive 
2180     domain. 
2181  
2182     .. versionadded:: 1.8.0 
2183  
2184     References 
2185     ---------- 
2186     .. [1] B. C. Carlson, "Numerical computation of real or complex elliptic 
2187            integrals," Numer. Algorithm, vol. 10, no. 1, pp. 13-26, 1995. 
2188            https://arxiv.org/abs/math/9409227 
2189            https://doi.org/10.1007/BF02198293 
2190     .. [2] B. C. Carlson, ed., Chapter 19 in "Digital Library of Mathematical 
2191            Functions," NIST, US Dept. of Commerce. 
2192            https://dlmf.nist.gov/19.20.iii 
2193     .. [3] B. C. Carlson, J. FitzSimmons, "Reduction Theorems for Elliptic 
2194            Integrands with the Square Root of Two Quadratic Factors," J. 
2195            Comput. Appl. Math., vol. 118, nos. 1-2, pp. 71-85, 2000. 
2196            https://doi.org/10.1016/S0377-0427(00)00282-X 
2197     .. [4] F. Johansson, "Numerical Evaluation of Elliptic Functions, Elliptic 
2198            Integrals and Modular Forms," in J. Blumlein, C. Schneider, P. 
2199            Paule, eds., "Elliptic Integrals, Elliptic Functions and Modular 
2200            Forms in Quantum Field Theory," pp. 269-293, 2019 (Cham, 
2201            Switzerland: Springer Nature Switzerland) 
2202            https://arxiv.org/abs/1806.06725 
2203            https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-030-04480-0 
2204     .. [5] B. C. Carlson, J. L. Gustafson, "Asymptotic Approximations for 
2205            Symmetric Elliptic Integrals," SIAM J. Math. Anls., vol. 25, no. 2, 
2206            pp. 288-303, 1994. 
2207            https://arxiv.org/abs/math/9310223 
2208            https://doi.org/10.1137/S0036141092228477 
2209  
2210     Examples 
2211     -------- 
2212     Basic homogeneity property: 
2213  
2214     >>> import numpy as np 
2215     >>> from scipy.special import elliprj 
2216  
2217     >>> x = 1.2 + 3.4j 
2218     >>> y = 5. 
2219     >>> z = 6. 
2220     >>> p = 7. 
2221     >>> scale = 0.3 - 0.4j 
2222     >>> elliprj(scale*x, scale*y, scale*z, scale*p) 
2223     (0.10834905565679157+0.19694950747103812j) 
2224  
2225     >>> elliprj(x, y, z, p)*np.power(scale, -1.5) 
2226     (0.10834905565679556+0.19694950747103854j) 
2227  
2228     Reduction to simpler elliptic integral: 
2229  
2230     >>> elliprj(x, y, z, z) 
2231     (0.08288462362195129-0.028376809745123258j) 
2232  
2233     >>> from scipy.special import elliprd 
2234     >>> elliprd(x, y, z) 
2235     (0.08288462362195136-0.028376809745123296j) 
2236  
2237     All arguments coincide: 
2238  
2239     >>> elliprj(x, x, x, x) 
2240     (-0.03986825876151896-0.14051741840449586j) 
2241  
2242     >>> np.power(x, -1.5) 
2243     (-0.03986825876151894-0.14051741840449583j) 
2244  
2245     """)
2246 
2247 add_newdoc("entr",
2248     r""" 
2249     entr(x, out=None) 
2250  
2251     Elementwise function for computing entropy. 
2252  
2253     .. math:: \text{entr}(x) = \begin{cases} - x \log(x) & x > 0  \\ 0 & x = 0 
2254               \\ -\infty & \text{otherwise} \end{cases} 
2255  
2256     Parameters 
2257     ---------- 
2258     x : ndarray 
2259         Input array. 
2260     out : ndarray, optional 
2261         Optional output array for the function values 
2262  
2263     Returns 
2264     ------- 
2265     res : scalar or ndarray 
2266         The value of the elementwise entropy function at the given points `x`. 
2267  
2268     See Also 
2269     -------- 
2270     kl_div, rel_entr, scipy.stats.entropy 
2271  
2272     Notes 
2273     ----- 
2274     .. versionadded:: 0.15.0 
2275  
2276     This function is concave. 
2277  
2278     The origin of this function is in convex programming; see [1]_. 
2279     Given a probability distribution :math:`p_1, \ldots, p_n`, 
2280     the definition of entropy in the context of *information theory* is 
2281  
2282     .. math:: 
2283  
2284         \sum_{i = 1}^n \mathrm{entr}(p_i). 
2285  
2286     To compute the latter quantity, use `scipy.stats.entropy`. 
2287  
2288     References 
2289     ---------- 
2290     .. [1] Boyd, Stephen and Lieven Vandenberghe. *Convex optimization*. 
2291            Cambridge University Press, 2004. 
2292            :doi:`https://doi.org/10.1017/CBO9780511804441` 
2293  
2294     """)

2296 add_newdoc("erf",
2297     """ 
2298     erf(z, out=None) 
2299  
2300     Returns the error function of complex argument. 
2301  
2302     It is defined as ``2/sqrt(pi)*integral(exp(-t**2), t=0..z)``. 
2303  
2304     Parameters 
2305     ---------- 
2306     x : ndarray 
2307         Input array. 
2308     out : ndarray, optional 
2309         Optional output array for the function values 
2310  
2311     Returns 
2312     ------- 
2313     res : scalar or ndarray 
2314         The values of the error function at the given points `x`. 
2315  
2316     See Also 
2317     -------- 
2318     erfc, erfinv, erfcinv, wofz, erfcx, erfi 
2319  
2320     Notes 
2321     ----- 
2322     The cumulative of the unit normal distribution is given by 
2323     ``Phi(z) = 1/2[1 + erf(z/sqrt(2))]``. 
2324  
2325     References 
2326     ---------- 
2327     .. [1] https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Error_function 
2328     .. [2] Milton Abramowitz and Irene A. Stegun, eds. 
2329         Handbook of Mathematical Functions with Formulas, 
2330         Graphs, and Mathematical Tables. New York: Dover, 
2331         1972. http://www.math.sfu.ca/~cbm/aands/page_297.htm 
2332     .. [3] Steven G. Johnson, Faddeeva W function implementation. 
2333        http://ab-initio.mit.edu/Faddeeva 
2334  
2335     Examples 
2336     -------- 
2337     >>> import numpy as np 
2338     >>> from scipy import special 
2339     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
2340     >>> x = np.linspace(-3, 3) 
2341     >>> plt.plot(x, special.erf(x)) 
2342     >>> plt.xlabel('$x$') 
2343     >>> plt.ylabel('$erf(x)$') 
2344     >>> plt.show()

2346     """)

2479 add_newdoc(
2480     "erfinv",
2481     """ 
2482     erfinv(y, out=None) 
2483  
2484     Inverse of the error function. 
2485  
2486     Computes the inverse of the error function. 
2487  
2488     In the complex domain, there is no unique complex number w satisfying 
2489     erf(w)=z. This indicates a true inverse function would be multivalued. 
2490     When the domain restricts to the real, -1 < x < 1, there is a unique real 
2491     number satisfying erf(erfinv(x)) = x. 
2492  
2493     Parameters 
2494     ---------- 
2495     y : ndarray 
2496         Argument at which to evaluate. Domain: [-1, 1] 
2497     out : ndarray, optional 
2498         Optional output array for the function values 
2499  
2500     Returns 
2501     ------- 
2502     erfinv : scalar or ndarray 
2503         The inverse of erf of y, element-wise 
2504  
2505     See Also 
2506     -------- 
2507     erf : Error function of a complex argument 
2508     erfc : Complementary error function, ``1 - erf(x)`` 
2509     erfcinv : Inverse of the complementary error function 
2510  
2511     Notes 
2512     ----- 
2513     This function wraps the ``erf_inv`` routine from the 
2514     Boost Math C++ library [1]_. 
2515  
2516     References 
2517     ---------- 
2518     .. [1] The Boost Developers. "Boost C++ Libraries". https://www.boost.org/. 
2519  
2520     Examples 
2521     -------- 
2522     >>> import numpy as np 
2523     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
2524     >>> from scipy.special import erfinv, erf 
2525  
2526     >>> erfinv(0.5) 
2527     0.4769362762044699 
2528  
2529     >>> y = np.linspace(-1.0, 1.0, num=9) 
2530     >>> x = erfinv(y) 
2531     >>> x 
2532     array([       -inf, -0.81341985, -0.47693628, -0.22531206,  0.        , 
2533             0.22531206,  0.47693628,  0.81341985,         inf]) 
2534  
2535     Verify that ``erf(erfinv(y))`` is ``y``. 
2536  
2537     >>> erf(x) 
2538     array([-1.  , -0.75, -0.5 , -0.25,  0.  ,  0.25,  0.5 ,  0.75,  1.  ]) 
2539  
2540     Plot the function: 
2541  
2542     >>> y = np.linspace(-1, 1, 200) 
2543     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
2544     >>> ax.plot(y, erfinv(y)) 
2545     >>> ax.grid(True) 
2546     >>> ax.set_xlabel('y') 
2547     >>> ax.set_title('erfinv(y)') 
2548     >>> plt.show() 
2549  
2550     """)

2555     erfcinv(y, out=None) 
2556  
2557     Inverse of the complementary error function. 
2558  
2559     Computes the inverse of the complementary error function. 
2560  
2561     In the complex domain, there is no unique complex number w satisfying 
2562     erfc(w)=z. This indicates a true inverse function would be multivalued. 
2563     When the domain restricts to the real, 0 < x < 2, there is a unique real 
2564     number satisfying erfc(erfcinv(x)) = erfcinv(erfc(x)). 
2565  
2566     It is related to inverse of the error function by erfcinv(1-x) = erfinv(x) 
2567  
2568     Parameters 
2569     ----------

2572     out : ndarray, optional 
2573         Optional output array for the function values 
2574  
2575     Returns 
2576     ------- 
2577     erfcinv : scalar or ndarray 
2578         The inverse of erfc of y, element-wise 
2579  
2580     See Also 
2581     -------- 
2582     erf : Error function of a complex argument 
2583     erfc : Complementary error function, ``1 - erf(x)`` 
2584     erfinv : Inverse of the error function 
2585  
2586     Examples 
2587     -------- 
2588     >>> import numpy as np 
2589     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
2590     >>> from scipy.special import erfcinv 
2591  
2592     >>> erfcinv(0.5) 
2593     0.4769362762044699 
2594  
2595     >>> y = np.linspace(0.0, 2.0, num=11) 
2596     >>> erfcinv(y) 
2597     array([        inf,  0.9061938 ,  0.59511608,  0.37080716,  0.17914345, 
2598            -0.        , -0.17914345, -0.37080716, -0.59511608, -0.9061938 , 
2599                   -inf]) 
2600  
2601     Plot the function: 
2602  
2603     >>> y = np.linspace(0, 2, 200) 
2604     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
2605     >>> ax.plot(y, erfcinv(y))

2613 add_newdoc("eval_jacobi",
2614     r""" 
2615     eval_jacobi(n, alpha, beta, x, out=None) 
2616  
2617     Evaluate Jacobi polynomial at a point. 
2618  
2619     The Jacobi polynomials can be defined via the Gauss hypergeometric 
2620     function :math:`{}_2F_1` as 
2621  
2622     .. math:: 
2623  
2624         P_n^{(\alpha, \beta)}(x) = \frac{(\alpha + 1)_n}{\Gamma(n + 1)} 
2625           {}_2F_1(-n, 1 + \alpha + \beta + n; \alpha + 1; (1 - z)/2) 
2626  
2627     where :math:`(\cdot)_n` is the Pochhammer symbol; see `poch`. When 
2628     :math:`n` is an integer the result is a polynomial of degree 
2629     :math:`n`. See 22.5.42 in [AS]_ for details. 
2630  
2631     Parameters 
2632     ---------- 
2633     n : array_like 
2634         Degree of the polynomial. If not an integer the result is 
2635         determined via the relation to the Gauss hypergeometric 
2636         function. 
2637     alpha : array_like 
2638         Parameter 
2639     beta : array_like 
2640         Parameter 
2641     x : array_like 
2642         Points at which to evaluate the polynomial 
2643     out : ndarray, optional 
2644         Optional output array for the function values 
2645  
2646     Returns 
2647     ------- 
2648     P : scalar or ndarray 
2649         Values of the Jacobi polynomial 
2650  
2651     See Also 
2652     -------- 
2653     roots_jacobi : roots and quadrature weights of Jacobi polynomials 
2654     jacobi : Jacobi polynomial object 
2655     hyp2f1 : Gauss hypergeometric function 
2656  
2657     References 
2658     ---------- 
2659     .. [AS] Milton Abramowitz and Irene A. Stegun, eds. 
2660         Handbook of Mathematical Functions with Formulas, 
2661         Graphs, and Mathematical Tables. New York: Dover, 1972. 
2662  
2663     """)
2664

2666     r""" 
2667     eval_sh_jacobi(n, p, q, x, out=None) 
2668  
2669     Evaluate shifted Jacobi polynomial at a point. 
2670  
2671     Defined by 
2672  
2673     .. math:: 
2674  
2675         G_n^{(p, q)}(x) 
2676           = \binom{2n + p - 1}{n}^{-1} P_n^{(p - q, q - 1)}(2x - 1), 
2677  
2678     where :math:`P_n^{(\cdot, \cdot)}` is the n-th Jacobi 
2679     polynomial. See 22.5.2 in [AS]_ for details. 
2680  
2681     Parameters 
2682     ---------- 
2683     n : int 
2684         Degree of the polynomial. If not an integer, the result is 
2685         determined via the relation to `binom` and `eval_jacobi`. 
2686     p : float 
2687         Parameter 
2688     q : float 
2689         Parameter 
2690     out : ndarray, optional 
2691         Optional output array for the function values 
2692  
2693     Returns 
2694     ------- 
2695     G : scalar or ndarray 
2696         Values of the shifted Jacobi polynomial. 
2697  
2698     See Also 
2699     -------- 
2700     roots_sh_jacobi : roots and quadrature weights of shifted Jacobi 
2701                       polynomials 
2702     sh_jacobi : shifted Jacobi polynomial object 
2703     eval_jacobi : evaluate Jacobi polynomials 
2704  
2705     References 
2706     ---------- 
2707     .. [AS] Milton Abramowitz and Irene A. Stegun, eds. 
2708         Handbook of Mathematical Functions with Formulas, 
2709         Graphs, and Mathematical Tables. New York: Dover, 1972. 
2710  
2711     """)

2734         determined via the relation to the Gauss hypergeometric 
2735         function.

2740     out : ndarray, optional

2747  
2748     See Also 
2749     --------

2763 add_newdoc("eval_chebyt",
2764     r""" 
2765     eval_chebyt(n, x, out=None) 
2766  
2767     Evaluate Chebyshev polynomial of the first kind at a point. 
2768  
2769     The Chebyshev polynomials of the first kind can be defined via the 
2770     Gauss hypergeometric function :math:`{}_2F_1` as 
2771  
2772     .. math:: 
2773  
2774         T_n(x) = {}_2F_1(n, -n; 1/2; (1 - x)/2). 
2775  
2776     When :math:`n` is an integer the result is a polynomial of degree 
2777     :math:`n`. See 22.5.47 in [AS]_ for details. 
2778  
2779     Parameters 
2780     ---------- 
2781     n : array_like 
2782         Degree of the polynomial. If not an integer, the result is 
2783         determined via the relation to the Gauss hypergeometric 
2784         function. 
2785     x : array_like 
2786         Points at which to evaluate the Chebyshev polynomial 
2787     out : ndarray, optional 
2788         Optional output array for the function values 
2789  
2790     Returns 
2791     ------- 
2792     T : scalar or ndarray 
2793         Values of the Chebyshev polynomial 
2794  
2795     See Also 
2796     -------- 
2797     roots_chebyt : roots and quadrature weights of Chebyshev 
2798                    polynomials of the first kind 
2799     chebyu : Chebychev polynomial object 
2800     eval_chebyu : evaluate Chebyshev polynomials of the second kind 
2801     hyp2f1 : Gauss hypergeometric function 
2802     numpy.polynomial.chebyshev.Chebyshev : Chebyshev series 
2803  
2804     Notes 
2805     ----- 
2806     This routine is numerically stable for `x` in ``[-1, 1]`` at least 
2807     up to order ``10000``. 
2808  
2809     References 
2810     ---------- 
2811     .. [AS] Milton Abramowitz and Irene A. Stegun, eds. 
2812         Handbook of Mathematical Functions with Formulas, 
2813         Graphs, and Mathematical Tables. New York: Dover, 1972. 
2814  
2815     """)

2868  
2869     Evaluate Chebyshev polynomial of the second kind on [-2, 2] at a

2871

2873  
2874     .. math:: 
2875  
2876         S_n(x) = U_n(x/2) 
2877

2879     kind. See 22.5.13 in [AS]_ for details. 
2880

2888     out : ndarray, optional 
2889         Optional output array for the function values

2895

2902

2906         Handbook of Mathematical Functions with Formulas, 
2907         Graphs, and Mathematical Tables. New York: Dover, 1972. 
2908

2910     --------

3079 add_newdoc("eval_legendre",
3080     r""" 
3081     eval_legendre(n, x, out=None) 
3082  
3083     Evaluate Legendre polynomial at a point. 
3084  
3085     The Legendre polynomials can be defined via the Gauss 
3086     hypergeometric function :math:`{}_2F_1` as 
3087  
3088     .. math:: 
3089  
3090         P_n(x) = {}_2F_1(-n, n + 1; 1; (1 - x)/2). 
3091  
3092     When :math:`n` is an integer the result is a polynomial of degree 
3093     :math:`n`. See 22.5.49 in [AS]_ for details. 
3094  
3095     Parameters 
3096     ---------- 
3097     n : array_like 
3098         Degree of the polynomial. If not an integer, the result is 
3099         determined via the relation to the Gauss hypergeometric 
3100         function. 
3101     x : array_like 
3102         Points at which to evaluate the Legendre polynomial 
3103     out : ndarray, optional 
3104         Optional output array for the function values 
3105  
3106     Returns 
3107     ------- 
3108     P : scalar or ndarray 
3109         Values of the Legendre polynomial 
3110  
3111     See Also 
3112     -------- 
3113     roots_legendre : roots and quadrature weights of Legendre 
3114                      polynomials 
3115     legendre : Legendre polynomial object 
3116     hyp2f1 : Gauss hypergeometric function 
3117     numpy.polynomial.legendre.Legendre : Legendre series 
3118  
3119     References 
3120     ---------- 
3121     .. [AS] Milton Abramowitz and Irene A. Stegun, eds. 
3122         Handbook of Mathematical Functions with Formulas, 
3123         Graphs, and Mathematical Tables. New York: Dover, 1972. 
3124  
3125     Examples 
3126     -------- 
3127     >>> import numpy as np 
3128     >>> from scipy.special import eval_legendre 
3129  
3130     Evaluate the zero-order Legendre polynomial at x = 0 
3131  
3132     >>> eval_legendre(0, 0) 
3133     1.0 
3134  
3135     Evaluate the first-order Legendre polynomial between -1 and 1 
3136  
3137     >>> X = np.linspace(-1, 1, 5)  # Domain of Legendre polynomials 
3138     >>> eval_legendre(1, X) 
3139     array([-1. , -0.5,  0. ,  0.5,  1. ]) 
3140  
3141     Evaluate Legendre polynomials of order 0 through 4 at x = 0 
3142  
3143     >>> N = range(0, 5) 
3144     >>> eval_legendre(N, 0) 
3145     array([ 1.   ,  0.   , -0.5  ,  0.   ,  0.375]) 
3146  
3147     Plot Legendre polynomials of order 0 through 4 
3148  
3149     >>> X = np.linspace(-1, 1) 
3150  
3151     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
3152     >>> for n in range(0, 5): 
3153     ...     y = eval_legendre(n, X) 
3154     ...     plt.plot(X, y, label=r'$P_{}(x)$'.format(n)) 
3155  
3156     >>> plt.title("Legendre Polynomials") 
3157     >>> plt.xlabel("x") 
3158     >>> plt.ylabel(r'$P_n(x)$') 
3159     >>> plt.legend(loc='lower right') 
3160     >>> plt.show() 
3161  
3162     """)

3212     eval_genlaguerre(n, alpha, x, out=None) 
3213  
3214     Evaluate generalized Laguerre polynomial at a point. 
3215  
3216     The generalized Laguerre polynomials can be defined via the 
3217     confluent hypergeometric function :math:`{}_1F_1` as 
3218

3220

3222           {}_1F_1(-n, \alpha + 1, x). 
3223

3225     :math:`n`. See 22.5.54 in [AS]_ for details. The Laguerre 
3226     polynomials are the special case where :math:`\alpha = 0`. 
3227  
3228     Parameters 
3229     ---------- 
3230     n : array_like 
3231         Degree of the polynomial. If not an integer, the result is 
3232         determined via the relation to the confluent hypergeometric 
3233         function. 
3234     alpha : array_like 
3235         Parameter; must have ``alpha > -1`` 
3236     x : array_like 
3237         Points at which to evaluate the generalized Laguerre 
3238         polynomial 
3239     out : ndarray, optional 
3240         Optional output array for the function values 
3241  
3242     Returns 
3243     ------- 
3244     L : scalar or ndarray 
3245         Values of the generalized Laguerre polynomial 
3246  
3247     See Also 
3248     -------- 
3249     roots_genlaguerre : roots and quadrature weights of generalized 
3250                         Laguerre polynomials 
3251     genlaguerre : generalized Laguerre polynomial object 
3252     hyp1f1 : confluent hypergeometric function 
3253     eval_laguerre : evaluate Laguerre polynomials 
3254  
3255     References 
3256     ---------- 
3257     .. [AS] Milton Abramowitz and Irene A. Stegun, eds. 
3258         Handbook of Mathematical Functions with Formulas, 
3259         Graphs, and Mathematical Tables. New York: Dover, 1972. 
3260  
3261     """)
