3466 add_newdoc("expm1",
3467     """ 
3468     expm1(x, out=None) 
3469  
3470     Compute ``exp(x) - 1``. 
3471  
3472     When `x` is near zero, ``exp(x)`` is near 1, so the numerical calculation 
3473     of ``exp(x) - 1`` can suffer from catastrophic loss of precision. 
3474     ``expm1(x)`` is implemented to avoid the loss of precision that occurs when 
3475     `x` is near zero. 
3476  
3477     Parameters 
3478     ---------- 
3479     x : array_like 
3480         `x` must contain real numbers. 
3481     out : ndarray, optional 
3482         Optional output array for the function values 
3483  
3484     Returns 
3485     ------- 
3486     scalar or ndarray 
3487         ``exp(x) - 1`` computed element-wise. 
3488  
3489     Examples 
3490     -------- 
3491     >>> import numpy as np 
3492     >>> from scipy.special import expm1 
3493  
3494     >>> expm1(1.0) 
3495     1.7182818284590451 
3496     >>> expm1([-0.2, -0.1, 0, 0.1, 0.2]) 
3497     array([-0.18126925, -0.09516258,  0.        ,  0.10517092,  0.22140276]) 
3498  
3499     The exact value of ``exp(7.5e-13) - 1`` is:: 
3500  
3501         7.5000000000028125000000007031250000001318...*10**-13. 
3502  
3503     Here is what ``expm1(7.5e-13)`` gives: 
3504  
3505     >>> expm1(7.5e-13) 
3506     7.5000000000028135e-13 
3507  
3508     Compare that to ``exp(7.5e-13) - 1``, where the subtraction results in 
3509     a "catastrophic" loss of precision: 
3510  
3511     >>> np.exp(7.5e-13) - 1 
3512     7.5006667543675576e-13 
3513  
3514     """)
3515 
3516 add_newdoc("expn",
3517     r""" 
3518     expn(n, x, out=None) 
3519  
3520     Generalized exponential integral En. 
3521  
3522     For integer :math:`n \geq 0` and real :math:`x \geq 0` the 
3523     generalized exponential integral is defined as [dlmf]_ 
3524  
3525     .. math:: 
3526  
3527         E_n(x) = x^{n - 1} \int_x^\infty \frac{e^{-t}}{t^n} dt. 
3528  
3529     Parameters 
3530     ---------- 
3531     n : array_like 
3532         Non-negative integers 
3533     x : array_like 
3534         Real argument 
3535     out : ndarray, optional 
3536         Optional output array for the function results 
3537  
3538     Returns 
3539     ------- 
3540     scalar or ndarray 
3541         Values of the generalized exponential integral 
3542  
3543     See Also 
3544     -------- 
3545     exp1 : special case of :math:`E_n` for :math:`n = 1` 
3546     expi : related to :math:`E_n` when :math:`n = 1` 
3547  
3548     References 
3549     ---------- 
3550     .. [dlmf] Digital Library of Mathematical Functions, 8.19.2 
3551               https://dlmf.nist.gov/8.19#E2 
3552  
3553     Examples 
3554     -------- 
3555     >>> import numpy as np 
3556     >>> import scipy.special as sc 
3557  
3558     Its domain is nonnegative n and x. 
3559  
3560     >>> sc.expn(-1, 1.0), sc.expn(1, -1.0) 
3561     (nan, nan) 
3562  
3563     It has a pole at ``x = 0`` for ``n = 1, 2``; for larger ``n`` it 
3564     is equal to ``1 / (n - 1)``. 
3565  
3566     >>> sc.expn([0, 1, 2, 3, 4], 0) 
3567     array([       inf,        inf, 1.        , 0.5       , 0.33333333]) 
3568  
3569     For n equal to 0 it reduces to ``exp(-x) / x``. 
3570  
3571     >>> x = np.array([1, 2, 3, 4]) 
3572     >>> sc.expn(0, x) 
3573     array([0.36787944, 0.06766764, 0.01659569, 0.00457891]) 
3574     >>> np.exp(-x) / x 
3575     array([0.36787944, 0.06766764, 0.01659569, 0.00457891]) 
3576  
3577     For n equal to 1 it reduces to `exp1`. 
3578  
3579     >>> sc.expn(1, x) 
3580     array([0.21938393, 0.04890051, 0.01304838, 0.00377935]) 
3581     >>> sc.exp1(x) 
3582     array([0.21938393, 0.04890051, 0.01304838, 0.00377935]) 
3583  
3584     """)
3585 
3586 add_newdoc("fdtr",
3587     r""" 
3588     fdtr(dfn, dfd, x, out=None) 
3589  
3590     F cumulative distribution function. 
3591  
3592     Returns the value of the cumulative distribution function of the 
3593     F-distribution, also known as Snedecor's F-distribution or the 
3594     Fisher-Snedecor distribution. 
3595  
3596     The F-distribution with parameters :math:`d_n` and :math:`d_d` is the 
3597     distribution of the random variable, 
3598  
3599     .. math:: 
3600         X = \frac{U_n/d_n}{U_d/d_d}, 
3601  
3602     where :math:`U_n` and :math:`U_d` are random variables distributed 
3603     :math:`\chi^2`, with :math:`d_n` and :math:`d_d` degrees of freedom, 
3604     respectively. 
3605  
3606     Parameters 
3607     ---------- 
3608     dfn : array_like 
3609         First parameter (positive float). 
3610     dfd : array_like 
3611         Second parameter (positive float). 
3612     x : array_like 
3613         Argument (nonnegative float). 
3614     out : ndarray, optional 
3615         Optional output array for the function values 
3616  
3617     Returns 
3618     ------- 
3619     y : scalar or ndarray 
3620         The CDF of the F-distribution with parameters `dfn` and `dfd` at `x`. 
3621  
3622     See Also 
3623     -------- 
3624     fdtrc : F distribution survival function 
3625     fdtri : F distribution inverse cumulative distribution 
3626     scipy.stats.f : F distribution 
3627  
3628     Notes 
3629     ----- 
3630     The regularized incomplete beta function is used, according to the 
3631     formula, 
3632  
3633     .. math:: 
3634         F(d_n, d_d; x) = I_{xd_n/(d_d + xd_n)}(d_n/2, d_d/2). 
3635  
3636     Wrapper for the Cephes [1]_ routine `fdtr`. The F distribution is also 
3637     available as `scipy.stats.f`. Calling `fdtr` directly can improve 
3638     performance compared to the ``cdf`` method of `scipy.stats.f` (see last 
3639     example below). 
3640  
3641     References 
3642     ---------- 
3643     .. [1] Cephes Mathematical Functions Library, 
3644            http://www.netlib.org/cephes/ 
3645  
3646     Examples 
3647     -------- 
3648     Calculate the function for ``dfn=1`` and ``dfd=2`` at ``x=1``. 
3649  
3650     >>> import numpy as np 
3651     >>> from scipy.special import fdtr 
3652     >>> fdtr(1, 2, 1) 
3653     0.5773502691896258 
3654  
3655     Calculate the function at several points by providing a NumPy array for 
3656     `x`. 
3657  
3658     >>> x = np.array([0.5, 2., 3.]) 
3659     >>> fdtr(1, 2, x) 
3660     array([0.4472136 , 0.70710678, 0.77459667]) 
3661  
3662     Plot the function for several parameter sets. 
3663  
3664     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
3665     >>> dfn_parameters = [1, 5, 10, 50] 
3666     >>> dfd_parameters = [1, 1, 2, 3] 
3667     >>> linestyles = ['solid', 'dashed', 'dotted', 'dashdot'] 
3668     >>> parameters_list = list(zip(dfn_parameters, dfd_parameters, 
3669     ...                            linestyles)) 
3670     >>> x = np.linspace(0, 30, 1000) 
3671     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
3672     >>> for parameter_set in parameters_list: 
3673     ...     dfn, dfd, style = parameter_set 
3674     ...     fdtr_vals = fdtr(dfn, dfd, x) 
3675     ...     ax.plot(x, fdtr_vals, label=rf"$d_n={dfn},\, d_d={dfd}$", 
3676     ...             ls=style) 
3677     >>> ax.legend() 
3678     >>> ax.set_xlabel("$x$") 
3679     >>> ax.set_title("F distribution cumulative distribution function") 
3680     >>> plt.show() 
3681  
3682     The F distribution is also available as `scipy.stats.f`. Using `fdtr` 
3683     directly can be much faster than calling the ``cdf`` method of 
3684     `scipy.stats.f`, especially for small arrays or individual values. 
3685     To get the same results one must use the following parametrization: 
3686     ``stats.f(dfn, dfd).cdf(x)=fdtr(dfn, dfd, x)``. 
3687  
3688     >>> from scipy.stats import f 
3689     >>> dfn, dfd = 1, 2 
3690     >>> x = 1 
3691     >>> fdtr_res = fdtr(dfn, dfd, x)  # this will often be faster than below 
3692     >>> f_dist_res = f(dfn, dfd).cdf(x) 
3693     >>> fdtr_res == f_dist_res  # test that results are equal 
3694     True 
3695     """)
3696 
3697 add_newdoc("fdtrc",
3698     r""" 
3699     fdtrc(dfn, dfd, x, out=None) 
3700  
3701     F survival function. 
3702  
3703     Returns the complemented F-distribution function (the integral of the 
3704     density from `x` to infinity). 
3705  
3706     Parameters 
3707     ---------- 
3708     dfn : array_like 
3709         First parameter (positive float). 
3710     dfd : array_like 
3711         Second parameter (positive float). 
3712     x : array_like 
3713         Argument (nonnegative float). 
3714     out : ndarray, optional 
3715         Optional output array for the function values 
3716  
3717     Returns 
3718     ------- 
3719     y : scalar or ndarray 
3720         The complemented F-distribution function with parameters `dfn` and 
3721         `dfd` at `x`. 
3722  
3723     See Also 
3724     -------- 
3725     fdtr : F distribution cumulative distribution function 
3726     fdtri : F distribution inverse cumulative distribution function 
3727     scipy.stats.f : F distribution 
3728  
3729     Notes 
3730     ----- 
3731     The regularized incomplete beta function is used, according to the 
3732     formula, 
3733  
3734     .. math:: 
3735         F(d_n, d_d; x) = I_{d_d/(d_d + xd_n)}(d_d/2, d_n/2). 
3736  
3737     Wrapper for the Cephes [1]_ routine `fdtrc`. The F distribution is also 
3738     available as `scipy.stats.f`. Calling `fdtrc` directly can improve 
3739     performance compared to the ``sf`` method of `scipy.stats.f` (see last 
3740     example below). 
3741  
3742     References 
3743     ---------- 
3744     .. [1] Cephes Mathematical Functions Library, 
3745            http://www.netlib.org/cephes/ 
3746  
3747     Examples 
3748     -------- 
3749     Calculate the function for ``dfn=1`` and ``dfd=2`` at ``x=1``. 
3750  
3751     >>> import numpy as np 
3752     >>> from scipy.special import fdtrc 
3753     >>> fdtrc(1, 2, 1) 
3754     0.42264973081037427 
3755  
3756     Calculate the function at several points by providing a NumPy array for 
3757     `x`. 
3758  
3759     >>> x = np.array([0.5, 2., 3.]) 
3760     >>> fdtrc(1, 2, x) 
3761     array([0.5527864 , 0.29289322, 0.22540333]) 
3762  
3763     Plot the function for several parameter sets. 
3764  
3765     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
3766     >>> dfn_parameters = [1, 5, 10, 50] 
3767     >>> dfd_parameters = [1, 1, 2, 3] 
3768     >>> linestyles = ['solid', 'dashed', 'dotted', 'dashdot'] 
3769     >>> parameters_list = list(zip(dfn_parameters, dfd_parameters, 
3770     ...                            linestyles)) 
3771     >>> x = np.linspace(0, 30, 1000) 
3772     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
3773     >>> for parameter_set in parameters_list: 
3774     ...     dfn, dfd, style = parameter_set 
3775     ...     fdtrc_vals = fdtrc(dfn, dfd, x) 
3776     ...     ax.plot(x, fdtrc_vals, label=rf"$d_n={dfn},\, d_d={dfd}$", 
3777     ...             ls=style) 
3778     >>> ax.legend() 
3779     >>> ax.set_xlabel("$x$") 
3780     >>> ax.set_title("F distribution survival function") 
3781     >>> plt.show() 
3782  
3783     The F distribution is also available as `scipy.stats.f`. Using `fdtrc` 
3784     directly can be much faster than calling the ``sf`` method of 
3785     `scipy.stats.f`, especially for small arrays or individual values. 
3786     To get the same results one must use the following parametrization: 
3787     ``stats.f(dfn, dfd).sf(x)=fdtrc(dfn, dfd, x)``. 
3788  
3789     >>> from scipy.stats import f 
3790     >>> dfn, dfd = 1, 2 
3791     >>> x = 1 
3792     >>> fdtrc_res = fdtrc(dfn, dfd, x)  # this will often be faster than below 
3793     >>> f_dist_res = f(dfn, dfd).sf(x) 
3794     >>> f_dist_res == fdtrc_res  # test that results are equal 
3795     True 
3796     """)

3798 add_newdoc("fdtri",
3799     r""" 
3800     fdtri(dfn, dfd, p, out=None) 
3801  
3802     The `p`-th quantile of the F-distribution. 
3803  
3804     This function is the inverse of the F-distribution CDF, `fdtr`, returning 
3805     the `x` such that `fdtr(dfn, dfd, x) = p`. 
3806  
3807     Parameters 
3808     ---------- 
3809     dfn : array_like 
3810         First parameter (positive float). 
3811     dfd : array_like 
3812         Second parameter (positive float). 
3813     p : array_like 
3814         Cumulative probability, in [0, 1]. 
3815     out : ndarray, optional 
3816         Optional output array for the function values 
3817  
3818     Returns 
3819     ------- 
3820     x : scalar or ndarray 
3821         The quantile corresponding to `p`. 
3822  
3823     See Also 
3824     -------- 
3825     fdtr : F distribution cumulative distribution function 
3826     fdtrc : F distribution survival function 
3827     scipy.stats.f : F distribution 
3828  
3829     Notes 
3830     ----- 
3831     The computation is carried out using the relation to the inverse 
3832     regularized beta function, :math:`I^{-1}_x(a, b)`.  Let 
3833     :math:`z = I^{-1}_p(d_d/2, d_n/2).`  Then, 
3834  
3835     .. math:: 
3836         x = \frac{d_d (1 - z)}{d_n z}. 
3837  
3838     If `p` is such that :math:`x < 0.5`, the following relation is used 
3839     instead for improved stability: let 
3840     :math:`z' = I^{-1}_{1 - p}(d_n/2, d_d/2).` Then, 
3841  
3842     .. math:: 
3843         x = \frac{d_d z'}{d_n (1 - z')}. 
3844  
3845     Wrapper for the Cephes [1]_ routine `fdtri`. 
3846  
3847     The F distribution is also available as `scipy.stats.f`. Calling 
3848     `fdtri` directly can improve performance compared to the ``ppf`` 
3849     method of `scipy.stats.f` (see last example below). 
3850  
3851     References 
3852     ---------- 
3853     .. [1] Cephes Mathematical Functions Library, 
3854            http://www.netlib.org/cephes/ 
3855  
3856     Examples 
3857     -------- 
3858     `fdtri` represents the inverse of the F distribution CDF which is 
3859     available as `fdtr`. Here, we calculate the CDF for ``df1=1``, ``df2=2`` 
3860     at ``x=3``. `fdtri` then returns ``3`` given the same values for `df1`, 
3861     `df2` and the computed CDF value. 
3862  
3863     >>> import numpy as np 
3864     >>> from scipy.special import fdtri, fdtr 
3865     >>> df1, df2 = 1, 2 
3866     >>> x = 3 
3867     >>> cdf_value =  fdtr(df1, df2, x) 
3868     >>> fdtri(df1, df2, cdf_value) 
3869     3.000000000000006 
3870  
3871     Calculate the function at several points by providing a NumPy array for 
3872     `x`. 
3873  
3874     >>> x = np.array([0.1, 0.4, 0.7]) 
3875     >>> fdtri(1, 2, x) 
3876     array([0.02020202, 0.38095238, 1.92156863]) 
3877  
3878     Plot the function for several parameter sets. 
3879  
3880     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
3881     >>> dfn_parameters = [50, 10, 1, 50] 
3882     >>> dfd_parameters = [0.5, 1, 1, 5] 
3883     >>> linestyles = ['solid', 'dashed', 'dotted', 'dashdot'] 
3884     >>> parameters_list = list(zip(dfn_parameters, dfd_parameters, 
3885     ...                            linestyles)) 
3886     >>> x = np.linspace(0, 1, 1000) 
3887     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
3888     >>> for parameter_set in parameters_list: 
3889     ...     dfn, dfd, style = parameter_set 
3890     ...     fdtri_vals = fdtri(dfn, dfd, x) 
3891     ...     ax.plot(x, fdtri_vals, label=rf"$d_n={dfn},\, d_d={dfd}$", 
3892     ...             ls=style) 
3893     >>> ax.legend() 
3894     >>> ax.set_xlabel("$x$") 
3895     >>> title = "F distribution inverse cumulative distribution function" 
3896     >>> ax.set_title(title) 
3897     >>> ax.set_ylim(0, 30) 
3898     >>> plt.show() 
3899  
3900     The F distribution is also available as `scipy.stats.f`. Using `fdtri` 
3901     directly can be much faster than calling the ``ppf`` method of 
3902     `scipy.stats.f`, especially for small arrays or individual values. 
3903     To get the same results one must use the following parametrization: 
3904     ``stats.f(dfn, dfd).ppf(x)=fdtri(dfn, dfd, x)``. 
3905  
3906     >>> from scipy.stats import f 
3907     >>> dfn, dfd = 1, 2 
3908     >>> x = 0.7 
3909     >>> fdtri_res = fdtri(dfn, dfd, x)  # this will often be faster than below 
3910     >>> f_dist_res = f(dfn, dfd).ppf(x) 
3911     >>> f_dist_res == fdtri_res  # test that results are equal 
3912     True 
3913     """)
3914

4001 add_newdoc("gdtr",
4002     r""" 
4003     gdtr(a, b, x, out=None) 
4004  
4005     Gamma distribution cumulative distribution function. 
4006  
4007     Returns the integral from zero to `x` of the gamma probability density 
4008     function, 
4009  
4010     .. math:: 
4011  
4012         F = \int_0^x \frac{a^b}{\Gamma(b)} t^{b-1} e^{-at}\,dt, 
4013  
4014     where :math:`\Gamma` is the gamma function. 
4015  
4016     Parameters 
4017     ---------- 
4018     a : array_like 
4019         The rate parameter of the gamma distribution, sometimes denoted 
4020         :math:`\beta` (float).  It is also the reciprocal of the scale 
4021         parameter :math:`\theta`. 
4022     b : array_like 
4023         The shape parameter of the gamma distribution, sometimes denoted 
4024         :math:`\alpha` (float). 
4025     x : array_like 
4026         The quantile (upper limit of integration; float). 
4027     out : ndarray, optional 
4028         Optional output array for the function values 
4029  
4030     Returns 
4031     ------- 
4032     F : scalar or ndarray 
4033         The CDF of the gamma distribution with parameters `a` and `b` 
4034         evaluated at `x`. 
4035  
4036     See Also 
4037     -------- 
4038     gdtrc : 1 - CDF of the gamma distribution. 
4039     scipy.stats.gamma: Gamma distribution 
4040  
4041     Notes 
4042     ----- 
4043     The evaluation is carried out using the relation to the incomplete gamma 
4044     integral (regularized gamma function). 
4045  
4046     Wrapper for the Cephes [1]_ routine `gdtr`. Calling `gdtr` directly can 
4047     improve performance compared to the ``cdf`` method of `scipy.stats.gamma` 
4048     (see last example below). 
4049  
4050     References 
4051     ---------- 
4052     .. [1] Cephes Mathematical Functions Library, 
4053            http://www.netlib.org/cephes/ 
4054  
4055     Examples 
4056     -------- 
4057     Compute the function for ``a=1``, ``b=2`` at ``x=5``. 
4058  
4059     >>> import numpy as np 
4060     >>> from scipy.special import gdtr 
4061     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
4062     >>> gdtr(1., 2., 5.) 
4063     0.9595723180054873 
4064  
4065     Compute the function for ``a=1`` and ``b=2`` at several points by 
4066     providing a NumPy array for `x`. 
4067  
4068     >>> xvalues = np.array([1., 2., 3., 4]) 
4069     >>> gdtr(1., 1., xvalues) 
4070     array([0.63212056, 0.86466472, 0.95021293, 0.98168436]) 
4071  
4072     `gdtr` can evaluate different parameter sets by providing arrays with 
4073     broadcasting compatible shapes for `a`, `b` and `x`. Here we compute the 
4074     function for three different `a` at four positions `x` and ``b=3``, 
4075     resulting in a 3x4 array. 
4076  
4077     >>> a = np.array([[0.5], [1.5], [2.5]]) 
4078     >>> x = np.array([1., 2., 3., 4]) 
4079     >>> a.shape, x.shape 
4080     ((3, 1), (4,)) 
4081  
4082     >>> gdtr(a, 3., x) 
4083     array([[0.01438768, 0.0803014 , 0.19115317, 0.32332358], 
4084            [0.19115317, 0.57680992, 0.82642193, 0.9380312 ], 
4085            [0.45618688, 0.87534798, 0.97974328, 0.9972306 ]]) 
4086  
4087     Plot the function for four different parameter sets. 
4088  
4089     >>> a_parameters = [0.3, 1, 2, 6] 
4090     >>> b_parameters = [2, 10, 15, 20] 
4091     >>> linestyles = ['solid', 'dashed', 'dotted', 'dashdot'] 
4092     >>> parameters_list = list(zip(a_parameters, b_parameters, linestyles)) 
4093     >>> x = np.linspace(0, 30, 1000) 
4094     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
4095     >>> for parameter_set in parameters_list: 
4096     ...     a, b, style = parameter_set 
4097     ...     gdtr_vals = gdtr(a, b, x) 
4098     ...     ax.plot(x, gdtr_vals, label=fr"$a= {a},\, b={b}$", ls=style) 
4099     >>> ax.legend() 
4100     >>> ax.set_xlabel("$x$") 
4101     >>> ax.set_title("Gamma distribution cumulative distribution function") 
4102     >>> plt.show() 
4103  
4104     The gamma distribution is also available as `scipy.stats.gamma`. Using 
4105     `gdtr` directly can be much faster than calling the ``cdf`` method of 
4106     `scipy.stats.gamma`, especially for small arrays or individual values. 
4107     To get the same results one must use the following parametrization: 
4108     ``stats.gamma(b, scale=1/a).cdf(x)=gdtr(a, b, x)``. 
4109  
4110     >>> from scipy.stats import gamma 
4111     >>> a = 2. 
4112     >>> b = 3 
4113     >>> x = 1. 
4114     >>> gdtr_result = gdtr(a, b, x)  # this will often be faster than below 
4115     >>> gamma_dist_result = gamma(b, scale=1/a).cdf(x) 
4116     >>> gdtr_result == gamma_dist_result  # test that results are equal 
4117     True 
4118     """)
4119 
4120 add_newdoc("gdtrc",
4121     r""" 
4122     gdtrc(a, b, x, out=None) 
4123  
4124     Gamma distribution survival function. 
4125  
4126     Integral from `x` to infinity of the gamma probability density function, 
4127  
4128     .. math:: 
4129  
4130         F = \int_x^\infty \frac{a^b}{\Gamma(b)} t^{b-1} e^{-at}\,dt, 
4131  
4132     where :math:`\Gamma` is the gamma function. 
4133  
4134     Parameters 
4135     ---------- 
4136     a : array_like 
4137         The rate parameter of the gamma distribution, sometimes denoted 
4138         :math:`\beta` (float). It is also the reciprocal of the scale 
4139         parameter :math:`\theta`. 
4140     b : array_like 
4141         The shape parameter of the gamma distribution, sometimes denoted 
4142         :math:`\alpha` (float). 
4143     x : array_like 
4144         The quantile (lower limit of integration; float). 
4145     out : ndarray, optional 
4146         Optional output array for the function values 
4147  
4148     Returns 
4149     ------- 
4150     F : scalar or ndarray 
4151         The survival function of the gamma distribution with parameters `a` 
4152         and `b` evaluated at `x`. 
4153  
4154     See Also 
4155     -------- 
4156     gdtr: Gamma distribution cumulative distribution function 
4157     scipy.stats.gamma: Gamma distribution 
4158     gdtrix 
4159  
4160     Notes 
4161     ----- 
4162     The evaluation is carried out using the relation to the incomplete gamma 
4163     integral (regularized gamma function). 
4164  
4165     Wrapper for the Cephes [1]_ routine `gdtrc`. Calling `gdtrc` directly can 
4166     improve performance compared to the ``sf`` method of `scipy.stats.gamma` 
4167     (see last example below). 
4168  
4169     References 
4170     ---------- 
4171     .. [1] Cephes Mathematical Functions Library, 
4172            http://www.netlib.org/cephes/ 
4173  
4174     Examples 
4175     -------- 
4176     Compute the function for ``a=1`` and ``b=2`` at ``x=5``. 
4177  
4178     >>> import numpy as np 
4179     >>> from scipy.special import gdtrc 
4180     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
4181     >>> gdtrc(1., 2., 5.) 
4182     0.04042768199451279 
4183  
4184     Compute the function for ``a=1``, ``b=2`` at several points by providing 
4185     a NumPy array for `x`. 
4186  
4187     >>> xvalues = np.array([1., 2., 3., 4]) 
4188     >>> gdtrc(1., 1., xvalues) 
4189     array([0.36787944, 0.13533528, 0.04978707, 0.01831564]) 
4190  
4191     `gdtrc` can evaluate different parameter sets by providing arrays with 
4192     broadcasting compatible shapes for `a`, `b` and `x`. Here we compute the 
4193     function for three different `a` at four positions `x` and ``b=3``, 
4194     resulting in a 3x4 array. 
4195  
4196     >>> a = np.array([[0.5], [1.5], [2.5]]) 
4197     >>> x = np.array([1., 2., 3., 4]) 
4198     >>> a.shape, x.shape 
4199     ((3, 1), (4,)) 
4200  
4201     >>> gdtrc(a, 3., x) 
4202     array([[0.98561232, 0.9196986 , 0.80884683, 0.67667642], 
4203            [0.80884683, 0.42319008, 0.17357807, 0.0619688 ], 
4204            [0.54381312, 0.12465202, 0.02025672, 0.0027694 ]]) 
4205  
4206     Plot the function for four different parameter sets. 
4207  
4208     >>> a_parameters = [0.3, 1, 2, 6] 
4209     >>> b_parameters = [2, 10, 15, 20] 
4210     >>> linestyles = ['solid', 'dashed', 'dotted', 'dashdot'] 
4211     >>> parameters_list = list(zip(a_parameters, b_parameters, linestyles)) 
4212     >>> x = np.linspace(0, 30, 1000) 
4213     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
4214     >>> for parameter_set in parameters_list: 
4215     ...     a, b, style = parameter_set 
4216     ...     gdtrc_vals = gdtrc(a, b, x) 
4217     ...     ax.plot(x, gdtrc_vals, label=fr"$a= {a},\, b={b}$", ls=style) 
4218     >>> ax.legend() 
4219     >>> ax.set_xlabel("$x$") 
4220     >>> ax.set_title("Gamma distribution survival function") 
4221     >>> plt.show() 
4222  
4223     The gamma distribution is also available as `scipy.stats.gamma`. 
4224     Using `gdtrc` directly can be much faster than calling the ``sf`` method 
4225     of `scipy.stats.gamma`, especially for small arrays or individual 
4226     values. To get the same results one must use the following parametrization: 
4227     ``stats.gamma(b, scale=1/a).sf(x)=gdtrc(a, b, x)``. 
4228  
4229     >>> from scipy.stats import gamma 
4230     >>> a = 2 
4231     >>> b = 3 
4232     >>> x = 1. 
4233     >>> gdtrc_result = gdtrc(a, b, x)  # this will often be faster than below 
4234     >>> gamma_dist_result = gamma(b, scale=1/a).sf(x) 
4235     >>> gdtrc_result == gamma_dist_result  # test that results are equal 
4236     True 
4237     """)
4238 
4239 add_newdoc("gdtria",
4240     """ 
4241     gdtria(p, b, x, out=None) 
4242  
4243     Inverse of `gdtr` vs a. 
4244  
4245     Returns the inverse with respect to the parameter `a` of ``p = 
4246     gdtr(a, b, x)``, the cumulative distribution function of the gamma 
4247     distribution. 
4248  
4249     Parameters 
4250     ---------- 
4251     p : array_like 
4252         Probability values. 
4253     b : array_like 
4254         `b` parameter values of `gdtr(a, b, x)`. `b` is the "shape" parameter 
4255         of the gamma distribution. 
4256     x : array_like 
4257         Nonnegative real values, from the domain of the gamma distribution. 
4258     out : ndarray, optional 
4259         If a fourth argument is given, it must be a numpy.ndarray whose size 
4260         matches the broadcast result of `a`, `b` and `x`.  `out` is then the 
4261         array returned by the function. 
4262  
4263     Returns 
4264     ------- 
4265     a : scalar or ndarray 
4266         Values of the `a` parameter such that ``p = gdtr(a, b, x)`.  ``1/a`` 
4267         is the "scale" parameter of the gamma distribution. 
4268  
4269     See Also 
4270     -------- 
4271     gdtr : CDF of the gamma distribution. 
4272     gdtrib : Inverse with respect to `b` of `gdtr(a, b, x)`. 
4273     gdtrix : Inverse with respect to `x` of `gdtr(a, b, x)`. 
4274  
4275     Notes 
4276     ----- 
4277     Wrapper for the CDFLIB [1]_ Fortran routine `cdfgam`. 
4278  
4279     The cumulative distribution function `p` is computed using a routine by 
4280     DiDinato and Morris [2]_. Computation of `a` involves a search for a value 
4281     that produces the desired value of `p`. The search relies on the 
4282     monotonicity of `p` with `a`. 
4283  
4284     References 
4285     ---------- 
4286     .. [1] Barry Brown, James Lovato, and Kathy Russell, 
4287            CDFLIB: Library of Fortran Routines for Cumulative Distribution 
4288            Functions, Inverses, and Other Parameters. 
4289     .. [2] DiDinato, A. R. and Morris, A. H., 
4290            Computation of the incomplete gamma function ratios and their 
4291            inverse.  ACM Trans. Math. Softw. 12 (1986), 377-393. 
4292  
4293     Examples 
4294     -------- 
4295     First evaluate `gdtr`. 
4296  
4297     >>> from scipy.special import gdtr, gdtria 
4298     >>> p = gdtr(1.2, 3.4, 5.6) 
4299     >>> print(p) 
4300     0.94378087442 
4301  
4302     Verify the inverse. 
4303  
4304     >>> gdtria(p, 3.4, 5.6) 
4305     1.2 
4306     """)
4307 
4308 add_newdoc("gdtrib",
4309     """ 
4310     gdtrib(a, p, x, out=None) 
4311  
4312     Inverse of `gdtr` vs b. 
4313  
4314     Returns the inverse with respect to the parameter `b` of ``p = 
4315     gdtr(a, b, x)``, the cumulative distribution function of the gamma 
4316     distribution. 
4317  
4318     Parameters 
4319     ---------- 
4320     a : array_like 
4321         `a` parameter values of ``gdtr(a, b, x)`. ``1/a`` is the "scale" 
4322         parameter of the gamma distribution. 
4323     p : array_like 
4324         Probability values. 
4325     x : array_like 
4326         Nonnegative real values, from the domain of the gamma distribution. 
4327     out : ndarray, optional 
4328         If a fourth argument is given, it must be a numpy.ndarray whose size 
4329         matches the broadcast result of `a`, `b` and `x`.  `out` is then the 
4330         array returned by the function. 
4331  
4332     Returns 
4333     ------- 
4334     b : scalar or ndarray 
4335         Values of the `b` parameter such that `p = gdtr(a, b, x)`.  `b` is 
4336         the "shape" parameter of the gamma distribution. 
4337  
4338     See Also 
4339     -------- 
4340     gdtr : CDF of the gamma distribution. 
4341     gdtria : Inverse with respect to `a` of `gdtr(a, b, x)`. 
4342     gdtrix : Inverse with respect to `x` of `gdtr(a, b, x)`. 
4343  
4344     Notes 
4345     ----- 
4346  
4347     The cumulative distribution function `p` is computed using the Cephes [1]_ 
4348     routines `igam` and `igamc`. Computation of `b` involves a search for a value 
4349     that produces the desired value of `p` using Chandrupatla's bracketing 
4350     root finding algorithm [2]_. 
4351  
4352     Note that there are some edge cases where `gdtrib` is extended by taking 
4353     limits where they are uniquely defined. In particular 
4354     ``x == 0`` with ``p > 0`` and ``p == 0`` with ``x > 0``. 
4355     For these edge cases, a numerical result will be returned for 
4356     ``gdtrib(a, p, x)`` even though ``gdtr(a, gdtrib(a, p, x), x)`` is 
4357     undefined. 
4358  
4359     References 
4360     ---------- 
4361     .. [1] Cephes Mathematical Functions Library, 
4362            http://www.netlib.org/cephes/ 
4363     .. [2] Chandrupatla, Tirupathi R. 
4364            "A new hybrid quadratic/bisection algorithm for finding the zero of a 
4365            nonlinear function without using derivatives". 
4366            Advances in Engineering Software, 28(3), 145-149. 
4367            https://doi.org/10.1016/s0965-9978(96)00051-8 
4368  
4369     Examples 
4370     -------- 
4371     First evaluate `gdtr`. 
4372  
4373     >>> from scipy.special import gdtr, gdtrib 
4374     >>> p = gdtr(1.2, 3.4, 5.6) 
4375     >>> print(p) 
4376     0.94378087442 
4377  
4378     Verify the inverse. 
4379  
4380     >>> gdtrib(1.2, p, 5.6) 
4381     3.3999999999999995 
4382     """)
4383

4386     gdtrix(a, b, p, out=None) 
4387  
4388     Inverse of `gdtr` vs x. 
4389  
4390     Returns the inverse with respect to the parameter `x` of ``p = 
4391     gdtr(a, b, x)``, the cumulative distribution function of the gamma 
4392     distribution. This is also known as the pth quantile of the 
4393     distribution. 
4394  
4395     Parameters

4398         `a` parameter values of ``gdtr(a, b, x)``. ``1/a`` is the "scale" 
4399         parameter of the gamma distribution. 
4400     b : array_like 
4401         `b` parameter values of ``gdtr(a, b, x)``. `b` is the "shape" parameter 
4402         of the gamma distribution. 
4403     p : array_like 
4404         Probability values. 
4405     out : ndarray, optional 
4406         If a fourth argument is given, it must be a numpy.ndarray whose size 
4407         matches the broadcast result of `a`, `b` and `x`. `out` is then the 
4408         array returned by the function. 
4409  
4410     Returns 
4411     ------- 
4412     x : scalar or ndarray 
4413         Values of the `x` parameter such that `p = gdtr(a, b, x)`. 
4414  
4415     See Also 
4416     -------- 
4417     gdtr : CDF of the gamma distribution. 
4418     gdtria : Inverse with respect to `a` of ``gdtr(a, b, x)``. 
4419     gdtrib : Inverse with respect to `b` of ``gdtr(a, b, x)``. 
4420  
4421     Notes 
4422     ----- 
4423     Wrapper for the CDFLIB [1]_ Fortran routine `cdfgam`. 
4424  
4425     The cumulative distribution function `p` is computed using a routine by 
4426     DiDinato and Morris [2]_. Computation of `x` involves a search for a value 
4427     that produces the desired value of `p`. The search relies on the 
4428     monotonicity of `p` with `x`. 
4429  
4430     References 
4431     ---------- 
4432     .. [1] Barry Brown, James Lovato, and Kathy Russell, 
4433            CDFLIB: Library of Fortran Routines for Cumulative Distribution 
4434            Functions, Inverses, and Other Parameters. 
4435     .. [2] DiDinato, A. R. and Morris, A. H., 
4436            Computation of the incomplete gamma function ratios and their 
4437            inverse.  ACM Trans. Math. Softw. 12 (1986), 377-393. 
4438  
4439     Examples 
4440     -------- 
4441     First evaluate `gdtr`. 
4442  
4443     >>> from scipy.special import gdtr, gdtrix 
4444     >>> p = gdtr(1.2, 3.4, 5.6)

4446     0.94378087442

4457  
4458     Hankel function of the first kind 
4459

4640 add_newdoc("huber",
4641     r""" 
4642     huber(delta, r, out=None) 
4643  
4644     Huber loss function. 
4645  
4646     .. math:: \text{huber}(\delta, r) = \begin{cases} \infty & \delta < 0  \\ 
4647               \frac{1}{2}r^2 & 0 \le \delta, | r | \le \delta \\ 
4648               \delta ( |r| - \frac{1}{2}\delta ) & \text{otherwise} \end{cases} 
4649  
4650     Parameters 
4651     ---------- 
4652     delta : ndarray 
4653         Input array, indicating the quadratic vs. linear loss changepoint. 
4654     r : ndarray 
4655         Input array, possibly representing residuals. 
4656     out : ndarray, optional 
4657         Optional output array for the function values 
4658  
4659     Returns 
4660     ------- 
4661     scalar or ndarray 
4662         The computed Huber loss function values. 
4663  
4664     See Also 
4665     -------- 
4666     pseudo_huber : smooth approximation of this function 
4667  
4668     Notes 
4669     ----- 
4670     `huber` is useful as a loss function in robust statistics or machine 
4671     learning to reduce the influence of outliers as compared to the common 
4672     squared error loss, residuals with a magnitude higher than `delta` are 
4673     not squared [1]_. 
4674  
4675     Typically, `r` represents residuals, the difference 
4676     between a model prediction and data. Then, for :math:`|r|\leq\delta`, 
4677     `huber` resembles the squared error and for :math:`|r|>\delta` the 
4678     absolute error. This way, the Huber loss often achieves 
4679     a fast convergence in model fitting for small residuals like the squared 
4680     error loss function and still reduces the influence of outliers 
4681     (:math:`|r|>\delta`) like the absolute error loss. As :math:`\delta` is 
4682     the cutoff between squared and absolute error regimes, it has 
4683     to be tuned carefully for each problem. `huber` is also 
4684     convex, making it suitable for gradient based optimization. 
4685  
4686     .. versionadded:: 0.15.0 
4687  
4688     References 
4689     ---------- 
4690     .. [1] Peter Huber. "Robust Estimation of a Location Parameter", 
4691            1964. Annals of Statistics. 53 (1): 73 - 101. 
4692  
4693     Examples 
4694     -------- 
4695     Import all necessary modules. 
4696  
4697     >>> import numpy as np 
4698     >>> from scipy.special import huber 
4699     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
4700  
4701     Compute the function for ``delta=1`` at ``r=2`` 
4702  
4703     >>> huber(1., 2.) 
4704     1.5 
4705  
4706     Compute the function for different `delta` by providing a NumPy array or 
4707     list for `delta`. 
4708  
4709     >>> huber([1., 3., 5.], 4.) 
4710     array([3.5, 7.5, 8. ]) 
4711  
4712     Compute the function at different points by providing a NumPy array or 
4713     list for `r`. 
4714  
4715     >>> huber(2., np.array([1., 1.5, 3.])) 
4716     array([0.5  , 1.125, 4.   ]) 
4717  
4718     The function can be calculated for different `delta` and `r` by 
4719     providing arrays for both with compatible shapes for broadcasting. 
4720  
4721     >>> r = np.array([1., 2.5, 8., 10.]) 
4722     >>> deltas = np.array([[1.], [5.], [9.]]) 
4723     >>> print(r.shape, deltas.shape) 
4724     (4,) (3, 1) 
4725  
4726     >>> huber(deltas, r) 
4727     array([[ 0.5  ,  2.   ,  7.5  ,  9.5  ], 
4728            [ 0.5  ,  3.125, 27.5  , 37.5  ], 
4729            [ 0.5  ,  3.125, 32.   , 49.5  ]]) 
4730  
4731     Plot the function for different `delta`. 
4732  
4733     >>> x = np.linspace(-4, 4, 500) 
4734     >>> deltas = [1, 2, 3] 
4735     >>> linestyles = ["dashed", "dotted", "dashdot"] 
4736     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
4737     >>> combined_plot_parameters = list(zip(deltas, linestyles)) 
4738     >>> for delta, style in combined_plot_parameters: 
4739     ...     ax.plot(x, huber(delta, x), label=fr"$\delta={delta}$", ls=style) 
4740     >>> ax.legend(loc="upper center") 
4741     >>> ax.set_xlabel("$x$") 
4742     >>> ax.set_title(r"Huber loss function $h_{\delta}(x)$") 
4743     >>> ax.set_xlim(-4, 4) 
4744     >>> ax.set_ylim(0, 8) 
4745     >>> plt.show() 
4746     """)

4748 add_newdoc("hyp0f1",
4749     r""" 
4750     hyp0f1(v, z, out=None) 
4751  
4752     Confluent hypergeometric limit function 0F1. 
4753  
4754     Parameters 
4755     ---------- 
4756     v : array_like 
4757         Real-valued parameter 
4758     z : array_like 
4759         Real- or complex-valued argument 
4760     out : ndarray, optional 
4761         Optional output array for the function results 
4762  
4763     Returns 
4764     ------- 
4765     scalar or ndarray 
4766         The confluent hypergeometric limit function 
4767  
4768     Notes 
4769     ----- 
4770     This function is defined as: 
4771  
4772     .. math:: _0F_1(v, z) = \sum_{k=0}^{\infty}\frac{z^k}{(v)_k k!}. 
4773  
4774     It's also the limit as :math:`q \to \infty` of :math:`_1F_1(q; v; z/q)`, 
4775     and satisfies the differential equation :math:`f''(z) + vf'(z) = 
4776     f(z)`. See [1]_ for more information. 
4777  
4778     References 
4779     ---------- 
4780     .. [1] Wolfram MathWorld, "Confluent Hypergeometric Limit Function", 
4781            http://mathworld.wolfram.com/ConfluentHypergeometricLimitFunction.html 
4782  
4783     Examples 
4784     -------- 
4785     >>> import numpy as np 
4786     >>> import scipy.special as sc 
4787  
4788     It is one when `z` is zero. 
4789  
4790     >>> sc.hyp0f1(1, 0) 
4791     1.0 
4792  
4793     It is the limit of the confluent hypergeometric function as `q` 
4794     goes to infinity. 
4795  
4796     >>> q = np.array([1, 10, 100, 1000]) 
4797     >>> v = 1 
4798     >>> z = 1 
4799     >>> sc.hyp1f1(q, v, z / q) 
4800     array([2.71828183, 2.31481985, 2.28303778, 2.27992985]) 
4801     >>> sc.hyp0f1(v, z) 
4802     2.2795853023360673 
4803  
4804     It is related to Bessel functions. 
4805  
4806     >>> n = 1 
4807     >>> x = np.linspace(0, 1, 5) 
4808     >>> sc.jv(n, x) 
4809     array([0.        , 0.12402598, 0.24226846, 0.3492436 , 0.44005059]) 
4810     >>> (0.5 * x)**n / sc.factorial(n) * sc.hyp0f1(n + 1, -0.25 * x**2) 
4811     array([0.        , 0.12402598, 0.24226846, 0.3492436 , 0.44005059]) 
4812  
4813     """)
4814 
4815 add_newdoc("hyp1f1",
4816     r""" 
4817     hyp1f1(a, b, x, out=None) 
4818  
4819     Confluent hypergeometric function 1F1. 
4820  
4821     The confluent hypergeometric function is defined by the series 
4822  
4823     .. math:: 
4824  
4825        {}_1F_1(a; b; x) = \sum_{k = 0}^\infty \frac{(a)_k}{(b)_k k!} x^k. 
4826  
4827     See [dlmf]_ for more details. Here :math:`(\cdot)_k` is the 
4828     Pochhammer symbol; see `poch`. 
4829  
4830     Parameters 
4831     ---------- 
4832     a, b : array_like 
4833         Real parameters 
4834     x : array_like 
4835         Real or complex argument 
4836     out : ndarray, optional 
4837         Optional output array for the function results 
4838  
4839     Returns 
4840     ------- 
4841     scalar or ndarray 
4842         Values of the confluent hypergeometric function 
4843  
4844     See Also 
4845     -------- 
4846     hyperu : another confluent hypergeometric function 
4847     hyp0f1 : confluent hypergeometric limit function 
4848     hyp2f1 : Gaussian hypergeometric function 
4849  
4850     Notes 
4851     ----- 
4852     For real values, this function uses the ``hyp1f1`` routine from the C++ Boost 
4853     library [2]_, for complex values a C translation of the specfun 
4854     Fortran library [3]_. 
4855  
4856     References 
4857     ---------- 
4858     .. [dlmf] NIST Digital Library of Mathematical Functions 
4859               https://dlmf.nist.gov/13.2#E2 
4860     .. [2] The Boost Developers. "Boost C++ Libraries". https://www.boost.org/. 
4861     .. [3] Zhang, Jin, "Computation of Special Functions", John Wiley 
4862            and Sons, Inc, 1996. 
4863  
4864     Examples 
4865     -------- 
4866     >>> import numpy as np 
4867     >>> import scipy.special as sc 
4868  
4869     It is one when `x` is zero: 
4870  
4871     >>> sc.hyp1f1(0.5, 0.5, 0) 
4872     1.0 
4873  
4874     It is singular when `b` is a nonpositive integer. 
4875  
4876     >>> sc.hyp1f1(0.5, -1, 0) 
4877     inf 
4878  
4879     It is a polynomial when `a` is a nonpositive integer. 
4880  
4881     >>> a, b, x = -1, 0.5, np.array([1.0, 2.0, 3.0, 4.0]) 
4882     >>> sc.hyp1f1(a, b, x) 
4883     array([-1., -3., -5., -7.]) 
4884     >>> 1 + (a / b) * x 
4885     array([-1., -3., -5., -7.]) 
4886  
4887     It reduces to the exponential function when ``a = b``. 
4888  
4889     >>> sc.hyp1f1(2, 2, [1, 2, 3, 4]) 
4890     array([ 2.71828183,  7.3890561 , 20.08553692, 54.59815003]) 
4891     >>> np.exp([1, 2, 3, 4]) 
4892     array([ 2.71828183,  7.3890561 , 20.08553692, 54.59815003]) 
4893  
4894     """)
4895 
4896 add_newdoc("hyperu",
4897     r""" 
4898     hyperu(a, b, x, out=None) 
4899  
4900     Confluent hypergeometric function U 
4901  
4902     It is defined as the solution to the equation 
4903  
4904     .. math:: 
4905  
4906        x \frac{d^2w}{dx^2} + (b - x) \frac{dw}{dx} - aw = 0 
4907  
4908     which satisfies the property 
4909  
4910     .. math:: 
4911  
4912        U(a, b, x) \sim x^{-a} 
4913  
4914     as :math:`x \to \infty`. See [dlmf]_ for more details. 
4915  
4916     Parameters 
4917     ---------- 
4918     a, b : array_like 
4919         Real-valued parameters 
4920     x : array_like 
4921         Real-valued argument 
4922     out : ndarray, optional 
4923         Optional output array for the function values 
4924  
4925     Returns 
4926     ------- 
4927     scalar or ndarray 
4928         Values of `U` 
4929  
4930     References 
4931     ---------- 
4932     .. [dlmf] NIST Digital Library of Mathematics Functions 
4933               https://dlmf.nist.gov/13.2#E6 
4934  
4935     Examples 
4936     -------- 
4937     >>> import numpy as np 
4938     >>> import scipy.special as sc 
4939  
4940     It has a branch cut along the negative `x` axis. 
4941  
4942     >>> x = np.linspace(-0.1, -10, 5) 
4943     >>> sc.hyperu(1, 1, x) 
4944     array([nan, nan, nan, nan, nan]) 
4945  
4946     It approaches zero as `x` goes to infinity. 
4947  
4948     >>> x = np.array([1, 10, 100]) 
4949     >>> sc.hyperu(1, 1, x) 
4950     array([0.59634736, 0.09156333, 0.00990194]) 
4951  
4952     It satisfies Kummer's transformation. 
4953  
4954     >>> a, b, x = 2, 1, 1 
4955     >>> sc.hyperu(a, b, x) 
4956     0.1926947246463881 
4957     >>> x**(1 - b) * sc.hyperu(a - b + 1, 2 - b, x) 
4958     0.1926947246463881 
4959  
4960     """)
4961

4964     Internal function, do not use.

4967 add_newdoc("iv",
4968     r""" 
4969     iv(v, z, out=None) 
4970  
4971     Modified Bessel function of the first kind of real order. 
4972  
4973     Parameters 
4974     ---------- 
4975     v : array_like 
4976         Order. If `z` is of real type and negative, `v` must be integer 
4977         valued. 
4978     z : array_like of float or complex 
4979         Argument. 
4980     out : ndarray, optional 
4981         Optional output array for the function values 
4982  
4983     Returns 
4984     ------- 
4985     scalar or ndarray 
4986         Values of the modified Bessel function. 
4987  
4988     See Also 
4989     -------- 
4990     ive : This function with leading exponential behavior stripped off. 
4991     i0 : Faster version of this function for order 0. 
4992     i1 : Faster version of this function for order 1. 
4993  
4994     Notes 
4995     ----- 
4996     For real `z` and :math:`v \in [-50, 50]`, the evaluation is carried out 
4997     using Temme's method [1]_.  For larger orders, uniform asymptotic 
4998     expansions are applied. 
4999  
5000     For complex `z` and positive `v`, the AMOS [2]_ `zbesi` routine is 
5001     called. It uses a power series for small `z`, the asymptotic expansion 
5002     for large `abs(z)`, the Miller algorithm normalized by the Wronskian 
5003     and a Neumann series for intermediate magnitudes, and the uniform 
5004     asymptotic expansions for :math:`I_v(z)` and :math:`J_v(z)` for large 
5005     orders. Backward recurrence is used to generate sequences or reduce 
5006     orders when necessary. 
5007  
5008     The calculations above are done in the right half plane and continued 
5009     into the left half plane by the formula, 
5010  
5011     .. math:: I_v(z \exp(\pm\imath\pi)) = \exp(\pm\pi v) I_v(z) 
5012  
5013     (valid when the real part of `z` is positive).  For negative `v`, the 
5014     formula 
5015  
5016     .. math:: I_{-v}(z) = I_v(z) + \frac{2}{\pi} \sin(\pi v) K_v(z) 
5017  
5018     is used, where :math:`K_v(z)` is the modified Bessel function of the 
5019     second kind, evaluated using the AMOS routine `zbesk`. 
5020  
5021     References 
5022     ---------- 
5023     .. [1] Temme, Journal of Computational Physics, vol 21, 343 (1976) 
5024     .. [2] Donald E. Amos, "AMOS, A Portable Package for Bessel Functions 
5025            of a Complex Argument and Nonnegative Order", 
5026            http://netlib.org/amos/ 
5027  
5028     Examples 
5029     -------- 
5030     Evaluate the function of order 0 at one point. 
5031  
5032     >>> from scipy.special import iv 
5033     >>> iv(0, 1.) 
5034     1.2660658777520084 
5035  
5036     Evaluate the function at one point for different orders. 
5037  
5038     >>> iv(0, 1.), iv(1, 1.), iv(1.5, 1.) 
5039     (1.2660658777520084, 0.565159103992485, 0.2935253263474798) 
5040  
5041     The evaluation for different orders can be carried out in one call by 
5042     providing a list or NumPy array as argument for the `v` parameter: 
5043  
5044     >>> iv([0, 1, 1.5], 1.) 
5045     array([1.26606588, 0.5651591 , 0.29352533]) 
5046  
5047     Evaluate the function at several points for order 0 by providing an 
5048     array for `z`. 
5049  
5050     >>> import numpy as np 
5051     >>> points = np.array([-2., 0., 3.]) 
5052     >>> iv(0, points) 
5053     array([2.2795853 , 1.        , 4.88079259]) 
5054  
5055     If `z` is an array, the order parameter `v` must be broadcastable to 
5056     the correct shape if different orders shall be computed in one call. 
5057     To calculate the orders 0 and 1 for an 1D array: 
5058  
5059     >>> orders = np.array([[0], [1]]) 
5060     >>> orders.shape 
5061     (2, 1) 
5062  
5063     >>> iv(orders, points) 
5064     array([[ 2.2795853 ,  1.        ,  4.88079259], 
5065            [-1.59063685,  0.        ,  3.95337022]]) 
5066  
5067     Plot the functions of order 0 to 3 from -5 to 5. 
5068  
5069     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
5070     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
5071     >>> x = np.linspace(-5., 5., 1000) 
5072     >>> for i in range(4): 
5073     ...     ax.plot(x, iv(i, x), label=f'$I_{i!r}$') 
5074     >>> ax.legend() 
5075     >>> plt.show() 
5076  
5077     """)
5078 
5079 add_newdoc("ive",
5080     r""" 
5081     ive(v, z, out=None) 
5082  
5083     Exponentially scaled modified Bessel function of the first kind. 
5084  
5085     Defined as:: 
5086  
5087         ive(v, z) = iv(v, z) * exp(-abs(z.real)) 
5088  
5089     For imaginary numbers without a real part, returns the unscaled 
5090     Bessel function of the first kind `iv`. 
5091  
5092     Parameters 
5093     ---------- 
5094     v : array_like of float 
5095         Order. 
5096     z : array_like of float or complex 
5097         Argument. 
5098     out : ndarray, optional 
5099         Optional output array for the function values 
5100  
5101     Returns 
5102     ------- 
5103     scalar or ndarray 
5104         Values of the exponentially scaled modified Bessel function. 
5105  
5106     See Also 
5107     -------- 
5108     iv: Modified Bessel function of the first kind 
5109     i0e: Faster implementation of this function for order 0 
5110     i1e: Faster implementation of this function for order 1 
5111  
5112     Notes 
5113     ----- 
5114     For positive `v`, the AMOS [1]_ `zbesi` routine is called. It uses a 
5115     power series for small `z`, the asymptotic expansion for large 
5116     `abs(z)`, the Miller algorithm normalized by the Wronskian and a 
5117     Neumann series for intermediate magnitudes, and the uniform asymptotic 
5118     expansions for :math:`I_v(z)` and :math:`J_v(z)` for large orders. 
5119     Backward recurrence is used to generate sequences or reduce orders when 
5120     necessary. 
5121  
5122     The calculations above are done in the right half plane and continued 
5123     into the left half plane by the formula, 
5124  
5125     .. math:: I_v(z \exp(\pm\imath\pi)) = \exp(\pm\pi v) I_v(z) 
5126  
5127     (valid when the real part of `z` is positive).  For negative `v`, the 
5128     formula 
5129  
5130     .. math:: I_{-v}(z) = I_v(z) + \frac{2}{\pi} \sin(\pi v) K_v(z) 
5131  
5132     is used, where :math:`K_v(z)` is the modified Bessel function of the 
5133     second kind, evaluated using the AMOS routine `zbesk`. 
5134  
5135     `ive` is useful for large arguments `z`: for these, `iv` easily overflows, 
5136     while `ive` does not due to the exponential scaling. 
5137  
5138     References 
5139     ---------- 
5140     .. [1] Donald E. Amos, "AMOS, A Portable Package for Bessel Functions 
5141            of a Complex Argument and Nonnegative Order", 
5142            http://netlib.org/amos/ 
5143  
5144     Examples 
5145     -------- 
5146     In the following example `iv` returns infinity whereas `ive` still returns 
5147     a finite number. 
5148  
5149     >>> from scipy.special import iv, ive 
5150     >>> import numpy as np 
5151     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
5152     >>> iv(3, 1000.), ive(3, 1000.) 
5153     (inf, 0.01256056218254712) 
5154  
5155     Evaluate the function at one point for different orders by 
5156     providing a list or NumPy array as argument for the `v` parameter: 
5157  
5158     >>> ive([0, 1, 1.5], 1.) 
5159     array([0.46575961, 0.20791042, 0.10798193]) 
5160  
5161     Evaluate the function at several points for order 0 by providing an 
5162     array for `z`. 
5163  
5164     >>> points = np.array([-2., 0., 3.]) 
5165     >>> ive(0, points) 
5166     array([0.30850832, 1.        , 0.24300035]) 
5167  
5168     Evaluate the function at several points for different orders by 
5169     providing arrays for both `v` for `z`. Both arrays have to be 
5170     broadcastable to the correct shape. To calculate the orders 0, 1 
5171     and 2 for a 1D array of points: 
5172  
5173     >>> ive([[0], [1], [2]], points) 
5174     array([[ 0.30850832,  1.        ,  0.24300035], 
5175            [-0.21526929,  0.        ,  0.19682671], 
5176            [ 0.09323903,  0.        ,  0.11178255]]) 
5177  
5178     Plot the functions of order 0 to 3 from -5 to 5. 
5179  
5180     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
5181     >>> x = np.linspace(-5., 5., 1000) 
5182     >>> for i in range(4): 
5183     ...     ax.plot(x, ive(i, x), label=fr'$I_{i!r}(z)\cdot e^{{-|z|}}$') 
5184     >>> ax.legend() 
5185     >>> ax.set_xlabel(r"$z$") 
5186     >>> plt.show() 
5187     """)
5188

5222 add_newdoc("jv",
5223     r""" 
5224     jv(v, z, out=None) 
5225  
5226     Bessel function of the first kind of real order and complex argument. 
5227  
5228     Parameters 
5229     ---------- 
5230     v : array_like 
5231         Order (float). 
5232     z : array_like 
5233         Argument (float or complex). 
5234     out : ndarray, optional 
5235         Optional output array for the function values 
5236  
5237     Returns 
5238     ------- 
5239     J : scalar or ndarray 
5240         Value of the Bessel function, :math:`J_v(z)`. 
5241  
5242     See Also 
5243     -------- 
5244     jve : :math:`J_v` with leading exponential behavior stripped off. 
5245     spherical_jn : spherical Bessel functions. 
5246     j0 : faster version of this function for order 0. 
5247     j1 : faster version of this function for order 1. 
5248  
5249     Notes 
5250     ----- 
5251     For positive `v` values, the computation is carried out using the AMOS 
5252     [1]_ `zbesj` routine, which exploits the connection to the modified 
5253     Bessel function :math:`I_v`, 
5254  
5255     .. math:: 
5256         J_v(z) = \exp(v\pi\imath/2) I_v(-\imath z)\qquad (\Im z > 0) 
5257  
5258         J_v(z) = \exp(-v\pi\imath/2) I_v(\imath z)\qquad (\Im z < 0) 
5259  
5260     For negative `v` values the formula, 
5261  
5262     .. math:: J_{-v}(z) = J_v(z) \cos(\pi v) - Y_v(z) \sin(\pi v) 
5263  
5264     is used, where :math:`Y_v(z)` is the Bessel function of the second 
5265     kind, computed using the AMOS routine `zbesy`.  Note that the second 
5266     term is exactly zero for integer `v`; to improve accuracy the second 
5267     term is explicitly omitted for `v` values such that `v = floor(v)`. 
5268  
5269     Not to be confused with the spherical Bessel functions (see `spherical_jn`). 
5270  
5271     References 
5272     ---------- 
5273     .. [1] Donald E. Amos, "AMOS, A Portable Package for Bessel Functions 
5274            of a Complex Argument and Nonnegative Order", 
5275            http://netlib.org/amos/ 
5276  
5277     Examples 
5278     -------- 
5279     Evaluate the function of order 0 at one point. 
5280  
5281     >>> from scipy.special import jv 
5282     >>> jv(0, 1.) 
5283     0.7651976865579666 
5284  
5285     Evaluate the function at one point for different orders. 
5286  
5287     >>> jv(0, 1.), jv(1, 1.), jv(1.5, 1.) 
5288     (0.7651976865579666, 0.44005058574493355, 0.24029783912342725) 
5289  
5290     The evaluation for different orders can be carried out in one call by 
5291     providing a list or NumPy array as argument for the `v` parameter: 
5292  
5293     >>> jv([0, 1, 1.5], 1.) 
5294     array([0.76519769, 0.44005059, 0.24029784]) 
5295  
5296     Evaluate the function at several points for order 0 by providing an 
5297     array for `z`. 
5298  
5299     >>> import numpy as np 
5300     >>> points = np.array([-2., 0., 3.]) 
5301     >>> jv(0, points) 
5302     array([ 0.22389078,  1.        , -0.26005195]) 
5303  
5304     If `z` is an array, the order parameter `v` must be broadcastable to 
5305     the correct shape if different orders shall be computed in one call. 
5306     To calculate the orders 0 and 1 for an 1D array: 
5307  
5308     >>> orders = np.array([[0], [1]]) 
5309     >>> orders.shape 
5310     (2, 1) 
5311  
5312     >>> jv(orders, points) 
5313     array([[ 0.22389078,  1.        , -0.26005195], 
5314            [-0.57672481,  0.        ,  0.33905896]]) 
5315  
5316     Plot the functions of order 0 to 3 from -10 to 10. 
5317  
5318     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
5319     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
5320     >>> x = np.linspace(-10., 10., 1000) 
5321     >>> for i in range(4): 
5322     ...     ax.plot(x, jv(i, x), label=f'$J_{i!r}$') 
5323     >>> ax.legend() 
5324     >>> plt.show() 
5325  
5326     """)
5327 
5328 add_newdoc("jve",
5329     r""" 
5330     jve(v, z, out=None) 
5331  
5332     Exponentially scaled Bessel function of the first kind of order `v`. 
5333  
5334     Defined as:: 
5335  
5336         jve(v, z) = jv(v, z) * exp(-abs(z.imag)) 
5337  
5338     Parameters 
5339     ---------- 
5340     v : array_like 
5341         Order (float). 
5342     z : array_like 
5343         Argument (float or complex). 
5344     out : ndarray, optional 
5345         Optional output array for the function values 
5346  
5347     Returns 
5348     ------- 
5349     J : scalar or ndarray 
5350         Value of the exponentially scaled Bessel function. 
5351  
5352     See Also 
5353     -------- 
5354     jv: Unscaled Bessel function of the first kind 
5355  
5356     Notes 
5357     ----- 
5358     For positive `v` values, the computation is carried out using the AMOS 
5359     [1]_ `zbesj` routine, which exploits the connection to the modified 
5360     Bessel function :math:`I_v`, 
5361  
5362     .. math:: 
5363         J_v(z) = \exp(v\pi\imath/2) I_v(-\imath z)\qquad (\Im z > 0) 
5364  
5365         J_v(z) = \exp(-v\pi\imath/2) I_v(\imath z)\qquad (\Im z < 0) 
5366  
5367     For negative `v` values the formula, 
5368  
5369     .. math:: J_{-v}(z) = J_v(z) \cos(\pi v) - Y_v(z) \sin(\pi v) 
5370  
5371     is used, where :math:`Y_v(z)` is the Bessel function of the second 
5372     kind, computed using the AMOS routine `zbesy`.  Note that the second 
5373     term is exactly zero for integer `v`; to improve accuracy the second 
5374     term is explicitly omitted for `v` values such that `v = floor(v)`. 
5375  
5376     Exponentially scaled Bessel functions are useful for large arguments `z`: 
5377     for these, the unscaled Bessel functions can easily under-or overflow. 
5378  
5379     References 
5380     ---------- 
5381     .. [1] Donald E. Amos, "AMOS, A Portable Package for Bessel Functions 
5382            of a Complex Argument and Nonnegative Order", 
5383            http://netlib.org/amos/ 
5384  
5385     Examples 
5386     -------- 
5387     Compare the output of `jv` and `jve` for large complex arguments for `z` 
5388     by computing their values for order ``v=1`` at ``z=1000j``. We see that 
5389     `jv` overflows but `jve` returns a finite number: 
5390  
5391     >>> import numpy as np 
5392     >>> from scipy.special import jv, jve 
5393     >>> v = 1 
5394     >>> z = 1000j 
5395     >>> jv(v, z), jve(v, z) 
5396     ((inf+infj), (7.721967686709077e-19+0.012610930256928629j)) 
5397  
5398     For real arguments for `z`, `jve` returns the same as `jv`. 
5399  
5400     >>> v, z = 1, 1000 
5401     >>> jv(v, z), jve(v, z) 
5402     (0.004728311907089523, 0.004728311907089523) 
5403  
5404     The function can be evaluated for several orders at the same time by 
5405     providing a list or NumPy array for `v`: 
5406  
5407     >>> jve([1, 3, 5], 1j) 
5408     array([1.27304208e-17+2.07910415e-01j, -4.99352086e-19-8.15530777e-03j, 
5409            6.11480940e-21+9.98657141e-05j]) 
5410  
5411     In the same way, the function can be evaluated at several points in one 
5412     call by providing a list or NumPy array for `z`: 
5413  
5414     >>> jve(1, np.array([1j, 2j, 3j])) 
5415     array([1.27308412e-17+0.20791042j, 1.31814423e-17+0.21526929j, 
5416            1.20521602e-17+0.19682671j]) 
5417  
5418     It is also possible to evaluate several orders at several points 
5419     at the same time by providing arrays for `v` and `z` with 
5420     compatible shapes for broadcasting. Compute `jve` for two different orders 
5421     `v` and three points `z` resulting in a 2x3 array. 
5422  
5423     >>> v = np.array([[1], [3]]) 
5424     >>> z = np.array([1j, 2j, 3j]) 
5425     >>> v.shape, z.shape 
5426     ((2, 1), (3,)) 
5427  
5428     >>> jve(v, z) 
5429     array([[1.27304208e-17+0.20791042j,  1.31810070e-17+0.21526929j, 
5430             1.20517622e-17+0.19682671j], 
5431            [-4.99352086e-19-0.00815531j, -1.76289571e-18-0.02879122j, 
5432             -2.92578784e-18-0.04778332j]]) 
5433     """)
5434

5540 add_newdoc("kl_div",
5541     r""" 
5542     kl_div(x, y, out=None) 
5543  
5544     Elementwise function for computing Kullback-Leibler divergence. 
5545  
5546     .. math:: 
5547  
5548         \mathrm{kl\_div}(x, y) = 
5549           \begin{cases} 
5550             x \log(x / y) - x + y & x > 0, y > 0 \\ 
5551             y & x = 0, y \ge 0 \\ 
5552             \infty & \text{otherwise} 
5553           \end{cases} 
5554  
5555     Parameters 
5556     ---------- 
5557     x, y : array_like 
5558         Real arguments 
5559     out : ndarray, optional 
5560         Optional output array for the function results 
5561  
5562     Returns 
5563     ------- 
5564     scalar or ndarray 
5565         Values of the Kullback-Liebler divergence. 
5566  
5567     See Also 
5568     -------- 
5569     entr, rel_entr, scipy.stats.entropy 
5570  
5571     Notes 
5572     ----- 
5573     .. versionadded:: 0.15.0 
5574  
5575     This function is non-negative and is jointly convex in `x` and `y`. 
5576  
5577     The origin of this function is in convex programming; see [1]_ for 
5578     details. This is why the function contains the extra :math:`-x 
5579     + y` terms over what might be expected from the Kullback-Leibler 
5580     divergence. For a version of the function without the extra terms, 
5581     see `rel_entr`. 
5582  
5583     References 
5584     ---------- 
5585     .. [1] Boyd, Stephen and Lieven Vandenberghe. *Convex optimization*. 
5586            Cambridge University Press, 2004. 
5587            :doi:`https://doi.org/10.1017/CBO9780511804441` 
5588  
5589     """)
5590

5658 add_newdoc("kolmogi",
5659     """ 
5660     kolmogi(p, out=None) 
5661  
5662     Inverse Survival Function of Kolmogorov distribution 
5663  
5664     It is the inverse function to `kolmogorov`. 
5665     Returns y such that ``kolmogorov(y) == p``. 
5666  
5667     Parameters 
5668     ---------- 
5669     p : float array_like 
5670         Probability 
5671     out : ndarray, optional 
5672         Optional output array for the function results 
5673  
5674     Returns 
5675     ------- 
5676     scalar or ndarray 
5677         The value(s) of kolmogi(p) 
5678  
5679     See Also 
5680     -------- 
5681     kolmogorov : The Survival Function for the distribution 
5682     scipy.stats.kstwobign : Provides the functionality as a continuous distribution 
5683     smirnov, smirnovi : Functions for the one-sided distribution 
5684  
5685     Notes 
5686     ----- 
5687     `kolmogorov` is used by `stats.kstest` in the application of the 
5688     Kolmogorov-Smirnov Goodness of Fit test. For historical reasons this 
5689     function is exposed in `scpy.special`, but the recommended way to achieve 
5690     the most accurate CDF/SF/PDF/PPF/ISF computations is to use the 
5691     `stats.kstwobign` distribution. 
5692  
5693     Examples 
5694     -------- 
5695     >>> from scipy.special import kolmogi 
5696     >>> kolmogi([0, 0.1, 0.25, 0.5, 0.75, 0.9, 1.0]) 
5697     array([        inf,  1.22384787,  1.01918472,  0.82757356,  0.67644769, 
5698             0.57117327,  0.        ]) 
5699  
5700     """)
5701 
5702 add_newdoc("kolmogorov",
5703     r""" 
5704     kolmogorov(y, out=None) 
5705  
5706     Complementary cumulative distribution (Survival Function) function of 
5707     Kolmogorov distribution. 
5708  
5709     Returns the complementary cumulative distribution function of 
5710     Kolmogorov's limiting distribution (``D_n*\sqrt(n)`` as n goes to infinity) 
5711     of a two-sided test for equality between an empirical and a theoretical 
5712     distribution. It is equal to the (limit as n->infinity of the) 
5713     probability that ``sqrt(n) * max absolute deviation > y``. 
5714  
5715     Parameters 
5716     ---------- 
5717     y : float array_like 
5718       Absolute deviation between the Empirical CDF (ECDF) and the target CDF, 
5719       multiplied by sqrt(n). 
5720     out : ndarray, optional 
5721         Optional output array for the function results 
5722  
5723     Returns 
5724     ------- 
5725     scalar or ndarray 
5726         The value(s) of kolmogorov(y) 
5727  
5728     See Also 
5729     -------- 
5730     kolmogi : The Inverse Survival Function for the distribution 
5731     scipy.stats.kstwobign : Provides the functionality as a continuous distribution 
5732     smirnov, smirnovi : Functions for the one-sided distribution 
5733  
5734     Notes 
5735     ----- 
5736     `kolmogorov` is used by `stats.kstest` in the application of the 
5737     Kolmogorov-Smirnov Goodness of Fit test. For historical reasons this 
5738     function is exposed in `scpy.special`, but the recommended way to achieve 
5739     the most accurate CDF/SF/PDF/PPF/ISF computations is to use the 
5740     `stats.kstwobign` distribution. 
5741  
5742     Examples 
5743     -------- 
5744     Show the probability of a gap at least as big as 0, 0.5 and 1.0. 
5745  
5746     >>> import numpy as np 
5747     >>> from scipy.special import kolmogorov 
5748     >>> from scipy.stats import kstwobign 
5749     >>> kolmogorov([0, 0.5, 1.0]) 
5750     array([ 1.        ,  0.96394524,  0.26999967]) 
5751  
5752     Compare a sample of size 1000 drawn from a Laplace(0, 1) distribution against 
5753     the target distribution, a Normal(0, 1) distribution. 
5754  
5755     >>> from scipy.stats import norm, laplace 
5756     >>> rng = np.random.default_rng() 
5757     >>> n = 1000 
5758     >>> lap01 = laplace(0, 1) 
5759     >>> x = np.sort(lap01.rvs(n, random_state=rng)) 
5760     >>> np.mean(x), np.std(x) 
5761     (-0.05841730131499543, 1.3968109101997568) 
5762  
5763     Construct the Empirical CDF and the K-S statistic Dn. 
5764  
5765     >>> target = norm(0,1)  # Normal mean 0, stddev 1 
5766     >>> cdfs = target.cdf(x) 
5767     >>> ecdfs = np.arange(n+1, dtype=float)/n 
5768     >>> gaps = np.column_stack([cdfs - ecdfs[:n], ecdfs[1:] - cdfs]) 
5769     >>> Dn = np.max(gaps) 
5770     >>> Kn = np.sqrt(n) * Dn 
5771     >>> print('Dn=%f, sqrt(n)*Dn=%f' % (Dn, Kn)) 
5772     Dn=0.043363, sqrt(n)*Dn=1.371265 
5773     >>> print(chr(10).join(['For a sample of size n drawn from a N(0, 1) distribution:', 
5774     ...   ' the approximate Kolmogorov probability that sqrt(n)*Dn>=%f is %f' % 
5775     ...    (Kn, kolmogorov(Kn)), 
5776     ...   ' the approximate Kolmogorov probability that sqrt(n)*Dn<=%f is %f' % 
5777     ...    (Kn, kstwobign.cdf(Kn))])) 
5778     For a sample of size n drawn from a N(0, 1) distribution: 
5779      the approximate Kolmogorov probability that sqrt(n)*Dn>=1.371265 is 0.046533 
5780      the approximate Kolmogorov probability that sqrt(n)*Dn<=1.371265 is 0.953467 
5781  
5782     Plot the Empirical CDF against the target N(0, 1) CDF. 
5783  
5784     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
5785     >>> plt.step(np.concatenate([[-3], x]), ecdfs, where='post', label='Empirical CDF') 
5786     >>> x3 = np.linspace(-3, 3, 100) 
5787     >>> plt.plot(x3, target.cdf(x3), label='CDF for N(0, 1)') 
5788     >>> plt.ylim([0, 1]); plt.grid(True); plt.legend(); 
5789     >>> # Add vertical lines marking Dn+ and Dn- 
5790     >>> iminus, iplus = np.argmax(gaps, axis=0) 
5791     >>> plt.vlines([x[iminus]], ecdfs[iminus], cdfs[iminus], 
5792     ...            color='r', linestyle='dashed', lw=4) 
5793     >>> plt.vlines([x[iplus]], cdfs[iplus], ecdfs[iplus+1], 
5794     ...            color='r', linestyle='dashed', lw=4) 
5795     >>> plt.show() 
5796     """)
5797

5800     Internal function, do not use.

5813 add_newdoc("kv",
5814     r""" 
5815     kv(v, z, out=None) 
5816  
5817     Modified Bessel function of the second kind of real order `v` 
5818  
5819     Returns the modified Bessel function of the second kind for real order 
5820     `v` at complex `z`. 
5821  
5822     These are also sometimes called functions of the third kind, Basset 
5823     functions, or Macdonald functions.  They are defined as those solutions 
5824     of the modified Bessel equation for which, 
5825  
5826     .. math:: 
5827         K_v(x) \sim \sqrt{\pi/(2x)} \exp(-x) 
5828  
5829     as :math:`x \to \infty` [3]_. 
5830  
5831     Parameters 
5832     ---------- 
5833     v : array_like of float 
5834         Order of Bessel functions 
5835     z : array_like of complex 
5836         Argument at which to evaluate the Bessel functions 
5837     out : ndarray, optional 
5838         Optional output array for the function results 
5839  
5840     Returns 
5841     ------- 
5842     scalar or ndarray 
5843         The results. Note that input must be of complex type to get complex 
5844         output, e.g. ``kv(3, -2+0j)`` instead of ``kv(3, -2)``. 
5845  
5846     See Also 
5847     -------- 
5848     kve : This function with leading exponential behavior stripped off. 
5849     kvp : Derivative of this function 
5850  
5851     Notes 
5852     ----- 
5853     Wrapper for AMOS [1]_ routine `zbesk`.  For a discussion of the 
5854     algorithm used, see [2]_ and the references therein. 
5855  
5856     References 
5857     ---------- 
5858     .. [1] Donald E. Amos, "AMOS, A Portable Package for Bessel Functions 
5859            of a Complex Argument and Nonnegative Order", 
5860            http://netlib.org/amos/ 
5861     .. [2] Donald E. Amos, "Algorithm 644: A portable package for Bessel 
5862            functions of a complex argument and nonnegative order", ACM 
5863            TOMS Vol. 12 Issue 3, Sept. 1986, p. 265 
5864     .. [3] NIST Digital Library of Mathematical Functions, 
5865            Eq. 10.25.E3. https://dlmf.nist.gov/10.25.E3 
5866  
5867     Examples 
5868     -------- 
5869     Plot the function of several orders for real input: 
5870  
5871     >>> import numpy as np 
5872     >>> from scipy.special import kv 
5873     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
5874     >>> x = np.linspace(0, 5, 1000) 
5875     >>> for N in np.linspace(0, 6, 5): 
5876     ...     plt.plot(x, kv(N, x), label='$K_{{{}}}(x)$'.format(N)) 
5877     >>> plt.ylim(0, 10) 
5878     >>> plt.legend() 
5879     >>> plt.title(r'Modified Bessel function of the second kind $K_\nu(x)$') 
5880     >>> plt.show() 
5881  
5882     Calculate for a single value at multiple orders: 
5883  
5884     >>> kv([4, 4.5, 5], 1+2j) 
5885     array([ 0.1992+2.3892j,  2.3493+3.6j   ,  7.2827+3.8104j]) 
5886  
5887     """)
5888 
5889 add_newdoc("kve",
5890     r""" 
5891     kve(v, z, out=None) 
5892  
5893     Exponentially scaled modified Bessel function of the second kind. 
5894  
5895     Returns the exponentially scaled, modified Bessel function of the 
5896     second kind (sometimes called the third kind) for real order `v` at 
5897     complex `z`:: 
5898  
5899         kve(v, z) = kv(v, z) * exp(z) 
5900  
5901     Parameters 
5902     ---------- 
5903     v : array_like of float 
5904         Order of Bessel functions 
5905     z : array_like of complex 
5906         Argument at which to evaluate the Bessel functions 
5907     out : ndarray, optional 
5908         Optional output array for the function results 
5909  
5910     Returns 
5911     ------- 
5912     scalar or ndarray 
5913         The exponentially scaled modified Bessel function of the second kind. 
5914  
5915     See Also 
5916     -------- 
5917     kv : This function without exponential scaling. 
5918     k0e : Faster version of this function for order 0. 
5919     k1e : Faster version of this function for order 1. 
5920  
5921     Notes 
5922     ----- 
5923     Wrapper for AMOS [1]_ routine `zbesk`.  For a discussion of the 
5924     algorithm used, see [2]_ and the references therein. 
5925  
5926     References 
5927     ---------- 
5928     .. [1] Donald E. Amos, "AMOS, A Portable Package for Bessel Functions 
5929            of a Complex Argument and Nonnegative Order", 
5930            http://netlib.org/amos/ 
5931     .. [2] Donald E. Amos, "Algorithm 644: A portable package for Bessel 
5932            functions of a complex argument and nonnegative order", ACM 
5933            TOMS Vol. 12 Issue 3, Sept. 1986, p. 265 
5934  
5935     Examples 
5936     -------- 
5937     In the following example `kv` returns 0 whereas `kve` still returns 
5938     a useful finite number. 
5939  
5940     >>> import numpy as np 
5941     >>> from scipy.special import kv, kve 
5942     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
5943     >>> kv(3, 1000.), kve(3, 1000.) 
5944     (0.0, 0.03980696128440973) 
5945  
5946     Evaluate the function at one point for different orders by 
5947     providing a list or NumPy array as argument for the `v` parameter: 
5948  
5949     >>> kve([0, 1, 1.5], 1.) 
5950     array([1.14446308, 1.63615349, 2.50662827]) 
5951  
5952     Evaluate the function at several points for order 0 by providing an 
5953     array for `z`. 
5954  
5955     >>> points = np.array([1., 3., 10.]) 
5956     >>> kve(0, points) 
5957     array([1.14446308, 0.6977616 , 0.39163193]) 
5958  
5959     Evaluate the function at several points for different orders by 
5960     providing arrays for both `v` for `z`. Both arrays have to be 
5961     broadcastable to the correct shape. To calculate the orders 0, 1 
5962     and 2 for a 1D array of points: 
5963  
5964     >>> kve([[0], [1], [2]], points) 
5965     array([[1.14446308, 0.6977616 , 0.39163193], 
5966            [1.63615349, 0.80656348, 0.41076657], 
5967            [4.41677005, 1.23547058, 0.47378525]]) 
5968  
5969     Plot the functions of order 0 to 3 from 0 to 5. 
5970  
5971     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
5972     >>> x = np.linspace(0., 5., 1000) 
5973     >>> for i in range(4): 
5974     ...     ax.plot(x, kve(i, x), label=fr'$K_{i!r}(z)\cdot e^z$') 
5975     >>> ax.legend() 
5976     >>> ax.set_xlabel(r"$z$") 
5977     >>> ax.set_ylim(0, 4) 
5978     >>> ax.set_xlim(0, 5) 
5979     >>> plt.show() 
5980     """)
5981

5987 add_newdoc(
5988     "_landau_pdf",
5989     """ 
5990     _landau_pdf(x, loc, scale) 
5991  
5992     Probability density function of the Landau distribution. 
5993  
5994     Parameters 
5995     ---------- 
5996     x : array_like 
5997         Real-valued argument 
5998     loc : array_like 
5999         Real-valued distribution location 
6000     scale : array_like 
6001         Positive, real-valued distribution scale 
6002  
6003     Returns 
6004     -------
