6140 add_newdoc("lpmv",
6141     r""" 
6142     lpmv(m, v, x, out=None) 
6143  
6144     Associated Legendre function of integer order and real degree. 
6145  
6146     Defined as 
6147  
6148     .. math:: 
6149  
6150         P_v^m = (-1)^m (1 - x^2)^{m/2} \frac{d^m}{dx^m} P_v(x) 
6151  
6152     where 
6153  
6154     .. math:: 
6155  
6156         P_v = \sum_{k = 0}^\infty \frac{(-v)_k (v + 1)_k}{(k!)^2} 
6157                 \left(\frac{1 - x}{2}\right)^k 
6158  
6159     is the Legendre function of the first kind. Here :math:`(\cdot)_k` 
6160     is the Pochhammer symbol; see `poch`. 
6161  
6162     Parameters 
6163     ---------- 
6164     m : array_like 
6165         Order (int or float). If passed a float not equal to an 
6166         integer the function returns NaN. 
6167     v : array_like 
6168         Degree (float). 
6169     x : array_like 
6170         Argument (float). Must have ``|x| <= 1``. 
6171     out : ndarray, optional 
6172         Optional output array for the function results 
6173  
6174     Returns 
6175     ------- 
6176     pmv : scalar or ndarray 
6177         Value of the associated Legendre function. 
6178  
6179     See Also 
6180     -------- 
6181     lpmn : Compute the associated Legendre function for all orders 
6182            ``0, ..., m`` and degrees ``0, ..., n``. 
6183     clpmn : Compute the associated Legendre function at complex 
6184             arguments. 
6185  
6186     Notes 
6187     ----- 
6188     Note that this implementation includes the Condon-Shortley phase. 
6189  
6190     References 
6191     ---------- 
6192     .. [1] Zhang, Jin, "Computation of Special Functions", John Wiley 
6193            and Sons, Inc, 1996. 
6194  
6195     """)
6196 
6197 add_newdoc("nbdtr",
6198     r""" 
6199     nbdtr(k, n, p, out=None) 
6200  
6201     Negative binomial cumulative distribution function. 
6202  
6203     Returns the sum of the terms 0 through `k` of the negative binomial 
6204     distribution probability mass function, 
6205  
6206     .. math:: 
6207  
6208         F = \sum_{j=0}^k {{n + j - 1}\choose{j}} p^n (1 - p)^j. 
6209  
6210     In a sequence of Bernoulli trials with individual success probabilities 
6211     `p`, this is the probability that `k` or fewer failures precede the nth 
6212     success. 
6213  
6214     Parameters 
6215     ---------- 
6216     k : array_like 
6217         The maximum number of allowed failures (nonnegative int). 
6218     n : array_like 
6219         The target number of successes (positive int). 
6220     p : array_like 
6221         Probability of success in a single event (float). 
6222     out : ndarray, optional 
6223         Optional output array for the function results 
6224  
6225     Returns 
6226     ------- 
6227     F : scalar or ndarray 
6228         The probability of `k` or fewer failures before `n` successes in a 
6229         sequence of events with individual success probability `p`. 
6230  
6231     See Also 
6232     -------- 
6233     nbdtrc : Negative binomial survival function 
6234     nbdtrik : Negative binomial quantile function 
6235     scipy.stats.nbinom : Negative binomial distribution 
6236  
6237     Notes 
6238     ----- 
6239     If floating point values are passed for `k` or `n`, they will be truncated 
6240     to integers. 
6241  
6242     The terms are not summed directly; instead the regularized incomplete beta 
6243     function is employed, according to the formula, 
6244  
6245     .. math:: 
6246         \mathrm{nbdtr}(k, n, p) = I_{p}(n, k + 1). 
6247  
6248     Wrapper for the Cephes [1]_ routine `nbdtr`. 
6249  
6250     The negative binomial distribution is also available as 
6251     `scipy.stats.nbinom`. Using `nbdtr` directly can improve performance 
6252     compared to the ``cdf`` method of `scipy.stats.nbinom` (see last example). 
6253  
6254     References 
6255     ---------- 
6256     .. [1] Cephes Mathematical Functions Library, 
6257            http://www.netlib.org/cephes/ 
6258  
6259     Examples 
6260     -------- 
6261     Compute the function for ``k=10`` and ``n=5`` at ``p=0.5``. 
6262  
6263     >>> import numpy as np 
6264     >>> from scipy.special import nbdtr 
6265     >>> nbdtr(10, 5, 0.5) 
6266     0.940765380859375 
6267  
6268     Compute the function for ``n=10`` and ``p=0.5`` at several points by 
6269     providing a NumPy array or list for `k`. 
6270  
6271     >>> nbdtr([5, 10, 15], 10, 0.5) 
6272     array([0.15087891, 0.58809853, 0.88523853]) 
6273  
6274     Plot the function for four different parameter sets. 
6275  
6276     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
6277     >>> k = np.arange(130) 
6278     >>> n_parameters = [20, 20, 20, 80] 
6279     >>> p_parameters = [0.2, 0.5, 0.8, 0.5] 
6280     >>> linestyles = ['solid', 'dashed', 'dotted', 'dashdot'] 
6281     >>> parameters_list = list(zip(p_parameters, n_parameters, 
6282     ...                            linestyles)) 
6283     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, 8)) 
6284     >>> for parameter_set in parameters_list: 
6285     ...     p, n, style = parameter_set 
6286     ...     nbdtr_vals = nbdtr(k, n, p) 
6287     ...     ax.plot(k, nbdtr_vals, label=rf"$n={n},\, p={p}$", 
6288     ...             ls=style) 
6289     >>> ax.legend() 
6290     >>> ax.set_xlabel("$k$") 
6291     >>> ax.set_title("Negative binomial cumulative distribution function") 
6292     >>> plt.show() 
6293  
6294     The negative binomial distribution is also available as 
6295     `scipy.stats.nbinom`. Using `nbdtr` directly can be much faster than 
6296     calling the ``cdf`` method of `scipy.stats.nbinom`, especially for small 
6297     arrays or individual values. To get the same results one must use the 
6298     following parametrization: ``nbinom(n, p).cdf(k)=nbdtr(k, n, p)``. 
6299  
6300     >>> from scipy.stats import nbinom 
6301     >>> k, n, p = 5, 3, 0.5 
6302     >>> nbdtr_res = nbdtr(k, n, p)  # this will often be faster than below 
6303     >>> stats_res = nbinom(n, p).cdf(k) 
6304     >>> stats_res, nbdtr_res  # test that results are equal 
6305     (0.85546875, 0.85546875) 
6306  
6307     `nbdtr` can evaluate different parameter sets by providing arrays with 
6308     shapes compatible for broadcasting for `k`, `n` and `p`. Here we compute 
6309     the function for three different `k` at four locations `p`, resulting in 
6310     a 3x4 array. 
6311  
6312     >>> k = np.array([[5], [10], [15]]) 
6313     >>> p = np.array([0.3, 0.5, 0.7, 0.9]) 
6314     >>> k.shape, p.shape 
6315     ((3, 1), (4,)) 
6316  
6317     >>> nbdtr(k, 5, p) 
6318     array([[0.15026833, 0.62304687, 0.95265101, 0.9998531 ], 
6319            [0.48450894, 0.94076538, 0.99932777, 0.99999999], 
6320            [0.76249222, 0.99409103, 0.99999445, 1.        ]]) 
6321     """)
6322 
6323 add_newdoc("nbdtrc",
6324     r""" 
6325     nbdtrc(k, n, p, out=None) 
6326  
6327     Negative binomial survival function. 
6328  
6329     Returns the sum of the terms `k + 1` to infinity of the negative binomial 
6330     distribution probability mass function, 
6331  
6332     .. math:: 
6333  
6334         F = \sum_{j=k + 1}^\infty {{n + j - 1}\choose{j}} p^n (1 - p)^j. 
6335  
6336     In a sequence of Bernoulli trials with individual success probabilities 
6337     `p`, this is the probability that more than `k` failures precede the nth 
6338     success. 
6339  
6340     Parameters 
6341     ---------- 
6342     k : array_like 
6343         The maximum number of allowed failures (nonnegative int). 
6344     n : array_like 
6345         The target number of successes (positive int). 
6346     p : array_like 
6347         Probability of success in a single event (float). 
6348     out : ndarray, optional 
6349         Optional output array for the function results 
6350  
6351     Returns 
6352     ------- 
6353     F : scalar or ndarray 
6354         The probability of `k + 1` or more failures before `n` successes in a 
6355         sequence of events with individual success probability `p`. 
6356  
6357     See Also 
6358     -------- 
6359     nbdtr : Negative binomial cumulative distribution function 
6360     nbdtrik : Negative binomial percentile function 
6361     scipy.stats.nbinom : Negative binomial distribution 
6362  
6363     Notes 
6364     ----- 
6365     If floating point values are passed for `k` or `n`, they will be truncated 
6366     to integers. 
6367  
6368     The terms are not summed directly; instead the regularized incomplete beta 
6369     function is employed, according to the formula, 
6370  
6371     .. math:: 
6372         \mathrm{nbdtrc}(k, n, p) = I_{1 - p}(k + 1, n). 
6373  
6374     Wrapper for the Cephes [1]_ routine `nbdtrc`. 
6375  
6376     The negative binomial distribution is also available as 
6377     `scipy.stats.nbinom`. Using `nbdtrc` directly can improve performance 
6378     compared to the ``sf`` method of `scipy.stats.nbinom` (see last example). 
6379  
6380     References 
6381     ---------- 
6382     .. [1] Cephes Mathematical Functions Library, 
6383            http://www.netlib.org/cephes/ 
6384  
6385     Examples 
6386     -------- 
6387     Compute the function for ``k=10`` and ``n=5`` at ``p=0.5``. 
6388  
6389     >>> import numpy as np 
6390     >>> from scipy.special import nbdtrc 
6391     >>> nbdtrc(10, 5, 0.5) 
6392     0.059234619140624986 
6393  
6394     Compute the function for ``n=10`` and ``p=0.5`` at several points by 
6395     providing a NumPy array or list for `k`. 
6396  
6397     >>> nbdtrc([5, 10, 15], 10, 0.5) 
6398     array([0.84912109, 0.41190147, 0.11476147]) 
6399  
6400     Plot the function for four different parameter sets. 
6401  
6402     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
6403     >>> k = np.arange(130) 
6404     >>> n_parameters = [20, 20, 20, 80] 
6405     >>> p_parameters = [0.2, 0.5, 0.8, 0.5] 
6406     >>> linestyles = ['solid', 'dashed', 'dotted', 'dashdot'] 
6407     >>> parameters_list = list(zip(p_parameters, n_parameters, 
6408     ...                            linestyles)) 
6409     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, 8)) 
6410     >>> for parameter_set in parameters_list: 
6411     ...     p, n, style = parameter_set 
6412     ...     nbdtrc_vals = nbdtrc(k, n, p) 
6413     ...     ax.plot(k, nbdtrc_vals, label=rf"$n={n},\, p={p}$", 
6414     ...             ls=style) 
6415     >>> ax.legend() 
6416     >>> ax.set_xlabel("$k$") 
6417     >>> ax.set_title("Negative binomial distribution survival function") 
6418     >>> plt.show() 
6419  
6420     The negative binomial distribution is also available as 
6421     `scipy.stats.nbinom`. Using `nbdtrc` directly can be much faster than 
6422     calling the ``sf`` method of `scipy.stats.nbinom`, especially for small 
6423     arrays or individual values. To get the same results one must use the 
6424     following parametrization: ``nbinom(n, p).sf(k)=nbdtrc(k, n, p)``. 
6425  
6426     >>> from scipy.stats import nbinom 
6427     >>> k, n, p = 3, 5, 0.5 
6428     >>> nbdtr_res = nbdtrc(k, n, p)  # this will often be faster than below 
6429     >>> stats_res = nbinom(n, p).sf(k) 
6430     >>> stats_res, nbdtr_res  # test that results are equal 
6431     (0.6367187499999999, 0.6367187499999999) 
6432  
6433     `nbdtrc` can evaluate different parameter sets by providing arrays with 
6434     shapes compatible for broadcasting for `k`, `n` and `p`. Here we compute 
6435     the function for three different `k` at four locations `p`, resulting in 
6436     a 3x4 array. 
6437  
6438     >>> k = np.array([[5], [10], [15]]) 
6439     >>> p = np.array([0.3, 0.5, 0.7, 0.9]) 
6440     >>> k.shape, p.shape 
6441     ((3, 1), (4,)) 
6442  
6443     >>> nbdtrc(k, 5, p) 
6444     array([[8.49731667e-01, 3.76953125e-01, 4.73489874e-02, 1.46902600e-04], 
6445            [5.15491059e-01, 5.92346191e-02, 6.72234070e-04, 9.29610100e-09], 
6446            [2.37507779e-01, 5.90896606e-03, 5.55025308e-06, 3.26346760e-13]]) 
6447     """)

6449 add_newdoc(
6450     "nbdtri",
6451     r""" 
6452     nbdtri(k, n, y, out=None) 
6453  
6454     Returns the inverse with respect to the parameter `p` of 
6455     ``y = nbdtr(k, n, p)``, the negative binomial cumulative distribution 
6456     function. 
6457  
6458     Parameters 
6459     ---------- 
6460     k : array_like 
6461         The maximum number of allowed failures (nonnegative int). 
6462     n : array_like 
6463         The target number of successes (positive int). 
6464     y : array_like 
6465         The probability of `k` or fewer failures before `n` successes (float). 
6466     out : ndarray, optional 
6467         Optional output array for the function results 
6468  
6469     Returns 
6470     ------- 
6471     p : scalar or ndarray 
6472         Probability of success in a single event (float) such that 
6473         `nbdtr(k, n, p) = y`. 
6474  
6475     See Also 
6476     -------- 
6477     nbdtr : Cumulative distribution function of the negative binomial. 
6478     nbdtrc : Negative binomial survival function. 
6479     scipy.stats.nbinom : negative binomial distribution. 
6480     nbdtrik : Inverse with respect to `k` of `nbdtr(k, n, p)`. 
6481     nbdtrin : Inverse with respect to `n` of `nbdtr(k, n, p)`. 
6482     scipy.stats.nbinom : Negative binomial distribution 
6483  
6484     Notes 
6485     ----- 
6486     Wrapper for the Cephes [1]_ routine `nbdtri`. 
6487  
6488     The negative binomial distribution is also available as 
6489     `scipy.stats.nbinom`. Using `nbdtri` directly can improve performance 
6490     compared to the ``ppf`` method of `scipy.stats.nbinom`. 
6491  
6492     References 
6493     ---------- 
6494     .. [1] Cephes Mathematical Functions Library, 
6495            http://www.netlib.org/cephes/ 
6496  
6497     Examples 
6498     -------- 
6499     `nbdtri` is the inverse of `nbdtr` with respect to `p`. 
6500     Up to floating point errors the following holds: 
6501     ``nbdtri(k, n, nbdtr(k, n, p))=p``. 
6502  
6503     >>> import numpy as np 
6504     >>> from scipy.special import nbdtri, nbdtr 
6505     >>> k, n, y = 5, 10, 0.2 
6506     >>> cdf_val = nbdtr(k, n, y) 
6507     >>> nbdtri(k, n, cdf_val) 
6508     0.20000000000000004 
6509  
6510     Compute the function for ``k=10`` and ``n=5`` at several points by 
6511     providing a NumPy array or list for `y`. 
6512  
6513     >>> y = np.array([0.1, 0.4, 0.8]) 
6514     >>> nbdtri(3, 5, y) 
6515     array([0.34462319, 0.51653095, 0.69677416]) 
6516  
6517     Plot the function for three different parameter sets. 
6518  
6519     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
6520     >>> n_parameters = [5, 20, 30, 30] 
6521     >>> k_parameters = [20, 20, 60, 80] 
6522     >>> linestyles = ['solid', 'dashed', 'dotted', 'dashdot'] 
6523     >>> parameters_list = list(zip(n_parameters, k_parameters, linestyles)) 
6524     >>> cdf_vals = np.linspace(0, 1, 1000) 
6525     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, 8)) 
6526     >>> for parameter_set in parameters_list: 
6527     ...     n, k, style = parameter_set 
6528     ...     nbdtri_vals = nbdtri(k, n, cdf_vals) 
6529     ...     ax.plot(cdf_vals, nbdtri_vals, label=rf"$k={k},\ n={n}$", 
6530     ...             ls=style) 
6531     >>> ax.legend() 
6532     >>> ax.set_ylabel("$p$") 
6533     >>> ax.set_xlabel("$CDF$") 
6534     >>> title = "nbdtri: inverse of negative binomial CDF with respect to $p$" 
6535     >>> ax.set_title(title) 
6536     >>> plt.show() 
6537  
6538     `nbdtri` can evaluate different parameter sets by providing arrays with 
6539     shapes compatible for broadcasting for `k`, `n` and `p`. Here we compute 
6540     the function for three different `k` at four locations `p`, resulting in 
6541     a 3x4 array. 
6542  
6543     >>> k = np.array([[5], [10], [15]]) 
6544     >>> y = np.array([0.3, 0.5, 0.7, 0.9]) 
6545     >>> k.shape, y.shape 
6546     ((3, 1), (4,)) 
6547  
6548     >>> nbdtri(k, 5, y) 
6549     array([[0.37258157, 0.45169416, 0.53249956, 0.64578407], 
6550            [0.24588501, 0.30451981, 0.36778453, 0.46397088], 
6551            [0.18362101, 0.22966758, 0.28054743, 0.36066188]]) 
6552     """)
6553 
6554 add_newdoc("nbdtrik",
6555     r""" 
6556     nbdtrik(y, n, p, out=None) 
6557  
6558     Negative binomial percentile function. 
6559  
6560     Returns the inverse with respect to the parameter `k` of 
6561     ``y = nbdtr(k, n, p)``, the negative binomial cumulative distribution 
6562     function. 
6563  
6564     Parameters 
6565     ---------- 
6566     y : array_like 
6567         The probability of `k` or fewer failures before `n` successes (float). 
6568     n : array_like 
6569         The target number of successes (positive int). 
6570     p : array_like 
6571         Probability of success in a single event (float). 
6572     out : ndarray, optional 
6573         Optional output array for the function results 
6574  
6575     Returns 
6576     ------- 
6577     k : scalar or ndarray 
6578         The maximum number of allowed failures such that `nbdtr(k, n, p) = y`. 
6579  
6580     See Also 
6581     -------- 
6582     nbdtr : Cumulative distribution function of the negative binomial. 
6583     nbdtrc : Survival function of the negative binomial. 
6584     nbdtri : Inverse with respect to `p` of `nbdtr(k, n, p)`. 
6585     nbdtrin : Inverse with respect to `n` of `nbdtr(k, n, p)`. 
6586     scipy.stats.nbinom : Negative binomial distribution 
6587  
6588     Notes 
6589     ----- 
6590     Wrapper for the CDFLIB [1]_ Fortran routine `cdfnbn`. 
6591  
6592     Formula 26.5.26 of [2]_, 
6593  
6594     .. math:: 
6595         \sum_{j=k + 1}^\infty {{n + j - 1} 
6596         \choose{j}} p^n (1 - p)^j = I_{1 - p}(k + 1, n), 
6597  
6598     is used to reduce calculation of the cumulative distribution function to 
6599     that of a regularized incomplete beta :math:`I`. 
6600  
6601     Computation of `k` involves a search for a value that produces the desired 
6602     value of `y`.  The search relies on the monotonicity of `y` with `k`. 
6603  
6604     References 
6605     ---------- 
6606     .. [1] Barry Brown, James Lovato, and Kathy Russell, 
6607            CDFLIB: Library of Fortran Routines for Cumulative Distribution 
6608            Functions, Inverses, and Other Parameters. 
6609     .. [2] Milton Abramowitz and Irene A. Stegun, eds. 
6610            Handbook of Mathematical Functions with Formulas, 
6611            Graphs, and Mathematical Tables. New York: Dover, 1972. 
6612  
6613     Examples 
6614     -------- 
6615     Compute the negative binomial cumulative distribution function for an 
6616     exemplary parameter set. 
6617  
6618     >>> import numpy as np 
6619     >>> from scipy.special import nbdtr, nbdtrik 
6620     >>> k, n, p = 5, 2, 0.5 
6621     >>> cdf_value = nbdtr(k, n, p) 
6622     >>> cdf_value 
6623     0.9375 
6624  
6625     Verify that `nbdtrik` recovers the original value for `k`. 
6626  
6627     >>> nbdtrik(cdf_value, n, p) 
6628     5.0 
6629  
6630     Plot the function for different parameter sets. 
6631  
6632     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
6633     >>> p_parameters = [0.2, 0.5, 0.7, 0.5] 
6634     >>> n_parameters = [30, 30, 30, 80] 
6635     >>> linestyles = ['solid', 'dashed', 'dotted', 'dashdot'] 
6636     >>> parameters_list = list(zip(p_parameters, n_parameters, linestyles)) 
6637     >>> cdf_vals = np.linspace(0, 1, 1000) 
6638     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, 8)) 
6639     >>> for parameter_set in parameters_list: 
6640     ...     p, n, style = parameter_set 
6641     ...     nbdtrik_vals = nbdtrik(cdf_vals, n, p) 
6642     ...     ax.plot(cdf_vals, nbdtrik_vals, label=rf"$n={n},\ p={p}$", 
6643     ...             ls=style) 
6644     >>> ax.legend() 
6645     >>> ax.set_ylabel("$k$") 
6646     >>> ax.set_xlabel("$CDF$") 
6647     >>> ax.set_title("Negative binomial percentile function") 
6648     >>> plt.show() 
6649  
6650     The negative binomial distribution is also available as 
6651     `scipy.stats.nbinom`. The percentile function  method ``ppf`` 
6652     returns the result of `nbdtrik` rounded up to integers: 
6653  
6654     >>> from scipy.stats import nbinom 
6655     >>> q, n, p = 0.6, 5, 0.5 
6656     >>> nbinom.ppf(q, n, p), nbdtrik(q, n, p) 
6657     (5.0, 4.800428460273882) 
6658  
6659     """)
6660 
6661 add_newdoc("nbdtrin",
6662     r""" 
6663     nbdtrin(k, y, p, out=None) 
6664  
6665     Inverse of `nbdtr` vs `n`. 
6666  
6667     Returns the inverse with respect to the parameter `n` of 
6668     ``y = nbdtr(k, n, p)``, the negative binomial cumulative distribution 
6669     function. 
6670  
6671     Parameters 
6672     ---------- 
6673     k : array_like 
6674         The maximum number of allowed failures (nonnegative int). 
6675     y : array_like 
6676         The probability of `k` or fewer failures before `n` successes (float). 
6677     p : array_like 
6678         Probability of success in a single event (float). 
6679     out : ndarray, optional 
6680         Optional output array for the function results 
6681  
6682     Returns 
6683     ------- 
6684     n : scalar or ndarray 
6685         The number of successes `n` such that `nbdtr(k, n, p) = y`. 
6686  
6687     See Also 
6688     -------- 
6689     nbdtr : Cumulative distribution function of the negative binomial. 
6690     nbdtri : Inverse with respect to `p` of `nbdtr(k, n, p)`. 
6691     nbdtrik : Inverse with respect to `k` of `nbdtr(k, n, p)`. 
6692  
6693     Notes 
6694     ----- 
6695     Wrapper for the CDFLIB [1]_ Fortran routine `cdfnbn`. 
6696  
6697     Formula 26.5.26 of [2]_, 
6698  
6699     .. math:: 
6700         \sum_{j=k + 1}^\infty {{n + j - 1} 
6701         \choose{j}} p^n (1 - p)^j = I_{1 - p}(k + 1, n), 
6702  
6703     is used to reduce calculation of the cumulative distribution function to 
6704     that of a regularized incomplete beta :math:`I`. 
6705  
6706     Computation of `n` involves a search for a value that produces the desired 
6707     value of `y`.  The search relies on the monotonicity of `y` with `n`. 
6708  
6709     References 
6710     ---------- 
6711     .. [1] Barry Brown, James Lovato, and Kathy Russell, 
6712            CDFLIB: Library of Fortran Routines for Cumulative Distribution 
6713            Functions, Inverses, and Other Parameters. 
6714     .. [2] Milton Abramowitz and Irene A. Stegun, eds. 
6715            Handbook of Mathematical Functions with Formulas, 
6716            Graphs, and Mathematical Tables. New York: Dover, 1972. 
6717  
6718     Examples 
6719     -------- 
6720     Compute the negative binomial cumulative distribution function for an 
6721     exemplary parameter set. 
6722  
6723     >>> from scipy.special import nbdtr, nbdtrin 
6724     >>> k, n, p = 5, 2, 0.5 
6725     >>> cdf_value = nbdtr(k, n, p) 
6726     >>> cdf_value 
6727     0.9375 
6728  
6729     Verify that `nbdtrin` recovers the original value for `n` up to floating 
6730     point accuracy. 
6731  
6732     >>> nbdtrin(k, cdf_value, p) 
6733     1.999999999998137 
6734     """)
6735 
6736 add_newdoc("ncfdtr",
6737     r""" 
6738     ncfdtr(dfn, dfd, nc, f, out=None) 
6739  
6740     Cumulative distribution function of the non-central F distribution. 
6741  
6742     The non-central F describes the distribution of, 
6743  
6744     .. math:: 
6745         Z = \frac{X/d_n}{Y/d_d} 
6746  
6747     where :math:`X` and :math:`Y` are independently distributed, with 
6748     :math:`X` distributed non-central :math:`\chi^2` with noncentrality 
6749     parameter `nc` and :math:`d_n` degrees of freedom, and :math:`Y` 
6750     distributed :math:`\chi^2` with :math:`d_d` degrees of freedom. 
6751  
6752     Parameters 
6753     ---------- 
6754     dfn : array_like 
6755         Degrees of freedom of the numerator sum of squares.  Range (0, inf). 
6756     dfd : array_like 
6757         Degrees of freedom of the denominator sum of squares.  Range (0, inf). 
6758     nc : array_like 
6759         Noncentrality parameter.  Range [0, inf). 
6760     f : array_like 
6761         Quantiles, i.e. the upper limit of integration. 
6762     out : ndarray, optional 
6763         Optional output array for the function results 
6764  
6765     Returns 
6766     ------- 
6767     cdf : scalar or ndarray 
6768         The calculated CDF.  If all inputs are scalar, the return will be a 
6769         float.  Otherwise it will be an array. 
6770  
6771     See Also 
6772     -------- 
6773     ncfdtri : Quantile function; inverse of `ncfdtr` with respect to `f`. 
6774     ncfdtridfd : Inverse of `ncfdtr` with respect to `dfd`. 
6775     ncfdtridfn : Inverse of `ncfdtr` with respect to `dfn`. 
6776     ncfdtrinc : Inverse of `ncfdtr` with respect to `nc`. 
6777     scipy.stats.ncf : Non-central F distribution. 
6778  
6779     Notes 
6780     ----- 
6781     This function calculates the CDF of the non-central f distribution using 
6782     the Boost Math C++ library [1]_. 
6783  
6784     The cumulative distribution function is computed using Formula 26.6.20 of 
6785     [2]_: 
6786  
6787     .. math:: 
6788         F(d_n, d_d, n_c, f) = \sum_{j=0}^\infty e^{-n_c/2} 
6789         \frac{(n_c/2)^j}{j!} I_{x}(\frac{d_n}{2} + j, \frac{d_d}{2}), 
6790  
6791     where :math:`I` is the regularized incomplete beta function, and 
6792     :math:`x = f d_n/(f d_n + d_d)`. 
6793  
6794     Note that argument order of `ncfdtr` is different from that of the 
6795     similar ``cdf`` method of `scipy.stats.ncf`: `f` is the last 
6796     parameter of `ncfdtr` but the first parameter of ``scipy.stats.ncf.cdf``. 
6797  
6798     References 
6799     ---------- 
6800     .. [1] The Boost Developers. "Boost C++ Libraries". https://www.boost.org/. 
6801     .. [2] Milton Abramowitz and Irene A. Stegun, eds. 
6802            Handbook of Mathematical Functions with Formulas, 
6803            Graphs, and Mathematical Tables. New York: Dover, 1972. 
6804  
6805     Examples 
6806     -------- 
6807     >>> import numpy as np 
6808     >>> from scipy import special 
6809     >>> from scipy import stats 
6810     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
6811  
6812     Plot the CDF of the non-central F distribution, for nc=0.  Compare with the 
6813     F-distribution from scipy.stats: 
6814  
6815     >>> x = np.linspace(-1, 8, num=500) 
6816     >>> dfn = 3 
6817     >>> dfd = 2 
6818     >>> ncf_stats = stats.f.cdf(x, dfn, dfd) 
6819     >>> ncf_special = special.ncfdtr(dfn, dfd, 0, x) 
6820  
6821     >>> fig = plt.figure() 
6822     >>> ax = fig.add_subplot(111) 
6823     >>> ax.plot(x, ncf_stats, 'b-', lw=3) 
6824     >>> ax.plot(x, ncf_special, 'r-') 
6825     >>> plt.show() 
6826  
6827     """)
6828 
6829 add_newdoc("ncfdtri",
6830     """ 
6831     ncfdtri(dfn, dfd, nc, p, out=None) 
6832  
6833     Inverse with respect to `f` of the CDF of the non-central F distribution. 
6834  
6835     See `ncfdtr` for more details. 
6836  
6837     Parameters 
6838     ---------- 
6839     dfn : array_like 
6840         Degrees of freedom of the numerator sum of squares.  Range (0, inf). 
6841     dfd : array_like 
6842         Degrees of freedom of the denominator sum of squares.  Range (0, inf). 
6843     nc : array_like 
6844         Noncentrality parameter.  Range [0, inf). 
6845     p : array_like 
6846         Value of the cumulative distribution function.  Must be in the 
6847         range [0, 1]. 
6848     out : ndarray, optional 
6849         Optional output array for the function results 
6850  
6851     Returns 
6852     ------- 
6853     f : scalar or ndarray 
6854         Quantiles, i.e., the upper limit of integration. 
6855  
6856     See Also 
6857     -------- 
6858     ncfdtr : CDF of the non-central F distribution. 
6859     ncfdtridfd : Inverse of `ncfdtr` with respect to `dfd`. 
6860     ncfdtridfn : Inverse of `ncfdtr` with respect to `dfn`. 
6861     ncfdtrinc : Inverse of `ncfdtr` with respect to `nc`. 
6862     scipy.stats.ncf : Non-central F distribution. 
6863  
6864     Notes 
6865     ----- 
6866     This function calculates the Quantile of the non-central f distribution 
6867     using the Boost Math C++ library [1]_. 
6868  
6869     Note that argument order of `ncfdtri` is different from that of the 
6870     similar ``ppf`` method of `scipy.stats.ncf`. `p` is the last parameter 
6871     of `ncfdtri` but the first parameter of ``scipy.stats.ncf.ppf``. 
6872  
6873     References 
6874     ---------- 
6875     .. [1] The Boost Developers. "Boost C++ Libraries". https://www.boost.org/. 
6876  
6877     Examples 
6878     -------- 
6879     >>> from scipy.special import ncfdtr, ncfdtri 
6880  
6881     Compute the CDF for several values of `f`: 
6882  
6883     >>> f = [0.5, 1, 1.5] 
6884     >>> p = ncfdtr(2, 3, 1.5, f) 
6885     >>> p 
6886     array([ 0.20782291,  0.36107392,  0.47345752]) 
6887  
6888     Compute the inverse.  We recover the values of `f`, as expected: 
6889  
6890     >>> ncfdtri(2, 3, 1.5, p) 
6891     array([ 0.5,  1. ,  1.5]) 
6892  
6893     """)

6895 add_newdoc("ncfdtridfd",
6896     """ 
6897     ncfdtridfd(dfn, p, nc, f, out=None) 
6898  
6899     Calculate degrees of freedom (denominator) for the noncentral F-distribution. 
6900  
6901     This is the inverse with respect to `dfd` of `ncfdtr`. 
6902     See `ncfdtr` for more details. 
6903  
6904     Parameters 
6905     ---------- 
6906     dfn : array_like 
6907         Degrees of freedom of the numerator sum of squares.  Range (0, inf). 
6908     p : array_like 
6909         Value of the cumulative distribution function.  Must be in the 
6910         range [0, 1]. 
6911     nc : array_like 
6912         Noncentrality parameter.  Should be in range (0, 1e4). 
6913     f : array_like 
6914         Quantiles, i.e., the upper limit of integration. 
6915     out : ndarray, optional 
6916         Optional output array for the function results 
6917  
6918     Returns 
6919     ------- 
6920     dfd : scalar or ndarray 
6921         Degrees of freedom of the denominator sum of squares. 
6922  
6923     See Also 
6924     -------- 
6925     ncfdtr : CDF of the non-central F distribution. 
6926     ncfdtri : Quantile function; inverse of `ncfdtr` with respect to `f`. 
6927     ncfdtridfn : Inverse of `ncfdtr` with respect to `dfn`. 
6928     ncfdtrinc : Inverse of `ncfdtr` with respect to `nc`. 
6929  
6930     Notes 
6931     ----- 
6932     The value of the cumulative noncentral F distribution is not necessarily 
6933     monotone in either degrees of freedom. There thus may be two values that 
6934     provide a given CDF value. This routine assumes monotonicity and will 
6935     find an arbitrary one of the two values. 
6936  
6937     Examples 
6938     -------- 
6939     >>> from scipy.special import ncfdtr, ncfdtridfd 
6940  
6941     Compute the CDF for several values of `dfd`: 
6942  
6943     >>> dfd = [1, 2, 3] 
6944     >>> p = ncfdtr(2, dfd, 0.25, 15) 
6945     >>> p 
6946     array([ 0.8097138 ,  0.93020416,  0.96787852]) 
6947  
6948     Compute the inverse.  We recover the values of `dfd`, as expected: 
6949  
6950     >>> ncfdtridfd(2, p, 0.25, 15) 
6951     array([ 1.,  2.,  3.]) 
6952  
6953     """)

7252         Quantiles

7258     nctdtrinc : Calculate non-centrality parameter, given CDF iCDF values. 
7259  
7260     Examples 
7261     -------- 
7262     >>> from scipy.special import nctdtr, nctdtrit 
7263  
7264     Compute the CDF for several values of `t`: 
7265  
7266     >>> t = [0.5, 1, 1.5] 
7267     >>> p = nctdtr(3, 1, t) 
7268     >>> p 
7269     array([0.29811049, 0.46922687, 0.6257559 ]) 
7270  
7271     Compute the inverse. We recover the values of `t`, as expected: 
7272  
7273     >>> nctdtrit(3, 1, p) 
7274     array([0.5, 1. , 1.5]) 
7275  
7276     """)

7552 add_newdoc("pdtr",
7553     r""" 
7554     pdtr(k, m, out=None) 
7555  
7556     Poisson cumulative distribution function. 
7557  
7558     Defined as the probability that a Poisson-distributed random 
7559     variable with event rate :math:`m` is less than or equal to 
7560     :math:`k`. More concretely, this works out to be [1]_ 
7561  
7562     .. math:: 
7563  
7564        \exp(-m) \sum_{j = 0}^{\lfloor{k}\rfloor} \frac{m^j}{j!}. 
7565  
7566     Parameters 
7567     ---------- 
7568     k : array_like 
7569         Number of occurrences (nonnegative, real) 
7570     m : array_like 
7571         Shape parameter (nonnegative, real) 
7572     out : ndarray, optional 
7573         Optional output array for the function results 
7574  
7575     Returns 
7576     ------- 
7577     scalar or ndarray 
7578         Values of the Poisson cumulative distribution function 
7579  
7580     See Also 
7581     -------- 
7582     pdtrc : Poisson survival function 
7583     pdtrik : inverse of `pdtr` with respect to `k` 
7584     pdtri : inverse of `pdtr` with respect to `m` 
7585  
7586     References 
7587     ---------- 
7588     .. [1] https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poisson_distribution 
7589  
7590     Examples 
7591     -------- 
7592     >>> import numpy as np 
7593     >>> import scipy.special as sc 
7594  
7595     It is a cumulative distribution function, so it converges to 1 
7596     monotonically as `k` goes to infinity. 
7597  
7598     >>> sc.pdtr([1, 10, 100, np.inf], 1) 
7599     array([0.73575888, 0.99999999, 1.        , 1.        ]) 
7600  
7601     It is discontinuous at integers and constant between integers. 
7602  
7603     >>> sc.pdtr([1, 1.5, 1.9, 2], 1) 
7604     array([0.73575888, 0.73575888, 0.73575888, 0.9196986 ]) 
7605  
7606     """)
7607 
7608 add_newdoc("pdtrc",
7609     """ 
7610     pdtrc(k, m, out=None) 
7611  
7612     Poisson survival function 
7613  
7614     Returns the sum of the terms from k+1 to infinity of the Poisson 
7615     distribution: sum(exp(-m) * m**j / j!, j=k+1..inf) = gammainc( 
7616     k+1, m). Arguments must both be non-negative doubles. 
7617  
7618     Parameters 
7619     ---------- 
7620     k : array_like 
7621         Number of occurrences (nonnegative, real) 
7622     m : array_like 
7623         Shape parameter (nonnegative, real) 
7624     out : ndarray, optional 
7625         Optional output array for the function results 
7626  
7627     Returns 
7628     ------- 
7629     scalar or ndarray 
7630         Values of the Poisson survival function 
7631  
7632     See Also 
7633     -------- 
7634     pdtr : Poisson cumulative distribution function 
7635     pdtrik : inverse of `pdtr` with respect to `k` 
7636     pdtri : inverse of `pdtr` with respect to `m` 
7637  
7638     Examples 
7639     -------- 
7640     >>> import numpy as np 
7641     >>> import scipy.special as sc 
7642  
7643     It is a survival function, so it decreases to 0 
7644     monotonically as `k` goes to infinity. 
7645  
7646     >>> k = np.array([1, 10, 100, np.inf]) 
7647     >>> sc.pdtrc(k, 1) 
7648     array([2.64241118e-001, 1.00477664e-008, 3.94147589e-161, 0.00000000e+000]) 
7649  
7650     It can be expressed in terms of the lower incomplete gamma 
7651     function `gammainc`. 
7652  
7653     >>> sc.gammainc(k + 1, 1) 
7654     array([2.64241118e-001, 1.00477664e-008, 3.94147589e-161, 0.00000000e+000]) 
7655  
7656     """)

7665     the Poisson density is equal to the given probability `y`:

7667     integer and `y` between 0 and 1.

7751 add_newdoc("poch",
7752     r""" 
7753     poch(z, m, out=None) 
7754  
7755     Pochhammer symbol. 
7756  
7757     The Pochhammer symbol (rising factorial) is defined as 
7758  
7759     .. math:: 
7760  
7761         (z)_m = \frac{\Gamma(z + m)}{\Gamma(z)} 
7762  
7763     For positive integer `m` it reads 
7764  
7765     .. math:: 
7766  
7767         (z)_m = z (z + 1) ... (z + m - 1) 
7768  
7769     See [dlmf]_ for more details. 
7770  
7771     Parameters 
7772     ---------- 
7773     z, m : array_like 
7774         Real-valued arguments. 
7775     out : ndarray, optional 
7776         Optional output array for the function results 
7777  
7778     Returns 
7779     ------- 
7780     scalar or ndarray 
7781         The value of the function. 
7782  
7783     References 
7784     ---------- 
7785     .. [dlmf] Nist, Digital Library of Mathematical Functions 
7786         https://dlmf.nist.gov/5.2#iii 
7787  
7788     Examples 
7789     -------- 
7790     >>> import scipy.special as sc 
7791  
7792     It is 1 when m is 0. 
7793  
7794     >>> sc.poch([1, 2, 3, 4], 0) 
7795     array([1., 1., 1., 1.]) 
7796  
7797     For z equal to 1 it reduces to the factorial function. 
7798  
7799     >>> sc.poch(1, 5) 
7800     120.0 
7801     >>> 1 * 2 * 3 * 4 * 5 
7802     120 
7803  
7804     It can be expressed in terms of the gamma function. 
7805  
7806     >>> z, m = 3.7, 2.1 
7807     >>> sc.poch(z, m) 
7808     20.529581933776953 
7809     >>> sc.gamma(z + m) / sc.gamma(z) 
7810     20.52958193377696 
7811  
7812     """)
7813 
7814 add_newdoc("powm1", """ 
7815     powm1(x, y, out=None) 
7816  
7817     Computes ``x**y - 1``. 
7818  
7819     This function is useful when `y` is near 0, or when `x` is near 1. 
7820  
7821     The function is implemented for real types only (unlike ``numpy.power``, 
7822     which accepts complex inputs). 
7823  
7824     Parameters 
7825     ---------- 
7826     x : array_like 
7827         The base. Must be a real type (i.e. integer or float, not complex). 
7828     y : array_like 
7829         The exponent. Must be a real type (i.e. integer or float, not complex). 
7830  
7831     Returns 
7832     ------- 
7833     array_like 
7834         Result of the calculation 
7835  
7836     Notes 
7837     ----- 
7838     .. versionadded:: 1.10.0 
7839  
7840     The underlying code is implemented for single precision and double 
7841     precision floats only.  Unlike `numpy.power`, integer inputs to 
7842     `powm1` are converted to floating point, and complex inputs are 
7843     not accepted. 
7844  
7845     Note the following edge cases: 
7846  
7847     * ``powm1(x, 0)`` returns 0 for any ``x``, including 0, ``inf`` 
7848       and ``nan``. 
7849     * ``powm1(1, y)`` returns 0 for any ``y``, including ``nan`` 
7850       and ``inf``. 
7851  
7852     This function wraps the ``powm1`` routine from the 
7853     Boost Math C++ library [1]_. 
7854  
7855     References 
7856     ---------- 
7857     .. [1] The Boost Developers. "Boost C++ Libraries". https://www.boost.org/. 
7858  
7859     Examples 
7860     -------- 
7861     >>> import numpy as np 
7862     >>> from scipy.special import powm1 
7863  
7864     >>> x = np.array([1.2, 10.0, 0.9999999975]) 
7865     >>> y = np.array([1e-9, 1e-11, 0.1875]) 
7866     >>> powm1(x, y) 
7867     array([ 1.82321557e-10,  2.30258509e-11, -4.68749998e-10]) 
7868  
7869     It can be verified that the relative errors in those results 
7870     are less than 2.5e-16. 
7871  
7872     Compare that to the result of ``x**y - 1``, where the 
7873     relative errors are all larger than 8e-8: 
7874  
7875     >>> x**y - 1 
7876     array([ 1.82321491e-10,  2.30258035e-11, -4.68750039e-10]) 
7877  
7878     """)
7879

7881 add_newdoc("pseudo_huber",
7882     r""" 
7883     pseudo_huber(delta, r, out=None) 
7884  
7885     Pseudo-Huber loss function. 
7886  
7887     .. math:: \mathrm{pseudo\_huber}(\delta, r) = 
7888               \delta^2 \left( \sqrt{ 1 + \left( \frac{r}{\delta} \right)^2 } - 1 \right) 
7889  
7890     Parameters 
7891     ---------- 
7892     delta : array_like 
7893         Input array, indicating the soft quadratic vs. linear loss changepoint. 
7894     r : array_like 
7895         Input array, possibly representing residuals. 
7896     out : ndarray, optional 
7897         Optional output array for the function results 
7898  
7899     Returns 
7900     ------- 
7901     res : scalar or ndarray 
7902         The computed Pseudo-Huber loss function values. 
7903  
7904     See Also 
7905     -------- 
7906     huber: Similar function which this function approximates 
7907  
7908     Notes 
7909     ----- 
7910     Like `huber`, `pseudo_huber` often serves as a robust loss function 
7911     in statistics or machine learning to reduce the influence of outliers. 
7912     Unlike `huber`, `pseudo_huber` is smooth. 
7913  
7914     Typically, `r` represents residuals, the difference 
7915     between a model prediction and data. Then, for :math:`|r|\leq\delta`, 
7916     `pseudo_huber` resembles the squared error and for :math:`|r|>\delta` the 
7917     absolute error. This way, the Pseudo-Huber loss often achieves 
7918     a fast convergence in model fitting for small residuals like the squared 
7919     error loss function and still reduces the influence of outliers 
7920     (:math:`|r|>\delta`) like the absolute error loss. As :math:`\delta` is 
7921     the cutoff between squared and absolute error regimes, it has 
7922     to be tuned carefully for each problem. `pseudo_huber` is also 
7923     convex, making it suitable for gradient based optimization. [1]_ [2]_ 
7924  
7925     .. versionadded:: 0.15.0 
7926  
7927     References 
7928     ---------- 
7929     .. [1] Hartley, Zisserman, "Multiple View Geometry in Computer Vision". 
7930            2003. Cambridge University Press. p. 619 
7931     .. [2] Charbonnier et al. "Deterministic edge-preserving regularization 
7932            in computed imaging". 1997. IEEE Trans. Image Processing. 
7933            6 (2): 298 - 311. 
7934  
7935     Examples 
7936     -------- 
7937     Import all necessary modules. 
7938  
7939     >>> import numpy as np 
7940     >>> from scipy.special import pseudo_huber, huber 
7941     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
7942  
7943     Calculate the function for ``delta=1`` at ``r=2``. 
7944  
7945     >>> pseudo_huber(1., 2.) 
7946     1.2360679774997898 
7947  
7948     Calculate the function at ``r=2`` for different `delta` by providing 
7949     a list or NumPy array for `delta`. 
7950  
7951     >>> pseudo_huber([1., 2., 4.], 3.) 
7952     array([2.16227766, 3.21110255, 4.        ]) 
7953  
7954     Calculate the function for ``delta=1`` at several points by providing 
7955     a list or NumPy array for `r`. 
7956  
7957     >>> pseudo_huber(2., np.array([1., 1.5, 3., 4.])) 
7958     array([0.47213595, 1.        , 3.21110255, 4.94427191]) 
7959  
7960     The function can be calculated for different `delta` and `r` by 
7961     providing arrays for both with compatible shapes for broadcasting. 
7962  
7963     >>> r = np.array([1., 2.5, 8., 10.]) 
7964     >>> deltas = np.array([[1.], [5.], [9.]]) 
7965     >>> print(r.shape, deltas.shape) 
7966     (4,) (3, 1) 
7967  
7968     >>> pseudo_huber(deltas, r) 
7969     array([[ 0.41421356,  1.6925824 ,  7.06225775,  9.04987562], 
7970            [ 0.49509757,  2.95084972, 22.16990566, 30.90169944], 
7971            [ 0.49846624,  3.06693762, 27.37435121, 40.08261642]]) 
7972  
7973     Plot the function for different `delta`. 
7974  
7975     >>> x = np.linspace(-4, 4, 500) 
7976     >>> deltas = [1, 2, 3] 
7977     >>> linestyles = ["dashed", "dotted", "dashdot"] 
7978     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
7979     >>> combined_plot_parameters = list(zip(deltas, linestyles)) 
7980     >>> for delta, style in combined_plot_parameters: 
7981     ...     ax.plot(x, pseudo_huber(delta, x), label=rf"$\delta={delta}$", 
7982     ...             ls=style) 
7983     >>> ax.legend(loc="upper center") 
7984     >>> ax.set_xlabel("$x$") 
7985     >>> ax.set_title(r"Pseudo-Huber loss function $h_{\delta}(x)$") 
7986     >>> ax.set_xlim(-4, 4) 
7987     >>> ax.set_ylim(0, 8) 
7988     >>> plt.show() 
7989  
7990     Finally, illustrate the difference between `huber` and `pseudo_huber` by 
7991     plotting them and their gradients with respect to `r`. The plot shows 
7992     that `pseudo_huber` is continuously differentiable while `huber` is not 
7993     at the points :math:`\pm\delta`. 
7994  
7995     >>> def huber_grad(delta, x): 
7996     ...     grad = np.copy(x) 
7997     ...     linear_area = np.argwhere(np.abs(x) > delta) 
7998     ...     grad[linear_area]=delta*np.sign(x[linear_area]) 
7999     ...     return grad 
8000     >>> def pseudo_huber_grad(delta, x): 
8001     ...     return x* (1+(x/delta)**2)**(-0.5) 
8002     >>> x=np.linspace(-3, 3, 500) 
8003     >>> delta = 1. 
8004     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(7, 7)) 
8005     >>> ax.plot(x, huber(delta, x), label="Huber", ls="dashed") 
8006     >>> ax.plot(x, huber_grad(delta, x), label="Huber Gradient", ls="dashdot") 
8007     >>> ax.plot(x, pseudo_huber(delta, x), label="Pseudo-Huber", ls="dotted") 
8008     >>> ax.plot(x, pseudo_huber_grad(delta, x), label="Pseudo-Huber Gradient", 
8009     ...         ls="solid") 
8010     >>> ax.legend(loc="upper center") 
8011     >>> plt.show() 
8012     """)

8014 add_newdoc("rel_entr",
8015     r""" 
8016     rel_entr(x, y, out=None) 
8017  
8018     Elementwise function for computing relative entropy. 
8019  
8020     .. math:: 
8021  
8022         \mathrm{rel\_entr}(x, y) = 
8023             \begin{cases} 
8024                 x \log(x / y) & x > 0, y > 0 \\ 
8025                 0 & x = 0, y \ge 0 \\ 
8026                 \infty & \text{otherwise} 
8027             \end{cases} 
8028  
8029     Parameters 
8030     ---------- 
8031     x, y : array_like 
8032         Input arrays 
8033     out : ndarray, optional 
8034         Optional output array for the function results 
8035  
8036     Returns 
8037     ------- 
8038     scalar or ndarray 
8039         Relative entropy of the inputs 
8040  
8041     See Also 
8042     -------- 
8043     entr, kl_div, scipy.stats.entropy 
8044  
8045     Notes 
8046     ----- 
8047     .. versionadded:: 0.15.0 
8048  
8049     This function is jointly convex in x and y. 
8050  
8051     The origin of this function is in convex programming; see 
8052     [1]_. Given two discrete probability distributions :math:`p_1, 
8053     \ldots, p_n` and :math:`q_1, \ldots, q_n`, the definition of relative 
8054     entropy in the context of *information theory* is 
8055  
8056     .. math:: 
8057  
8058         \sum_{i = 1}^n \mathrm{rel\_entr}(p_i, q_i). 
8059  
8060     To compute the latter quantity, use `scipy.stats.entropy`. 
8061  
8062     See [2]_ for details. 
8063  
8064     References 
8065     ---------- 
8066     .. [1] Boyd, Stephen and Lieven Vandenberghe. *Convex optimization*. 
8067            Cambridge University Press, 2004. 
8068            :doi:`https://doi.org/10.1017/CBO9780511804441` 
8069     .. [2] Kullback-Leibler divergence, 
8070            https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kullback%E2%80%93Leibler_divergence 
8071  
8072     """)
8073

8088         Optional output array for the function results. 
8089  
8090     Returns

8092     scalar or ndarray 
8093         The nearest integers to the elements of `x`. The result is of 
8094         floating type, not integer type. 
8095  
8096     Examples

8107 add_newdoc("shichi",
8108     r""" 
8109     shichi(x, out=None) 
8110  
8111     Hyperbolic sine and cosine integrals. 
8112  
8113     The hyperbolic sine integral is 
8114  
8115     .. math:: 
8116  
8117       \int_0^x \frac{\sinh{t}}{t}dt 
8118  
8119     and the hyperbolic cosine integral is 
8120  
8121     .. math:: 
8122  
8123       \gamma + \log(x) + \int_0^x \frac{\cosh{t} - 1}{t} dt 
8124  
8125     where :math:`\gamma` is Euler's constant and :math:`\log` is the 
8126     principal branch of the logarithm [1]_. 
8127  
8128     Parameters 
8129     ---------- 
8130     x : array_like 
8131         Real or complex points at which to compute the hyperbolic sine 
8132         and cosine integrals. 
8133     out : tuple of ndarray, optional 
8134         Optional output arrays for the function results 
8135  
8136     Returns 
8137     ------- 
8138     si : scalar or ndarray 
8139         Hyperbolic sine integral at ``x`` 
8140     ci : scalar or ndarray 
8141         Hyperbolic cosine integral at ``x`` 
8142  
8143     See Also 
8144     -------- 
8145     sici : Sine and cosine integrals. 
8146     exp1 : Exponential integral E1. 
8147     expi : Exponential integral Ei. 
8148  
8149     Notes 
8150     ----- 
8151     For real arguments with ``x < 0``, ``chi`` is the real part of the 
8152     hyperbolic cosine integral. For such points ``chi(x)`` and ``chi(x 
8153     + 0j)`` differ by a factor of ``1j*pi``. 
8154  
8155     For real arguments the function is computed by calling Cephes' 
8156     [2]_ *shichi* routine. For complex arguments the algorithm is based 
8157     on Mpmath's [3]_ *shi* and *chi* routines. 
8158  
8159     References 
8160     ---------- 
8161     .. [1] Milton Abramowitz and Irene A. Stegun, eds. 
8162            Handbook of Mathematical Functions with Formulas, 
8163            Graphs, and Mathematical Tables. New York: Dover, 1972. 
8164            (See Section 5.2.) 
8165     .. [2] Cephes Mathematical Functions Library, 
8166            http://www.netlib.org/cephes/ 
8167     .. [3] Fredrik Johansson and others. 
8168            "mpmath: a Python library for arbitrary-precision floating-point 
8169            arithmetic" (Version 0.19) http://mpmath.org/ 
8170  
8171     Examples 
8172     -------- 
8173     >>> import numpy as np 
8174     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
8175     >>> from scipy.special import shichi, sici 
8176  
8177     `shichi` accepts real or complex input: 
8178  
8179     >>> shichi(0.5) 
8180     (0.5069967498196671, -0.05277684495649357) 
8181     >>> shichi(0.5 + 2.5j) 
8182     ((0.11772029666668238+1.831091777729851j), 
8183      (0.29912435887648825+1.7395351121166562j)) 
8184  
8185     The hyperbolic sine and cosine integrals Shi(z) and Chi(z) are 
8186     related to the sine and cosine integrals Si(z) and Ci(z) by 
8187  
8188     * Shi(z) = -i*Si(i*z) 
8189     * Chi(z) = Ci(-i*z) + i*pi/2 
8190  
8191     >>> z = 0.25 + 5j 
8192     >>> shi, chi = shichi(z) 
8193     >>> shi, -1j*sici(1j*z)[0]            # Should be the same. 
8194     ((-0.04834719325101729+1.5469354086921228j), 
8195      (-0.04834719325101729+1.5469354086921228j)) 
8196     >>> chi, sici(-1j*z)[1] + 1j*np.pi/2  # Should be the same. 
8197     ((-0.19568708973868087+1.556276312103824j), 
8198      (-0.19568708973868087+1.556276312103824j)) 
8199  
8200     Plot the functions evaluated on the real axis: 
8201  
8202     >>> xp = np.geomspace(1e-8, 4.0, 250) 
8203     >>> x = np.concatenate((-xp[::-1], xp)) 
8204     >>> shi, chi = shichi(x) 
8205  
8206     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
8207     >>> ax.plot(x, shi, label='Shi(x)') 
8208     >>> ax.plot(x, chi, '--', label='Chi(x)') 
8209     >>> ax.set_xlabel('x') 
8210     >>> ax.set_title('Hyperbolic Sine and Cosine Integrals') 
8211     >>> ax.legend(shadow=True, framealpha=1, loc='lower right') 
8212     >>> ax.grid(True) 
8213     >>> plt.show() 
8214  
8215     """)
8216 
8217 add_newdoc("sici",
8218     r""" 
8219     sici(x, out=None) 
8220  
8221     Sine and cosine integrals. 
8222  
8223     The sine integral is 
8224  
8225     .. math:: 
8226  
8227       \int_0^x \frac{\sin{t}}{t}dt 
8228  
8229     and the cosine integral is 
8230  
8231     .. math:: 
8232  
8233       \gamma + \log(x) + \int_0^x \frac{\cos{t} - 1}{t}dt 
8234  
8235     where :math:`\gamma` is Euler's constant and :math:`\log` is the 
8236     principal branch of the logarithm [1]_. 
8237  
8238     Parameters 
8239     ---------- 
8240     x : array_like 
8241         Real or complex points at which to compute the sine and cosine 
8242         integrals. 
8243     out : tuple of ndarray, optional 
8244         Optional output arrays for the function results 
8245  
8246     Returns 
8247     ------- 
8248     si : scalar or ndarray 
8249         Sine integral at ``x`` 
8250     ci : scalar or ndarray 
8251         Cosine integral at ``x`` 
8252  
8253     See Also 
8254     -------- 
8255     shichi : Hyperbolic sine and cosine integrals. 
8256     exp1 : Exponential integral E1. 
8257     expi : Exponential integral Ei. 
8258  
8259     Notes 
8260     ----- 
8261     For real arguments with ``x < 0``, ``ci`` is the real part of the 
8262     cosine integral. For such points ``ci(x)`` and ``ci(x + 0j)`` 
8263     differ by a factor of ``1j*pi``. 
8264  
8265     For real arguments the function is computed by calling Cephes' 
8266     [2]_ *sici* routine. For complex arguments the algorithm is based 
8267     on Mpmath's [3]_ *si* and *ci* routines. 
8268  
8269     References 
8270     ---------- 
8271     .. [1] Milton Abramowitz and Irene A. Stegun, eds. 
8272            Handbook of Mathematical Functions with Formulas, 
8273            Graphs, and Mathematical Tables. New York: Dover, 1972. 
8274            (See Section 5.2.) 
8275     .. [2] Cephes Mathematical Functions Library, 
8276            http://www.netlib.org/cephes/ 
8277     .. [3] Fredrik Johansson and others. 
8278            "mpmath: a Python library for arbitrary-precision floating-point 
8279            arithmetic" (Version 0.19) http://mpmath.org/ 
8280  
8281     Examples 
8282     -------- 
8283     >>> import numpy as np 
8284     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
8285     >>> from scipy.special import sici, exp1 
8286  
8287     `sici` accepts real or complex input: 
8288  
8289     >>> sici(2.5) 
8290     (1.7785201734438267, 0.2858711963653835) 
8291     >>> sici(2.5 + 3j) 
8292     ((4.505735874563953+0.06863305018999577j), 
8293     (0.0793644206906966-2.935510262937543j)) 
8294  
8295     For z in the right half plane, the sine and cosine integrals are 
8296     related to the exponential integral E1 (implemented in SciPy as 
8297     `scipy.special.exp1`) by 
8298  
8299     * Si(z) = (E1(i*z) - E1(-i*z))/2i + pi/2 
8300     * Ci(z) = -(E1(i*z) + E1(-i*z))/2 
8301  
8302     See [1]_ (equations 5.2.21 and 5.2.23). 
8303  
8304     We can verify these relations: 
8305  
8306     >>> z = 2 - 3j 
8307     >>> sici(z) 
8308     ((4.54751388956229-1.3991965806460565j), 
8309     (1.408292501520851+2.9836177420296055j)) 
8310  
8311     >>> (exp1(1j*z) - exp1(-1j*z))/2j + np.pi/2  # Same as sine integral 
8312     (4.54751388956229-1.3991965806460565j) 
8313  
8314     >>> -(exp1(1j*z) + exp1(-1j*z))/2            # Same as cosine integral 
8315     (1.408292501520851+2.9836177420296055j) 
8316  
8317     Plot the functions evaluated on the real axis; the dotted horizontal 
8318     lines are at pi/2 and -pi/2: 
8319  
8320     >>> x = np.linspace(-16, 16, 150) 
8321     >>> si, ci = sici(x) 
8322  
8323     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
8324     >>> ax.plot(x, si, label='Si(x)') 
8325     >>> ax.plot(x, ci, '--', label='Ci(x)') 
8326     >>> ax.legend(shadow=True, framealpha=1, loc='upper left') 
8327     >>> ax.set_xlabel('x') 
8328     >>> ax.set_title('Sine and Cosine Integrals') 
8329     >>> ax.axhline(np.pi/2, linestyle=':', alpha=0.5, color='k') 
8330     >>> ax.axhline(-np.pi/2, linestyle=':', alpha=0.5, color='k') 
8331     >>> ax.grid(True) 
8332     >>> plt.show() 
8333  
8334     """)
8335 
8336 add_newdoc("smirnov",
8337     r""" 
8338     smirnov(n, d, out=None) 
8339  
8340     Kolmogorov-Smirnov complementary cumulative distribution function 
8341  
8342     Returns the exact Kolmogorov-Smirnov complementary cumulative 
8343     distribution function,(aka the Survival Function) of Dn+ (or Dn-) 
8344     for a one-sided test of equality between an empirical and a 
8345     theoretical distribution. It is equal to the probability that the 
8346     maximum difference between a theoretical distribution and an empirical 
8347     one based on `n` samples is greater than d. 
8348  
8349     Parameters 
8350     ---------- 
8351     n : int 
8352       Number of samples 
8353     d : float array_like 
8354       Deviation between the Empirical CDF (ECDF) and the target CDF. 
8355     out : ndarray, optional 
8356         Optional output array for the function results 
8357  
8358     Returns 
8359     ------- 
8360     scalar or ndarray 
8361         The value(s) of smirnov(n, d), Prob(Dn+ >= d) (Also Prob(Dn- >= d)) 
8362  
8363     See Also 
8364     -------- 
8365     smirnovi : The Inverse Survival Function for the distribution 
8366     scipy.stats.ksone : Provides the functionality as a continuous distribution 
8367     kolmogorov, kolmogi : Functions for the two-sided distribution 
8368  
8369     Notes 
8370     ----- 
8371     `smirnov` is used by `stats.kstest` in the application of the 
8372     Kolmogorov-Smirnov Goodness of Fit test. For historical reasons this 
8373     function is exposed in `scpy.special`, but the recommended way to achieve 
8374     the most accurate CDF/SF/PDF/PPF/ISF computations is to use the 
8375     `stats.ksone` distribution. 
8376  
8377     Examples 
8378     -------- 
8379     >>> import numpy as np 
8380     >>> from scipy.special import smirnov 
8381     >>> from scipy.stats import norm 
8382  
8383     Show the probability of a gap at least as big as 0, 0.5 and 1.0 for a 
8384     sample of size 5. 
8385  
8386     >>> smirnov(5, [0, 0.5, 1.0]) 
8387     array([ 1.   ,  0.056,  0.   ]) 
8388  
8389     Compare a sample of size 5 against N(0, 1), the standard normal 
8390     distribution with mean 0 and standard deviation 1. 
8391  
8392     `x` is the sample. 
8393  
8394     >>> x = np.array([-1.392, -0.135, 0.114, 0.190, 1.82]) 
8395  
8396     >>> target = norm(0, 1) 
8397     >>> cdfs = target.cdf(x) 
8398     >>> cdfs 
8399     array([0.0819612 , 0.44630594, 0.5453811 , 0.57534543, 0.9656205 ]) 
8400  
8401     Construct the empirical CDF and the K-S statistics (Dn+, Dn-, Dn). 
8402  
8403     >>> n = len(x) 
8404     >>> ecdfs = np.arange(n+1, dtype=float)/n 
8405     >>> cols = np.column_stack([x, ecdfs[1:], cdfs, cdfs - ecdfs[:n], 
8406     ...                        ecdfs[1:] - cdfs]) 
8407     >>> with np.printoptions(precision=3): 
8408     ...    print(cols) 
8409     [[-1.392  0.2    0.082  0.082  0.118] 
8410      [-0.135  0.4    0.446  0.246 -0.046] 
8411      [ 0.114  0.6    0.545  0.145  0.055] 
8412      [ 0.19   0.8    0.575 -0.025  0.225] 
8413      [ 1.82   1.     0.966  0.166  0.034]] 
8414     >>> gaps = cols[:, -2:] 
8415     >>> Dnpm = np.max(gaps, axis=0) 
8416     >>> print(f'Dn-={Dnpm[0]:f}, Dn+={Dnpm[1]:f}') 
8417     Dn-=0.246306, Dn+=0.224655 
8418     >>> probs = smirnov(n, Dnpm) 
8419     >>> print(f'For a sample of size {n} drawn from N(0, 1):', 
8420     ...       f' Smirnov n={n}: Prob(Dn- >= {Dnpm[0]:f}) = {probs[0]:.4f}', 
8421     ...       f' Smirnov n={n}: Prob(Dn+ >= {Dnpm[1]:f}) = {probs[1]:.4f}', 
8422     ...       sep='\n') 
8423     For a sample of size 5 drawn from N(0, 1): 
8424      Smirnov n=5: Prob(Dn- >= 0.246306) = 0.4711 
8425      Smirnov n=5: Prob(Dn+ >= 0.224655) = 0.5245 
8426  
8427     Plot the empirical CDF and the standard normal CDF. 
8428  
8429     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
8430     >>> plt.step(np.concatenate(([-2.5], x, [2.5])), 
8431     ...          np.concatenate((ecdfs, [1])), 
8432     ...          where='post', label='Empirical CDF') 
8433     >>> xx = np.linspace(-2.5, 2.5, 100) 
8434     >>> plt.plot(xx, target.cdf(xx), '--', label='CDF for N(0, 1)') 
8435  
8436     Add vertical lines marking Dn+ and Dn-. 
8437  
8438     >>> iminus, iplus = np.argmax(gaps, axis=0) 
8439     >>> plt.vlines([x[iminus]], ecdfs[iminus], cdfs[iminus], color='r', 
8440     ...            alpha=0.5, lw=4) 
8441     >>> plt.vlines([x[iplus]], cdfs[iplus], ecdfs[iplus+1], color='m', 
8442     ...            alpha=0.5, lw=4) 
8443  
8444     >>> plt.grid(True) 
8445     >>> plt.legend(framealpha=1, shadow=True) 
8446     >>> plt.show() 
8447     """)
8448

8452  
8453     Inverse to `smirnov` 
8454  
8455     Returns `d` such that ``smirnov(n, d) == p``, the critical value 
8456     corresponding to `p`. 
8457  
8458     Parameters 
8459     ----------

8462     p : float array_like

8475     scipy.stats.ksone : Provides the functionality as a continuous distribution 
8476     kolmogorov, kolmogi : Functions for the two-sided distribution 
8477     scipy.stats.kstwobign : Two-sided Kolmogorov-Smirnov distribution, large n 
8478  
8479     Notes 
8480     ----- 
8481     `smirnov` is used by `stats.kstest` in the application of the 
8482     Kolmogorov-Smirnov Goodness of Fit test. For historical reasons this 
8483     function is exposed in `scpy.special`, but the recommended way to achieve 
8484     the most accurate CDF/SF/PDF/PPF/ISF computations is to use the 
8485     `stats.ksone` distribution. 
8486  
8487     Examples 
8488     --------

8490  
8491     >>> n = 24 
8492     >>> deviations = [0.1, 0.2, 0.3]

8495     distribution for the given number of samples and deviations. 
8496  
8497     >>> p = smirnov(n, deviations) 
8498     >>> p 
8499     array([0.58105083, 0.12826832, 0.01032231]) 
8500  
8501     The inverse function ``smirnovi(n, p)`` returns ``deviations``. 
8502  
8503     >>> smirnovi(n, p) 
8504     array([0.1, 0.2, 0.3]) 
8505  
8506     """)

8525 add_newdoc("spence",
8526     r""" 
8527     spence(z, out=None) 
8528  
8529     Spence's function, also known as the dilogarithm. 
8530  
8531     It is defined to be 
8532  
8533     .. math:: 
8534       \int_1^z \frac{\log(t)}{1 - t}dt 
8535  
8536     for complex :math:`z`, where the contour of integration is taken 
8537     to avoid the branch cut of the logarithm. Spence's function is 
8538     analytic everywhere except the negative real axis where it has a 
8539     branch cut. 
8540  
8541     Parameters 
8542     ---------- 
8543     z : array_like 
8544         Points at which to evaluate Spence's function 
8545     out : ndarray, optional 
8546         Optional output array for the function results 
8547  
8548     Returns 
8549     ------- 
8550     s : scalar or ndarray 
8551         Computed values of Spence's function 
8552  
8553     Notes 
8554     ----- 
8555     There is a different convention which defines Spence's function by 
8556     the integral 
8557  
8558     .. math:: 
8559       -\int_0^z \frac{\log(1 - t)}{t}dt; 
8560  
8561     this is our ``spence(1 - z)``. 
8562  
8563     Examples 
8564     -------- 
8565     >>> import numpy as np 
8566     >>> from scipy.special import spence 
8567     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
8568  
8569     The function is defined for complex inputs: 
8570  
8571     >>> spence([1-1j, 1.5+2j, 3j, -10-5j]) 
8572     array([-0.20561676+0.91596559j, -0.86766909-1.39560134j, 
8573            -0.59422064-2.49129918j, -1.14044398+6.80075924j]) 
8574  
8575     For complex inputs on the branch cut, which is the negative real axis, 
8576     the function returns the limit for ``z`` with positive imaginary part. 
8577     For example, in the following, note the sign change of the imaginary 
8578     part of the output for ``z = -2`` and ``z = -2 - 1e-8j``: 
8579  
8580     >>> spence([-2 + 1e-8j, -2, -2 - 1e-8j]) 
8581     array([2.32018041-3.45139229j, 2.32018042-3.4513923j , 
8582            2.32018041+3.45139229j]) 
8583  
8584     The function returns ``nan`` for real inputs on the branch cut: 
8585  
8586     >>> spence(-1.5) 
8587     nan 
8588  
8589     Verify some particular values: ``spence(0) = pi**2/6``, 
8590     ``spence(1) = 0`` and ``spence(2) = -pi**2/12``. 
8591  
8592     >>> spence([0, 1, 2]) 
8593     array([ 1.64493407,  0.        , -0.82246703]) 
8594     >>> np.pi**2/6, -np.pi**2/12 
8595     (1.6449340668482264, -0.8224670334241132) 
8596  
8597     Verify the identity:: 
8598  
8599         spence(z) + spence(1 - z) = pi**2/6 - log(z)*log(1 - z) 
8600  
8601     >>> z = 3 + 4j 
8602     >>> spence(z) + spence(1 - z) 
8603     (-2.6523186143876067+1.8853470951513935j) 
8604     >>> np.pi**2/6 - np.log(z)*np.log(1 - z) 
8605     (-2.652318614387606+1.885347095151394j) 
8606  
8607     Plot the function for positive real input. 
8608  
8609     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
8610     >>> x = np.linspace(0, 6, 400) 
8611     >>> ax.plot(x, spence(x)) 
8612     >>> ax.grid() 
8613     >>> ax.set_xlabel('x') 
8614     >>> ax.set_title('spence(x)') 
8615     >>> plt.show() 
8616     """)

8618 add_newdoc(
8619     "stdtr",
8620     r""" 
8621     stdtr(df, t, out=None) 
8622  
8623     Student t distribution cumulative distribution function 
8624  
8625     Returns the integral: 
8626  
8627     .. math:: 
8628         \frac{\Gamma((df+1)/2)}{\sqrt{\pi df} \Gamma(df/2)} 
8629         \int_{-\infty}^t (1+x^2/df)^{-(df+1)/2}\, dx 
8630  
8631     Parameters 
8632     ---------- 
8633     df : array_like 
8634         Degrees of freedom 
8635     t : array_like 
8636         Upper bound of the integral 
8637     out : ndarray, optional 
8638         Optional output array for the function results 
8639  
8640     Returns 
8641     ------- 
8642     scalar or ndarray 
8643         Value of the Student t CDF at t 
8644  
8645     See Also 
8646     -------- 
8647     stdtridf : inverse of stdtr with respect to `df` 
8648     stdtrit : inverse of stdtr with respect to `t` 
8649     scipy.stats.t : student t distribution 
8650  
8651     Notes 
8652     ----- 
8653     The student t distribution is also available as `scipy.stats.t`. 
8654     Calling `stdtr` directly can improve performance compared to the 
8655     ``cdf`` method of `scipy.stats.t` (see last example below). 
8656  
8657     Examples 
8658     -------- 
8659     Calculate the function for ``df=3`` at ``t=1``. 
8660  
8661     >>> import numpy as np 
8662     >>> from scipy.special import stdtr 
8663     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
8664     >>> stdtr(3, 1) 
8665     0.8044988905221148 
8666  
8667     Plot the function for three different degrees of freedom. 
8668  
8669     >>> x = np.linspace(-10, 10, 1000) 
8670     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
8671     >>> parameters = [(1, "solid"), (3, "dashed"), (10, "dotted")] 
8672     >>> for (df, linestyle) in parameters: 
8673     ...     ax.plot(x, stdtr(df, x), ls=linestyle, label=f"$df={df}$") 
8674     >>> ax.legend() 
8675     >>> ax.set_title("Student t distribution cumulative distribution function") 
8676     >>> plt.show() 
8677  
8678     The function can be computed for several degrees of freedom at the same 
8679     time by providing a NumPy array or list for `df`: 
8680  
8681     >>> stdtr([1, 2, 3], 1) 
8682     array([0.75      , 0.78867513, 0.80449889]) 
8683  
8684     It is possible to calculate the function at several points for several 
8685     different degrees of freedom simultaneously by providing arrays for `df` 
8686     and `t` with shapes compatible for broadcasting. Compute `stdtr` at 
8687     4 points for 3 degrees of freedom resulting in an array of shape 3x4. 
8688  
8689     >>> dfs = np.array([[1], [2], [3]]) 
8690     >>> t = np.array([2, 4, 6, 8]) 
8691     >>> dfs.shape, t.shape 
8692     ((3, 1), (4,)) 
8693  
8694     >>> stdtr(dfs, t) 
8695     array([[0.85241638, 0.92202087, 0.94743154, 0.96041658], 
8696            [0.90824829, 0.97140452, 0.98666426, 0.99236596], 
8697            [0.93033702, 0.98599577, 0.99536364, 0.99796171]]) 
8698  
8699     The t distribution is also available as `scipy.stats.t`. Calling `stdtr` 
8700     directly can be much faster than calling the ``cdf`` method of 
8701     `scipy.stats.t`. To get the same results, one must use the following 
8702     parametrization: ``scipy.stats.t(df).cdf(x) = stdtr(df, x)``. 
8703  
8704     >>> from scipy.stats import t 
8705     >>> df, x = 3, 1 
8706     >>> stdtr_result = stdtr(df, x)  # this can be faster than below 
8707     >>> stats_result = t(df).cdf(x) 
8708     >>> stats_result == stdtr_result  # test that results are equal 
8709     True 
8710     """)
8711 
8712 add_newdoc("stdtridf",
8713     """ 
8714     stdtridf(p, t, out=None) 
8715  
8716     Inverse of `stdtr` vs df 
8717  
8718     Returns the argument df such that stdtr(df, t) is equal to `p`. 
8719  
8720     Parameters 
8721     ---------- 
8722     p : array_like 
8723         Probability 
8724     t : array_like 
8725         Upper bound of the integral 
8726     out : ndarray, optional 
8727         Optional output array for the function results 
8728  
8729     Returns 
8730     ------- 
8731     df : scalar or ndarray 
8732         Value of `df` such that ``stdtr(df, t) == p`` 
8733  
8734     See Also 
8735     -------- 
8736     stdtr : Student t CDF 
8737     stdtrit : inverse of stdtr with respect to `t` 
8738     scipy.stats.t : Student t distribution 
8739  
8740     Examples 
8741     -------- 
8742     Compute the student t cumulative distribution function for one 
8743     parameter set. 
8744  
8745     >>> from scipy.special import stdtr, stdtridf 
8746     >>> df, x = 5, 2 
8747     >>> cdf_value = stdtr(df, x) 
8748     >>> cdf_value 
8749     0.9490302605850709 
8750  
8751     Verify that `stdtridf` recovers the original value for `df` given 
8752     the CDF value and `x`. 
8753  
8754     >>> stdtridf(cdf_value, x) 
8755     5.0 
8756     """)

8758 add_newdoc("stdtrit",
8759     """ 
8760     stdtrit(df, p, out=None) 
8761  
8762     The `p`-th quantile of the student t distribution. 
8763  
8764     This function is the inverse of the student t distribution cumulative 
8765     distribution function (CDF), returning `t` such that `stdtr(df, t) = p`. 
8766  
8767     Returns the argument `t` such that stdtr(df, t) is equal to `p`. 
8768  
8769     Parameters 
8770     ---------- 
8771     df : array_like 
8772         Degrees of freedom 
8773     p : array_like 
8774         Probability 
8775     out : ndarray, optional 
8776         Optional output array for the function results 
8777  
8778     Returns 
8779     ------- 
8780     t : scalar or ndarray 
8781         Value of `t` such that ``stdtr(df, t) == p`` 
8782  
8783     See Also 
8784     -------- 
8785     stdtr : Student t CDF 
8786     stdtridf : inverse of stdtr with respect to `df` 
8787     scipy.stats.t : Student t distribution 
8788  
8789     Notes 
8790     ----- 
8791     The student t distribution is also available as `scipy.stats.t`. Calling 
8792     `stdtrit` directly can improve performance compared to the ``ppf`` 
8793     method of `scipy.stats.t` (see last example below). 
8794  
8795     Examples 
8796     -------- 
8797     `stdtrit` represents the inverse of the student t distribution CDF which 
8798     is available as `stdtr`. Here, we calculate the CDF for ``df`` at 
8799     ``x=1``. `stdtrit` then returns ``1`` up to floating point errors 
8800     given the same value for `df` and the computed CDF value. 
8801  
8802     >>> import numpy as np 
8803     >>> from scipy.special import stdtr, stdtrit 
8804     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
8805     >>> df = 3 
8806     >>> x = 1 
8807     >>> cdf_value = stdtr(df, x) 
8808     >>> stdtrit(df, cdf_value) 
8809     0.9999999994418539 
8810  
8811     Plot the function for three different degrees of freedom. 
8812  
8813     >>> x = np.linspace(0, 1, 1000) 
8814     >>> parameters = [(1, "solid"), (2, "dashed"), (5, "dotted")] 
8815     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
8816     >>> for (df, linestyle) in parameters: 
8817     ...     ax.plot(x, stdtrit(df, x), ls=linestyle, label=f"$df={df}$") 
8818     >>> ax.legend() 
8819     >>> ax.set_ylim(-10, 10) 
8820     >>> ax.set_title("Student t distribution quantile function") 
8821     >>> plt.show() 
8822  
8823     The function can be computed for several degrees of freedom at the same 
8824     time by providing a NumPy array or list for `df`: 
8825  
8826     >>> stdtrit([1, 2, 3], 0.7) 
8827     array([0.72654253, 0.6172134 , 0.58438973]) 
8828  
8829     It is possible to calculate the function at several points for several 
8830     different degrees of freedom simultaneously by providing arrays for `df` 
8831     and `p` with shapes compatible for broadcasting. Compute `stdtrit` at 
8832     4 points for 3 degrees of freedom resulting in an array of shape 3x4. 
8833  
8834     >>> dfs = np.array([[1], [2], [3]]) 
8835     >>> p = np.array([0.2, 0.4, 0.7, 0.8]) 
8836     >>> dfs.shape, p.shape 
8837     ((3, 1), (4,)) 
8838  
8839     >>> stdtrit(dfs, p) 
8840     array([[-1.37638192, -0.3249197 ,  0.72654253,  1.37638192], 
8841            [-1.06066017, -0.28867513,  0.6172134 ,  1.06066017], 
8842            [-0.97847231, -0.27667066,  0.58438973,  0.97847231]]) 
8843  
8844     The t distribution is also available as `scipy.stats.t`. Calling `stdtrit` 
8845     directly can be much faster than calling the ``ppf`` method of 
8846     `scipy.stats.t`. To get the same results, one must use the following 
8847     parametrization: ``scipy.stats.t(df).ppf(x) = stdtrit(df, x)``. 
8848  
8849     >>> from scipy.stats import t 
8850     >>> df, x = 3, 0.5 
8851     >>> stdtrit_result = stdtrit(df, x)  # this can be faster than below 
8852     >>> stats_result = t(df).ppf(x) 
8853     >>> stats_result == stdtrit_result  # test that results are equal 
8854     True 
8855     """)

8858     "tklmbda",
8859     r""" 
8860     tklmbda(x, lmbda, out=None) 
8861  
8862     Cumulative distribution function of the Tukey lambda distribution. 
8863  
8864     Parameters 
8865     ---------- 
8866     x, lmbda : array_like 
8867         Parameters 
8868     out : ndarray, optional 
8869         Optional output array for the function results 
8870  
8871     Returns 
8872     ------- 
8873     cdf : scalar or ndarray 
8874         Value of the Tukey lambda CDF 
8875  
8876     See Also 
8877     -------- 
8878     scipy.stats.tukeylambda : Tukey lambda distribution 
8879  
8880     Examples 
8881     -------- 
8882     >>> import numpy as np 
8883     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
8884     >>> from scipy.special import tklmbda, expit 
8885  
8886     Compute the cumulative distribution function (CDF) of the Tukey lambda 
8887     distribution at several ``x`` values for `lmbda` = -1.5. 
8888  
8889     >>> x = np.linspace(-2, 2, 9) 
8890     >>> x 
8891     array([-2. , -1.5, -1. , -0.5,  0. ,  0.5,  1. ,  1.5,  2. ]) 
8892     >>> tklmbda(x, -1.5) 
8893     array([0.34688734, 0.3786554 , 0.41528805, 0.45629737, 0.5       , 
8894            0.54370263, 0.58471195, 0.6213446 , 0.65311266]) 
8895  
8896     When `lmbda` is 0, the function is the logistic sigmoid function, 
8897     which is implemented in `scipy.special` as `expit`. 
8898  
8899     >>> tklmbda(x, 0) 
8900     array([0.11920292, 0.18242552, 0.26894142, 0.37754067, 0.5       , 
8901            0.62245933, 0.73105858, 0.81757448, 0.88079708]) 
8902     >>> expit(x) 
8903     array([0.11920292, 0.18242552, 0.26894142, 0.37754067, 0.5       , 
8904            0.62245933, 0.73105858, 0.81757448, 0.88079708]) 
8905  
8906     When `lmbda` is 1, the Tukey lambda distribution is uniform on the 
8907     interval [-1, 1], so the CDF increases linearly. 
8908  
8909     >>> t = np.linspace(-1, 1, 9) 
8910     >>> tklmbda(t, 1) 
8911     array([0.   , 0.125, 0.25 , 0.375, 0.5  , 0.625, 0.75 , 0.875, 1.   ]) 
8912  
8913     In the following, we generate plots for several values of `lmbda`. 
8914  
8915     The first figure shows graphs for `lmbda` <= 0. 
8916  
8917     >>> styles = ['-', '-.', '--', ':'] 
8918     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
8919     >>> x = np.linspace(-12, 12, 500) 
8920     >>> for k, lmbda in enumerate([-1.0, -0.5, 0.0]): 
8921     ...     y = tklmbda(x, lmbda) 
8922     ...     ax.plot(x, y, styles[k], label=rf'$\lambda$ = {lmbda:-4.1f}') 
8923  
8924     >>> ax.set_title(r'tklmbda(x, $\lambda$)') 
8925     >>> ax.set_label('x') 
8926     >>> ax.legend(framealpha=1, shadow=True) 
8927     >>> ax.grid(True) 
8928  
8929     The second figure shows graphs for `lmbda` > 0.  The dots in the 
8930     graphs show the bounds of the support of the distribution. 
8931  
8932     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
8933     >>> x = np.linspace(-4.2, 4.2, 500) 
8934     >>> lmbdas = [0.25, 0.5, 1.0, 1.5] 
8935     >>> for k, lmbda in enumerate(lmbdas): 
8936     ...     y = tklmbda(x, lmbda) 
8937     ...     ax.plot(x, y, styles[k], label=fr'$\lambda$ = {lmbda}') 
8938  
8939     >>> ax.set_prop_cycle(None) 
8940     >>> for lmbda in lmbdas: 
8941     ...     ax.plot([-1/lmbda, 1/lmbda], [0, 1], '.', ms=8) 
8942  
8943     >>> ax.set_title(r'tklmbda(x, $\lambda$)') 
8944     >>> ax.set_xlabel('x') 
8945     >>> ax.legend(framealpha=1, shadow=True) 
8946     >>> ax.grid(True) 
8947  
8948     >>> plt.tight_layout() 
8949     >>> plt.show() 
8950  
8951     The CDF of the Tukey lambda distribution is also implemented as the 
8952     ``cdf`` method of `scipy.stats.tukeylambda`.  In the following, 
8953     ``tukeylambda.cdf(x, -0.5)`` and ``tklmbda(x, -0.5)`` compute the 
8954     same values: 
8955  
8956     >>> from scipy.stats import tukeylambda 
8957     >>> x = np.linspace(-2, 2, 9) 
8958  
8959     >>> tukeylambda.cdf(x, -0.5) 
8960     array([0.21995157, 0.27093858, 0.33541677, 0.41328161, 0.5       , 
8961            0.58671839, 0.66458323, 0.72906142, 0.78004843]) 
8962  
8963     >>> tklmbda(x, -0.5) 
8964     array([0.21995157, 0.27093858, 0.33541677, 0.41328161, 0.5       , 
8965            0.58671839, 0.66458323, 0.72906142, 0.78004843]) 
8966  
8967     The implementation in ``tukeylambda`` also provides location and scale 
8968     parameters, and other methods such as ``pdf()`` (the probability 
8969     density function) and ``ppf()`` (the inverse of the CDF), so for 
8970     working with the Tukey lambda distribution, ``tukeylambda`` is more 
8971     generally useful.  The primary advantage of ``tklmbda`` is that it is 
8972     significantly faster than ``tukeylambda.cdf``. 
8973     """)
