9024 add_newdoc("xlogy",
9025     """ 
9026     xlogy(x, y, out=None) 
9027  
9028     Compute ``x*log(y)`` so that the result is 0 if ``x = 0``. 
9029  
9030     Parameters 
9031     ---------- 
9032     x : array_like 
9033         Multiplier 
9034     y : array_like 
9035         Argument 
9036     out : ndarray, optional 
9037         Optional output array for the function results 
9038  
9039     Returns 
9040     ------- 
9041     z : scalar or ndarray 
9042         Computed x*log(y) 
9043  
9044     Notes 
9045     ----- 
9046     The log function used in the computation is the natural log. 
9047  
9048     .. versionadded:: 0.13.0 
9049  
9050     Examples 
9051     -------- 
9052     We can use this function to calculate the binary logistic loss also 
9053     known as the binary cross entropy. This loss function is used for 
9054     binary classification problems and is defined as: 
9055  
9056     .. math:: 
9057         L = 1/n * \\sum_{i=0}^n -(y_i*log(y\\_pred_i) + (1-y_i)*log(1-y\\_pred_i)) 
9058  
9059     We can define the parameters `x` and `y` as y and y_pred respectively. 
9060     y is the array of the actual labels which over here can be either 0 or 1. 
9061     y_pred is the array of the predicted probabilities with respect to 
9062     the positive class (1). 
9063  
9064     >>> import numpy as np 
9065     >>> from scipy.special import xlogy 
9066     >>> y = np.array([0, 1, 0, 1, 1, 0]) 
9067     >>> y_pred = np.array([0.3, 0.8, 0.4, 0.7, 0.9, 0.2]) 
9068     >>> n = len(y) 
9069     >>> loss = -(xlogy(y, y_pred) + xlogy(1 - y, 1 - y_pred)).sum() 
9070     >>> loss /= n 
9071     >>> loss 
9072     0.29597052165495025 
9073  
9074     A lower loss is usually better as it indicates that the predictions are 
9075     similar to the actual labels. In this example since our predicted 
9076     probabilities are close to the actual labels, we get an overall loss 
9077     that is reasonably low and appropriate. 
9078  
9079     """)
9080 
9081 add_newdoc("xlog1py",
9082     """ 
9083     xlog1py(x, y, out=None) 
9084  
9085     Compute ``x*log1p(y)`` so that the result is 0 if ``x = 0``. 
9086  
9087     Parameters 
9088     ---------- 
9089     x : array_like 
9090         Multiplier 
9091     y : array_like 
9092         Argument 
9093     out : ndarray, optional 
9094         Optional output array for the function results 
9095  
9096     Returns 
9097     ------- 
9098     z : scalar or ndarray 
9099         Computed x*log1p(y) 
9100  
9101     Notes 
9102     ----- 
9103  
9104     .. versionadded:: 0.13.0 
9105  
9106     Examples 
9107     -------- 
9108     This example shows how the function can be used to calculate the log of 
9109     the probability mass function for a geometric discrete random variable. 
9110     The probability mass function of the geometric distribution is defined 
9111     as follows: 
9112  
9113     .. math:: f(k) = (1-p)^{k-1} p 
9114  
9115     where :math:`p` is the probability of a single success 
9116     and :math:`1-p` is the probability of a single failure 
9117     and :math:`k` is the number of trials to get the first success. 
9118  
9119     >>> import numpy as np 
9120     >>> from scipy.special import xlog1py 
9121     >>> p = 0.5 
9122     >>> k = 100 
9123     >>> _pmf = np.power(1 - p, k - 1) * p 
9124     >>> _pmf 
9125     7.888609052210118e-31 
9126  
9127     If we take k as a relatively large number the value of the probability 
9128     mass function can become very low. In such cases taking the log of the 
9129     pmf would be more suitable as the log function can change the values 
9130     to a scale that is more appropriate to work with. 
9131  
9132     >>> _log_pmf = xlog1py(k - 1, -p) + np.log(p) 
9133     >>> _log_pmf 
9134     -69.31471805599453 
9135  
9136     We can confirm that we get a value close to the original pmf value by 
9137     taking the exponential of the log pmf. 
9138  
9139     >>> _orig_pmf = np.exp(_log_pmf) 
9140     >>> np.isclose(_pmf, _orig_pmf) 
9141     True 
9142  
9143     """)
9144

9167     yv : For real order and real or complex argument. 
9168     y0: faster implementation of this function for order 0 
9169     y1: faster implementation of this function for order 1 
9170  
9171     Notes 
9172     ----- 
9173     Wrapper for the Cephes [1]_ routine `yn`. 
9174  
9175     The function is evaluated by forward recurrence on `n`, starting with 
9176     values computed by the Cephes routines `y0` and `y1`. If ``n = 0`` or 1, 
9177     the routine for `y0` or `y1` is called directly. 
9178  
9179     References 
9180     ---------- 
9181     .. [1] Cephes Mathematical Functions Library, 
9182            http://www.netlib.org/cephes/ 
9183  
9184     Examples 
9185     -------- 
9186     Evaluate the function of order 0 at one point. 
9187  
9188     >>> from scipy.special import yn 
9189     >>> yn(0, 1.) 
9190     0.08825696421567697 
9191  
9192     Evaluate the function at one point for different orders. 
9193  
9194     >>> yn(0, 1.), yn(1, 1.), yn(2, 1.) 
9195     (0.08825696421567697, -0.7812128213002888, -1.6506826068162546) 
9196  
9197     The evaluation for different orders can be carried out in one call by 
9198     providing a list or NumPy array as argument for the `v` parameter: 
9199  
9200     >>> yn([0, 1, 2], 1.) 
9201     array([ 0.08825696, -0.78121282, -1.65068261]) 
9202  
9203     Evaluate the function at several points for order 0 by providing an 
9204     array for `z`. 
9205  
9206     >>> import numpy as np 
9207     >>> points = np.array([0.5, 3., 8.]) 
9208     >>> yn(0, points) 
9209     array([-0.44451873,  0.37685001,  0.22352149]) 
9210  
9211     If `z` is an array, the order parameter `v` must be broadcastable to 
9212     the correct shape if different orders shall be computed in one call. 
9213     To calculate the orders 0 and 1 for an 1D array: 
9214  
9215     >>> orders = np.array([[0], [1]]) 
9216     >>> orders.shape 
9217     (2, 1) 
9218  
9219     >>> yn(orders, points) 
9220     array([[-0.44451873,  0.37685001,  0.22352149], 
9221            [-1.47147239,  0.32467442, -0.15806046]]) 
9222  
9223     Plot the functions of order 0 to 3 from 0 to 10. 
9224  
9225     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
9226     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
9227     >>> x = np.linspace(0., 10., 1000) 
9228     >>> for i in range(4): 
9229     ...     ax.plot(x, yn(i, x), label=f'$Y_{i!r}$') 
9230     >>> ax.set_ylim(-3, 1) 
9231     >>> ax.legend() 
9232     >>> plt.show() 
9233     """)
9234 
9235 add_newdoc("yv",
9236     r""" 
9237     yv(v, z, out=None) 
9238  
9239     Bessel function of the second kind of real order and complex argument. 
9240  
9241     Parameters 
9242     ---------- 
9243     v : array_like 
9244         Order (float). 
9245     z : array_like 
9246         Argument (float or complex). 
9247     out : ndarray, optional 
9248         Optional output array for the function results 
9249  
9250     Returns 
9251     ------- 
9252     Y : scalar or ndarray 
9253         Value of the Bessel function of the second kind, :math:`Y_v(x)`. 
9254  
9255     See Also 
9256     -------- 
9257     yve : :math:`Y_v` with leading exponential behavior stripped off. 
9258     y0: faster implementation of this function for order 0 
9259     y1: faster implementation of this function for order 1 
9260  
9261     Notes 
9262     ----- 
9263     For positive `v` values, the computation is carried out using the 
9264     AMOS [1]_ `zbesy` routine, which exploits the connection to the Hankel 
9265     Bessel functions :math:`H_v^{(1)}` and :math:`H_v^{(2)}`, 
9266  
9267     .. math:: Y_v(z) = \frac{1}{2\imath} (H_v^{(1)} - H_v^{(2)}). 
9268  
9269     For negative `v` values the formula, 
9270  
9271     .. math:: Y_{-v}(z) = Y_v(z) \cos(\pi v) + J_v(z) \sin(\pi v) 
9272  
9273     is used, where :math:`J_v(z)` is the Bessel function of the first kind, 
9274     computed using the AMOS routine `zbesj`.  Note that the second term is 
9275     exactly zero for integer `v`; to improve accuracy the second term is 
9276     explicitly omitted for `v` values such that `v = floor(v)`. 
9277  
9278     References 
9279     ---------- 
9280     .. [1] Donald E. Amos, "AMOS, A Portable Package for Bessel Functions 
9281            of a Complex Argument and Nonnegative Order", 
9282            http://netlib.org/amos/ 
9283  
9284     Examples 
9285     -------- 
9286     Evaluate the function of order 0 at one point. 
9287  
9288     >>> from scipy.special import yv 
9289     >>> yv(0, 1.) 
9290     0.088256964215677 
9291  
9292     Evaluate the function at one point for different orders. 
9293  
9294     >>> yv(0, 1.), yv(1, 1.), yv(1.5, 1.) 
9295     (0.088256964215677, -0.7812128213002889, -1.102495575160179) 
9296  
9297     The evaluation for different orders can be carried out in one call by 
9298     providing a list or NumPy array as argument for the `v` parameter: 
9299  
9300     >>> yv([0, 1, 1.5], 1.) 
9301     array([ 0.08825696, -0.78121282, -1.10249558]) 
9302  
9303     Evaluate the function at several points for order 0 by providing an 
9304     array for `z`. 
9305  
9306     >>> import numpy as np 
9307     >>> points = np.array([0.5, 3., 8.]) 
9308     >>> yv(0, points) 
9309     array([-0.44451873,  0.37685001,  0.22352149]) 
9310  
9311     If `z` is an array, the order parameter `v` must be broadcastable to 
9312     the correct shape if different orders shall be computed in one call. 
9313     To calculate the orders 0 and 1 for an 1D array: 
9314  
9315     >>> orders = np.array([[0], [1]]) 
9316     >>> orders.shape 
9317     (2, 1) 
9318  
9319     >>> yv(orders, points) 
9320     array([[-0.44451873,  0.37685001,  0.22352149], 
9321            [-1.47147239,  0.32467442, -0.15806046]]) 
9322  
9323     Plot the functions of order 0 to 3 from 0 to 10. 
9324  
9325     >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt 
9326     >>> fig, ax = plt.subplots() 
9327     >>> x = np.linspace(0., 10., 1000) 
9328     >>> for i in range(4): 
9329     ...     ax.plot(x, yv(i, x), label=f'$Y_{i!r}$') 
9330     >>> ax.set_ylim(-3, 1) 
9331     >>> ax.legend() 
9332     >>> plt.show() 
9333  
9334     """)
9335 
9336 add_newdoc("yve",
9337     r""" 
9338     yve(v, z, out=None) 
9339  
9340     Exponentially scaled Bessel function of the second kind of real order. 
9341  
9342     Returns the exponentially scaled Bessel function of the second 
9343     kind of real order `v` at complex `z`:: 
9344  
9345         yve(v, z) = yv(v, z) * exp(-abs(z.imag)) 
9346  
9347     Parameters 
9348     ---------- 
9349     v : array_like 
9350         Order (float). 
9351     z : array_like 
9352         Argument (float or complex). 
9353     out : ndarray, optional 
9354         Optional output array for the function results 
9355  
9356     Returns 
9357     ------- 
9358     Y : scalar or ndarray 
9359         Value of the exponentially scaled Bessel function. 
9360  
9361     See Also 
9362     -------- 
9363     yv: Unscaled Bessel function of the second kind of real order. 
9364  
9365     Notes 
9366     ----- 
9367     For positive `v` values, the computation is carried out using the 
9368     AMOS [1]_ `zbesy` routine, which exploits the connection to the Hankel 
9369     Bessel functions :math:`H_v^{(1)}` and :math:`H_v^{(2)}`, 
9370  
9371     .. math:: Y_v(z) = \frac{1}{2\imath} (H_v^{(1)} - H_v^{(2)}). 
9372  
9373     For negative `v` values the formula, 
9374  
9375     .. math:: Y_{-v}(z) = Y_v(z) \cos(\pi v) + J_v(z) \sin(\pi v) 
9376  
9377     is used, where :math:`J_v(z)` is the Bessel function of the first kind, 
9378     computed using the AMOS routine `zbesj`.  Note that the second term is 
9379     exactly zero for integer `v`; to improve accuracy the second term is 
9380     explicitly omitted for `v` values such that `v = floor(v)`. 
9381  
9382     Exponentially scaled Bessel functions are useful for large `z`: 
9383     for these, the unscaled Bessel functions can easily under-or overflow. 
9384  
9385     References 
9386     ---------- 
9387     .. [1] Donald E. Amos, "AMOS, A Portable Package for Bessel Functions 
9388            of a Complex Argument and Nonnegative Order", 
9389            http://netlib.org/amos/ 
9390  
9391     Examples 
9392     -------- 
9393     Compare the output of `yv` and `yve` for large complex arguments for `z` 
9394     by computing their values for order ``v=1`` at ``z=1000j``. We see that 
9395     `yv` returns nan but `yve` returns a finite number: 
9396  
9397     >>> import numpy as np 
9398     >>> from scipy.special import yv, yve 
9399     >>> v = 1 
9400     >>> z = 1000j 
9401     >>> yv(v, z), yve(v, z) 
9402     ((nan+nanj), (-0.012610930256928629+7.721967686709076e-19j)) 
9403  
9404     For real arguments for `z`, `yve` returns the same as `yv` up to 
9405     floating point errors. 
9406  
9407     >>> v, z = 1, 1000 
9408     >>> yv(v, z), yve(v, z) 
9409     (-0.02478433129235178, -0.02478433129235179) 
9410  
9411     The function can be evaluated for several orders at the same time by 
9412     providing a list or NumPy array for `v`: 
9413  
9414     >>> yve([1, 2, 3], 1j) 
9415     array([-0.20791042+0.14096627j,  0.38053618-0.04993878j, 
9416            0.00815531-1.66311097j]) 
9417  
9418     In the same way, the function can be evaluated at several points in one 
9419     call by providing a list or NumPy array for `z`: 
9420  
9421     >>> yve(1, np.array([1j, 2j, 3j])) 
9422     array([-0.20791042+0.14096627j, -0.21526929+0.01205044j, 
9423            -0.19682671+0.00127278j]) 
9424  
9425     It is also possible to evaluate several orders at several points 
9426     at the same time by providing arrays for `v` and `z` with 
9427     broadcasting compatible shapes. Compute `yve` for two different orders 
9428     `v` and three points `z` resulting in a 2x3 array. 
9429  
9430     >>> v = np.array([[1], [2]]) 
9431     >>> z = np.array([3j, 4j, 5j]) 
9432     >>> v.shape, z.shape 
9433     ((2, 1), (3,)) 
9434  
9435     >>> yve(v, z) 
9436     array([[-1.96826713e-01+1.27277544e-03j, -1.78750840e-01+1.45558819e-04j, 
9437             -1.63972267e-01+1.73494110e-05j], 
9438            [1.94960056e-03-1.11782545e-01j,  2.02902325e-04-1.17626501e-01j, 
9439             2.27727687e-05-1.17951906e-01j]]) 
9440     """)
9441

9544         where X and Y are independent standard normal random variables. 
9545  
9546     References

9549            Owen's T Function", Statistical Software vol. 5, pp. 1-25, 2000.

9565 add_newdoc("ndtri_exp",
9566     r""" 
9567     ndtri_exp(y, out=None) 
9568  
9569     Inverse of `log_ndtr` vs x. Allows for greater precision than 
9570     `ndtri` composed with `numpy.exp` for very small values of y and for 
9571     y close to 0. 
9572  
9573     Parameters 
9574     ---------- 
9575     y : array_like of float 
9576         Function argument 
9577     out : ndarray, optional 
9578         Optional output array for the function results 
9579  
9580     Returns 
9581     ------- 
9582     scalar or ndarray 
9583         Inverse of the log CDF of the standard normal distribution, evaluated 
9584         at y. 
9585  
9586     See Also 
9587     -------- 
9588     log_ndtr : log of the standard normal cumulative distribution function 
9589     ndtr : standard normal cumulative distribution function 
9590     ndtri : standard normal percentile function 
9591  
9592     Examples 
9593     -------- 
9594     >>> import numpy as np 
9595     >>> import scipy.special as sc 
9596  
9597     `ndtri_exp` agrees with the naive implementation when the latter does 
9598     not suffer from underflow. 
9599  
9600     >>> sc.ndtri_exp(-1) 
9601     -0.33747496376420244 
9602     >>> sc.ndtri(np.exp(-1)) 
9603     -0.33747496376420244 
9604  
9605     For extreme values of y, the naive approach fails 
9606  
9607     >>> sc.ndtri(np.exp(-800)) 
9608     -inf 
9609     >>> sc.ndtri(np.exp(-1e-20)) 
9610     inf 
9611  
9612     whereas `ndtri_exp` is still able to compute the result to high precision. 
9613  
9614     >>> sc.ndtri_exp(-800) 
9615     -39.88469483825668 
9616     >>> sc.ndtri_exp(-1e-20) 
9617     9.262340089798409 
9618     """)
